**Summary.** This paper introduces Engineered Random States (ERSs) as a universal resource for quantum metrology, proving that superior precision scaling (Heisenberg limit) is statistically generic across exponentially large Hilbert spaces. The theoretical framework uses random unitary ensembles to calculate the average Quantum Fisher Information, which is experimentally validated on a trapped-ion quantum processor, demonstrating a significant metrological enhancement.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, general paradigm showing that quantum enhancement can be accessed robustly from the vast, complex structure of high-dimensional quantum systems, directly applicable to trapped-ion and superconducting circuit platforms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is overcoming the exponential scaling barrier of the many-body Hilbert space to achieve quantum-enhanced metrology.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that quantum advantage (Heisenberg-limited scaling) can be a statistically generic property of Engineered Random States (ERSs) generated by random unitaries.

**Method.** The analysis uses ensemble averaging over random unitary ensembles ($\alpha$-random unitaries) to calculate the average Quantum Fisher Information ($	ext{QFI}$), confirming the scaling theoretically and experimentally.

**Model / system.** The work analyzes quantum systems of $N$ particles, focusing on ERSs generated by random unitaries, and validates the findings using a trapped-ion processor platform.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information ($	ext{QFI}$), metrological gain (in dB) beyond the Standard Quantum Limit (SQL), and the ensemble average $\mathbb{E}[F_Q]$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The randomness parameter $\alpha$, the number of particles $N$, and the degree of disorder $\Delta$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies heavily on the concentration-of-measure phenomenon in high-dimensional spaces to prove that the average scaling holds for most states.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare metrological gain across the vast Hilbert space vs. the symmetric subspace; simulations show $	ext{QFI}$ density following $\mathbb{E}[f_Q] = N/4$; and the experiment shows a $6.98 \pm 0.38$ dB gain on a trapped-ion processor.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the theory by first establishing the $	ext{QFI}$ scaling using $\alpha$-random unitaries (Theory), then analyzing specific state constructions like Random Phase States and Chimera-superposition states (Theory), and finally validating the framework using random quantum circuits on a trapped-ion platform (Experiment).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The exponential growth of many-body Hilbert space presents a fundamental barrier to quantum technology, obscuring the search for physically significant states within an astronomically vast landscape. Consequently, resources for quantum-enhanced metrology have been largely confined to the symmetric subspace whose dimensionality scales only polynomially with the particle number-leaving the vast majority of the Hilbert space largely unexplored and poorly understood. Here we challenge this paradigm by demonstrating that metrological advantage can arise as a ubiquitous feature across exponential-dimensional manifolds. By tailoring the first-moment structure of random unitaries, we uncover dense manifolds of engineered random states (ERSs) where Heisenberg-limited scaling emerges as a statistically generic property. This ubiquity endows these resource states with inherent resilience against parameter disorder. We experimentally validate this framework on a trapped-ion processor, achieving a metrological enhancement of $6.98 \pm 0.38$ dB beyond the standard quantum limit. Potential applications extend to diverse platforms, ranging from superconducting circuits and waveguide QED to solid-state spins and polar molecules. Our results establish a powerful paradigm where quantum-enhanced precision can be harvested from the exponential vastness of the Hilbert space.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31448"></a>
### [Pseudoentanglement in constant depth: How trivial states can have non-trivial entanglement structure](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31448v1)

**Authors:** Alexandru Gheorghiu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31448v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31448_figures/2605.31448_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Schematic 2D layout of the randomized encoding with four input columns and three representative rows. Sky-blue boxes are input bits, vermillion boxes are mask bits, and purple boxes are outputs. The w-outputs are computed by column-local nearest-neighbor gadgets: wj,0 depends only on xj and rj,1, while wj,1 and wj,2 depend only on adjacent r-masks in the same column. The z-outputs are computed inside constant-size plaquettes: zi,j depends only on the two neighboring horizontal masks si,j−1 and si,j, and, when Mi,j = 1, the local vertical mask rj,i. The vertical wires through the w-boxes should be read as connections to the adjacent r-masks above and below them, not as connections to...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31448_figures/2605.31448_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Local implementation of one plaquette. The four external ports carry rj,i from above, rj,i+1 to the row below, and the two horizontal masks si,j−1 and si,j. The plaquette computes wj,i = rj,i ⊕rj,i+1 and zi,j = si,j−1 ⊕si,j ⊕Mi,jrj,i. The gray register aj,i is only local workspace, initialized to 0 and uncomputed to 0 after use. One possible local schedule is as follows. First copy rj,i into the workspace, apply it to wj,i, and uncompute the workspace: rj,i →aj,i →wj,i followed by rj,i →aj,i. Then apply the incoming rj,i+1 from the bottom port to wj,i. Next reuse the same workspace for the horizontal mask: copy si,j−1 into aj,i, apply it to zi,j, and uncompute it; then apply si,j...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper constructs 'pseudoentangled' quantum states using shallow, local circuits whose entanglement structure is hard for quantum computers to measure. By mapping these states to local Hamiltonians, the authors prove that determining the ground state entanglement structure of 1D and 2D quantum systems is quantumly hard, provided certain cryptographic assumptions hold.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful tool to establish computational hardness for characterizing the ground state properties of quantum many-body systems, which is crucial for understanding the limits of quantum simulation and quantum phase detection.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to construct quantum states whose entanglement structure is computationally hard to determine (pseudoentanglement), thereby separating this property from pseudorandomness in the shallow-circuit regime.

**Main result.** It constructs families of 2D-local, constant-depth quantum circuits whose output states exhibit a large, non-negligible gap in entanglement entropy across a fixed cut, demonstrating quantum hardness for learning the entanglement structure of local Hamiltonians.

**Method.** The construction relies on the quantum intractability of the Dense-Sparse Learning Parity with Noise problem, utilizing a perfect randomized encoding of a linear map to generate the pseudoentangled states.

**Model / system.** The model involves 2D-local, constant-depth quantum circuits acting on qubits, which are then mapped to local Hamiltonians on 1D and 2D lattices for physical applications.

**Key observables.** Entanglement Entropy (specifically the gap $\Delta(n)$ across a fixed cut $W$), and the spectral gap of the resulting Hamiltonians.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit Depth (constant depth), System Size ($n$), and the gap size ($\Delta(n) = \omega(\log n)$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is the intractability of the Dense-Sparse LPN problem for quantum polynomial-time (QPT) adversaries.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the 2D layout of the randomized encoding and the local implementation of a single plaquette, detailing how the output bits depend on input masks and neighboring bits.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from cryptographic preliminaries (randomized encoding) to the main pseudoentanglement theorem, and finally applies this result to prove quantum hardness for learning the entanglement structure of ground states of 1D and 2D local Hamiltonians.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We construct a family of 2D-local constant-depth quantum circuits that output states whose entanglement entropy across a specified cut cannot be estimated in quantum polynomial time. As constant-depth quantum circuits can be learned from polynomially many quantum samples, our resulting pseudoentangled states are implicitly public-key and not pseudorandom. This separates pseudoentanglement from pseudorandomness in the shallow-circuit regime: the former is possible, while the latter is not. The construction is based on the quantum intractability of the Dense-Sparse Learning Parity with Noise problem introduced in [DJ25] and uses a bounded-fan-in, bounded-fan-out classical randomized encoding for linear maps $\mathbf{x} \mapsto \mathbf{Mx},$ which could be of independent interest. As applications, we obtain quantum hardness for the problem of learning the entanglement structure (across a fixed cut) of the ground-state of 1D and 2D local Hamiltonians. The 1D Hamiltonian has an inverse polynomial gap, whereas the 2D one has a constant gap. This complements the result of [BZZ24] that showed only factoring-based hardness for the 1D case, though achieving a volume versus area entanglement difference.

</details>

### [Experimental Implementation of the Quantum Volunteer's Dilemma on NISQ Hardware: Noise Analysis and Digital-Twin Validation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30676v1)

<sub>Figure 5 presents two complementary but distinct payoff metrics at optimization level L2. **Summary.** This work experimentally tests the Quantum Volunteer's Dilemma on NISQ hardware to quantify quantum advantage under noise. The results show that while the aggregate payoff remains quantum-enhanced, the fidelity of the desired quantum state degrades rapidly, limiting observable advantage to smaller system sizes. The study critically compares hardware performance against idealized noise models, highlighting the need for advanced error mitigation.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, game-theoretic testbed for assessing the practical limits of quantum advantage in the presence of realistic, non-ideal hardware noise, which is crucial for developing fault-tolerant quantum algorithms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To experimentally determine if a quantum advantage exists in the multiplayer Quantum Volunteer's Dilemma when implemented on noisy intermediate-scale quantum (NISQ) hardware.

**Main result.** The global average payoff robustly exceeds the classical Nash equilibrium across the tested range, demonstrating aggregate quantum advantage despite noise. However, the practical observability of state-level advantage is limited to $N \le 8$ under readout correction.

**Method.** The study uses experimental measurements on superconducting quantum processors, comparing raw data against readout-corrected results and a theoretical 'Digital Twin' simulation.

**Model / system.** The system is the multiplayer Quantum Volunteer's Dilemma, implemented using the EWL quantization protocol on the ibm_kingston NISQ backend. The analysis systematically varies the number of players ($N=2$ to $9$) and transpiler optimization levels.

**Key observables.** Target-state fidelity, global average payoff, and the comparison against the classical Nash equilibrium payoff.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of players ($N$), readout error correction effectiveness, and the transition point where single-qubit errors give way to multi-qubit contributions ($N \approx 6$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Digital Twin model assumes first-order, independent per-qubit noise channels, which fails to capture higher-order effects like crosstalk or temporal drift at large $N$.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare target-state fidelity decay across different noise mitigation levels and show the comparison between target-state weighted payoff and the global average payoff relative to classical benchmarks.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of problem definition (Quantum Volunteer's Dilemma), methodology (NISQ implementation, error mitigation, Digital Twin comparison), results presentation (fidelity decay, payoff analysis), and concluding discussion on the limits of quantum advantage observability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present an experimental implementation of the multiplayer Quantum Volunteer's Dilemma on noisy intermediate-scale quantum (NISQ) hardware, executed on the ibm_kingston backend via Qiskit Runtime. The game is evaluated for N = 2 to 9 players under four transpiler optimization levels, with 20 independent repetitions per configuration and 2048 shots per circuit, including post-processing readout error correction via mthree. Target-state fidelity decays with system size but remains above 70% (corrected) through N = 9. With readout correction, the global average payoff reproduces the quantum theoretical benchmark exactly for N <= 6 and exceeds the classical Nash equilibrium across the full tested range. Optimization level 2 is selected as the reference configuration after gate count analysis reveals that levels 2 and 3 produce identical transpiled circuits, with level 2 achieving superior fidelity stability. A Hamming distance analysis of raw measurement counts shows that single-qubit errors dominate at small N, with multi-qubit contributions growing beyond N = 6. A calibration-based digital twin captures global payoff trends but exhibits a linear fidelity decay profile that diverges from the hardware behavior at large N, exposing the limits of first-order independent per-qubit noise models. These results demonstrate that aggregate quantum advantage in multiplayer games is robust to NISQ noise conditions across the full tested range, while the practical observability of state-level advantage is constrained to N <= 8 under post-processed readout correction.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30428"></a>
### [Graph automorphisms to obtain Clifford symmetries in open and closed qudit models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30428v1)

**Authors:** Charlie Nation, Rick P. A. Simon, Shreya Banerjee, Francesco Martini, Alessandro Ricottone, Federico Cerisola, Luca Dellantonio  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30428v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30428_figures/2605.30428_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Search algorithm for constructing a permutation Π.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper develops a general algorithmic approach to find Clifford symmetries in qudit systems by reformulating the problem as a Graph Automorphism (GA) problem. It constructs a graph based on Pauli commutation invariants and uses advanced search heuristics like WL refinement and MRV to find the symmetries. The method is robust enough to handle both closed Hamiltonians and open Liouvillian dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, general algorithmic framework for characterizing symmetries in complex quantum models, which is crucial for understanding conserved quantities and simplifying the analysis of quantum dynamics in both closed and open settings.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the theoretical challenge of finding Clifford symmetries for quantum systems, which is mapped to a computationally hard Graph Automorphism (GA) problem.

**Main result.** An algorithm is presented that successfully finds these symmetries for general qudit systems, extending the formalism from closed Hamiltonians to open Liouvillian systems.

**Method.** The core method involves constructing a colored, edge-labeled graph based on the Pauli commutation relations and encoding the symmetry search as finding a graph automorphism that preserves these invariants.

**Model / system.** The work applies to general qudit systems, covering both closed systems described by Hamiltonians (e.g., Toric Code, TFI) and open systems governed by GKSL master equations.

**Key observables.** Clifford symmetries ($\hat{S}$), graph automorphisms ($\Pi$), Pauli commutation relations ($\langle \vec{p}_i, \vec{p}_j angle$), and phase correction vectors ($\vec{\phi}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The local dimension $d$ of the qudits, the number of qudits $n$, and the structure of the Hamiltonian/Liouvillian.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The search relies on mapping the symmetry problem to GA, which is computationally intensive, and the phase correction step requires careful handling beyond the pure graph structure.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time scaling of symmetry finding for various models (Random Pauli, TFI, Heisenberg XXZ, Toric Code) comparing different search heuristics (MRV vs. bliss).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the GA mapping for symmetries, detailing the graph construction using Pauli invariants, refining the search using WL coloring and MRV heuristics, and finally extending the formalism to open systems and specific physical examples like the ladder structure.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the recent article [arXiv:2605.18966], we demonstrated that finding Clifford symmetries can be mapped to a Graph Automorphism (GA) problem. Here, we provide an algorithm to obtain such symmetries on general qudit systems, that works on the principle of encoding Clifford invariants of a Hamiltonian onto properties of a graph. Labelling Hamiltonian terms as vertices, a permutation of such vertices that respects the Clifford invariants (a GA) is both a valid Clifford, and a symmetry up to phase correction checks. We test this on multiple physical models and discuss the scaling with respect to the number of qudits and Pauli strings, as well as various strategies for optimisation in different regimes. We further show that the graph automorphism representation of Clifford symmetries can be expanded to open quantum systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30684"></a>
### [A mathematical framework for dynamic emergent constraints in climate science](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30684v1)

**Authors:** Francesco Ragone, Valerio Lucarini  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30684v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30684_figures/2605.30684_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Scatter plot of ensemble average response of global precipitation (total, convective and large scale) vs</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30684_figures/2605.30684_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Global surface temperature response, original direct simulation at one year average (grey) and proxy</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30684_figures/2605.30684_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. a) Causality index as function of coarse graining time scale τ, in red (blue) global surface temperature as</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30684_figures/2605.30684_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. a) AMOC index response, observed annual averages (grey) and proxy reconstructed using global surface</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work establishes a mathematical framework for dynamic emergent constraints in climate science by generalizing linear response theory. It uses proxy Green's functions and causality indices to determine how relationships between observables (like temperature and precipitation) are constrained by the observation time scale. This provides a powerful, mathematically grounded protocol for analyzing complex, non-equilibrium physical systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical machinery developed—using linear response theory, Green's functions, and causality indices—is highly transferable to analyzing non-equilibrium dynamics in open quantum systems, such as those involving dissipation or environmental coupling.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a rigorous mathematical framework for quantifying 'dynamic emergent constraints' between different physical observables in complex systems like the climate, aiming to reduce projection uncertainties.

**Main result.** The framework generalizes traditional constraints to 'integral dynamic emergent constraints,' showing that the existence and form of these constraints depend critically on the causality of the system's proxy Green's function and the coarse-graining time scale.

**Method.** The study employs linear response theory, utilizing susceptibility ratios and proxy Green's functions derived from climate model simulations to establish integral relationships between observable responses to external forcing.

**Model / system.** The analysis is applied to global warming simulations using the MPI-ESM climate model, focusing on observables such as global surface temperature, precipitation, and AMOC intensity.

**Key observables.** Global surface temperature (T_s), Precipitation (P), Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation (M), and their respective responses ($\delta\Phi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coarse-graining time scale ($	au$), which dictates whether the relationship is instantaneous, directional, or reciprocal.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory relies on the assumption that the system dynamics can be described by a stochastic dynamical system amenable to linear response theory, and that the relationship can be captured by the proxy Green's function.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the causality index ($C_{\Phi_1\Phi_2}$) as a function of the time scale $	au$, showing how the predictive power and directionality of constraints change with the observation window.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the problem using linear response theory to generalizing constraints into integral forms. It then applies this theory to climate model data, analyzing how the causality index and proxy Green's functions reveal the dependence of emergent constraints on the coarse-graining time scale $	au$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Emergent constraints in climate science are empirical relations that link the response to a forcing of a physical observable to the properties of other observables, with the aim of reducing climate change projection uncertainties. Here we use recent results in linear response theory to develop a mathematical framework for dynamic emergent constraints, a class of emergent constraints linking the response of different observables to the same forcing. We show how traditional dynamic emergent constraints are a special case of more general relations, that we call integral dynamic emergent constraints. These relations allow to compute the response of a predictand as the convolution of the response of a predictor and the proxy Green's function of the predictand-predictor pair. The conditions for the existence of integral emergent constraints are related to the causality of the proxy Green's function and the time scales at which the system is observed. We apply this framework to global warming simulations with the MPI-ESM climate model, to study dynamic emergent constraints between different observables. These results allow to put the theory of dynamic emergent constraints on firm mathematical ground, and suggest a protocol to identify necessary conditions for the existence of such relations in climate data.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.30887"></a>
### [Activity-Enhanced Ordering in Fluctuation-Induced First-Order Transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30887v1)

**Authors:** Suvendra K. Sahoo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30887v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30887_figures/2605.30887_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Schematic illustration of active diblock copoly- mer with equal length subchains exhibiting microphase sepa- ration, (b) Red and blue segments represent distinct monomer species; interactions between unlike monomers are energeti- cally unfavorable compared to like-like interactions, (c) Each monomer possesses a self-propulsion velocity, with its direc- tion (indicated by arrows) evolving via diffusive dynamics.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30887_figures/2605.30887_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Renormalized mass parameter r as a function of r0 for different activity strengths τ. Increasing τ suppresses fluctua- tion corrections, reduces r, and shifts the transition to higher temperatures. The renormalized mass r remains strictly pos- itive, indicating the absence of a spinodal instability. Inset: Dependence on activity ξ.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30887_figures/2605.30887_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Static structure factor S(q) as a function of wave number q for increasing activity τ, showing an enhanced and sharpened dominant peak. Inset: S(q) for increasing ξ. Pa- rameters: r0 = −0.5, u = κ = q0 = 1.0, D = 0.5, ξ = a = 0; τ = 1.0 in the inset.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30887_figures/2605.30887_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Binder cumulant U Q 4 for the isotropic–nematic tran- sition at different activity strengths τ (inset: dependence on ξ). Increasing activity shifts the transition to higher temper- atures, indicating enhanced stability of the nematic phase. Parameters: u = κ = q0 = 1.0, D = 0.02, ξ = a = 0, L = 16π, R = 256; τ = 1.0 in the inset.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes how active noise modifies fluctuation-driven first-order phase transitions in condensed matter systems. The authors show that increasing activity systematically suppresses the fluctuation corrections, thereby stabilizing the ordered phase at higher temperatures. This establishes activity as a powerful, generic control parameter for tuning the transition strength.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a general theoretical framework for understanding how non-equilibrium driving forces (activity) can tune the nature of phase transitions, moving them away from purely equilibrium predictions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how introducing activity, modeled by correlated noise, modifies fluctuation-induced first-order phase transitions, particularly those governed by the Brazovskii mechanism.

**Main result.** Activity systematically suppresses the fluctuation effects driving the first-order transition, shifting the transition to higher temperatures and enhancing ordering without causing spinodal instability.

**Method.** The analysis employs response field formalism, Dyson equations, and functional integral methods to calculate renormalized propagators and effective potentials in the presence of colored noise.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled by a scalar order parameter $\phi(x, t)$ governed by a relaxational dynamics equation derived from a Swift–Hohenberg-like free energy functional. Activity is introduced via non-equilibrium, spatiotemporally correlated noise $\zeta(x, t)$.

**Key observables.** Renormalized mass parameter ($r$), static structure factor $S(q)$, and the Binder cumulant $U_Q^4$ for the nematic order parameter $Q$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Activity strength (controlled by noise correlation time $	au$ and length $\xi$), and the bare parameters of the free energy functional ($r_0, u, \kappa$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on perturbative expansions (e.g., decoupling interaction terms) and assumes the system dynamics are well-described by the given relaxational equation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the suppression of fluctuation effects (e.g., Fig. 2 showing $r$ vs. $	au$) and the enhancement of ordering, with the Binder cumulant (Fig. 4) confirming the transition shift with activity.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical framework using response field methods to analyze the effect of activity on the transition character. It then develops the necessary equations of state and compares results across different activity limits ($	au 	o 0$ vs. $	au 	o \infty$), culminating in numerical simulations confirming the suppression of fluctuations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fluctuations can drive otherwise continuous phase transitions to first order through the Brazovskii mechanism. We study how these fluctuation-induced transitions are modified in active systems by introducing nonequilibrium spatiotemporally correlated noise. We show that, while the transition remains fluctuation-induced first order, activity systematically suppresses these fluctuation effects, shifting the transition to higher temperatures and rendering it increasingly weakly first order. As a result, ordering is enhanced without inducing a spinodal instability of the isotropic phase, as confirmed by direct numerical simulations. In the strong-activity limit, fluctuation effects disappear and mean-field behavior is recovered. Our results identify activity as a generic control parameter for tuning the strength of fluctuation-induced first-order transitions.

</details>

### [Mean-squared displacements of rough particles in polydisperse granular gases](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30474v1)

**Authors:** Anna S. Bodrova  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30474v1>

**Summary.** This theoretical work analyzes the diffusion and mean-squared displacements of rough particles in a granular gas. By incorporating particle roughness via restitution coefficients, the authors show that particle motion is highly sensitive to these mechanical properties. The key finding is the emergence of ultraslow, logarithmic diffusion at long times, providing insights into non-equilibrium transport phenomena.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical granular dynamics, the study of non-equilibrium energy dissipation, cooling laws (Haff's law), and anomalous diffusion ($\log t$ scaling) provides theoretical tools relevant to understanding energy relaxation in complex many-body systems, which has parallels in quantum many-body physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the diffusion coefficients and mean-squared displacements (MSDs) of rough particles within a polydisperse granular gas undergoing a homogeneous cooling state.

**Main result.** The MSD exhibits ultraslow motion characterized by a logarithmic time-dependence ($\langle R^2(t) angle \propto \log t$) at long times, and the particle motility is strongly influenced by surface roughness.

**Method.** The analysis uses kinetic theory approaches, deriving MSDs from velocity correlation functions by solving coupled differential equations for granular temperatures.

**Model / system.** The system is a polydisperse granular gas modeled by binary collisions, incorporating particle roughness via normal ($\epsilon$) and tangential ($eta$) restitution coefficients. The dynamics are analyzed in the homogeneous cooling state.

**Key observables.** Diffusion coefficients ($D_k(t)$), Mean-Squared Displacements ($\langle \mathbf{R}^2(t) angle$), and partial granular temperatures ($T_{tr,k}, T_{rot,k}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Normal and tangential restitution coefficients ($\epsilon, eta$), particle size/mass polydispersity, and time scales ($	au_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is assumed to be in a rarefied gas regime dominated by binary collisions, and approximations like the adiabatic limit and molecular chaos hypothesis are employed.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time dependence of partial MSDs for ternary mixtures, showing dependence on parameters like $eta$ and particle mass/density.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the system dynamics and collision rules to deriving the MSD in terms of velocity correlation functions, applying approximations like the adiabatic limit, and finally presenting the long-time asymptotic behavior of the MSD.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the diffusion coefficients and mean-squared displacements in a polydisperse granular gas in a homogeneous cooling state by considering the roughness of the particles. We study their dependence on the normal and tangential restitution coefficients. We show that the motility of particles is strongly affected by their mechanical properties and surface characteristics.

</details>

### [Topological Phenomena Protected by Diabolical Textures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30421v1)

**Authors:** Sayantan Mandal, Neelima Pulletikurty, Abhishodh Prakash  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30421v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

</details>

<img src="2605.30421_figures/2605.30421_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

**Summary.** This work introduces 'diabolical textures' as a new source of topological phenomena in inhomogeneous quantum systems, modeled using fermions on a lattice. The authors show that these textures create distinct gapped phases separated by unique 'trap-scaling' critical points. By combining continuum field theory with homotopy methods, they establish a general classification scheme for these robust topological features.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of 'diabolical textures' provides a powerful, geometrically motivated framework for classifying topological phases beyond standard symmetry protection, which is highly relevant for designing novel quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates a novel class of topological phenomena arising from the adiabatic spatial variation of quantum states, termed 'diabolical textures,' in inhomogeneous electronic systems.

**Main result.** These distinct topological textures lead to gapped states separated by unique 'trap-scaling' critical points, which are stable against weak perturbations and can be systematically classified using homotopy theory.

**Method.** The analysis combines continuum field theory (bosonization) with finite-size scaling arguments, mapping the low-energy physics near criticality to a 2D Dirac fermion in a magnetic field.

**Model / system.** The primary model is a non-interacting fermion system described by a lattice Hamiltonian, exemplified by the spatially embedded Thouless pump (Rice-Mele model). The theory is generalized to arbitrary spatial dimensions and symmetries.

**Key observables.** Spectral gap ($\Delta$), entanglement entropy ($S(b)$), and the critical scaling exponent ($\vartheta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The parameters defining the texture, such as $\delta$ and $J$, and the critical point characterized by $\alpha^* = \pm 1$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes slow spatial variation of parameters and relies on bosonization techniques near the critical points.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Thouless pump's spatial embedding, the finite-size scaling of the spectral gap ($\Delta \sim L^{-1/2}$), and the comparison of single-particle energy spectra between lattice and continuum descriptions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from introducing the physical model (Thouless pump/Rice-Mele), deriving the low-energy continuum theory, establishing the unique trap-scaling critical behavior, and finally generalizing the classification framework using homotopy theory.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a new class of topological phenomena in inhomogeneous systems arising from the adiabatic spatial embedding of parametrized families of quantum states such as charge pumps and their generalizations. We demonstrate that each topologically distinct class of these "diabolical textures" gives rise to distinct gapped states that are separated by "trap-scaling" critical points. When the texture varies sufficiently rapidly in space, the critical line terminates abruptly, producing an "unnecessary critical" surface. We demonstrate our results using a microscopic model of non-interacting fermions with a spatially embedded Thouless pump. We study its phase diagram comprehensively and establish its stability to arbitrary perturbations, including interactions, in the vicinity of the critical regions. For systems in arbitrary spatial dimensions and global symmetries, we present a framework to systematically classify diabolical textures using Kitaev's $Ω$ spectrum conjecture.

</details>

### [Functional methods for quantum thermodynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31112v1)

**Authors:** Sibo Wang, Samuel Degen, Haozhao Liang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31112v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. Flow evolution for scheme I (minimal closure) at T/g = 1.0 and N = 5.0. Panels (a)–(c) show the running free energy per particle, chemical potential, and connected two-density correlator as functions of λ, respectively. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31112_figures/2605.31112_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Equation of state from scheme I (minimal closure) with SIC at three different temperatures T/g. Panels (a) and (b) show ¯E/g and g ˜G(2), respectively, compared with the exact thermodynamics.</sub>

</details>

</details>

<sub>FIG. 5. The same as Fig. 1, but for the truncation scheme IV (maximum-entropy closure).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work advances Functional Renormalization Group Density Functional Theory (FRG-DFT) to quantum thermodynamics by benchmarking it against the exactly solvable single-site Bose-Hubbard model. The authors identify that a self-interaction correction term is necessary for formal consistency, and they demonstrate that the maximum-entropy closure scheme provides the most accurate description of thermodynamic observables and density correlations. This establishes a controlled foundation for deriving quantum many-body functionals.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous development of FRG-DFT provides a powerful, non-perturbative tool for calculating quantum thermodynamic properties, offering a systematic path to derive ab initio functionals applicable across condensed matter, ultracold atoms, and nuclear physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop a systematic and controllable functional route using Functional Renormalization Group (FRG) to derive energy functionals for quantum many-body systems in the context of quantum thermodynamics.

**Main result.** The maximum-entropy closure scheme (Scheme IV) is shown to be the most accurate, successfully reproducing exact thermodynamic quantities and the low-temperature oscillatory structure of density correlators.

**Method.** The core method is FRG-DFT, which involves deriving flow equations for the effective action by systematically truncating an infinite hierarchy of equations derived from the imaginary-time path integral.

**Model / system.** The primary testbed is the single-site Bose-Hubbard model, which is analytically solvable and allows for rigorous benchmarking against exact finite-temperature thermodynamics.

**Key observables.** Free energy, chemical potential, and connected density correlators ($	ilde{G}^{(2)}, 	ilde{G}^{(3)}, 	ilde{G}^{(4)}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($T$), interaction strength ($g$), and particle density ($ho$ or $N$). The low-temperature (strong-coupling) regime is a key diagnostic test.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation requires a careful Hubbard-Stratonovich transformation and the inclusion of a Self-Interaction Correction (SIC) term to maintain formal consistency. The analysis relies on comparing results across various closure approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the flow evolution of $	ilde{G}^{(2)}$ and other correlators across different closure schemes (I, II, III, IV), demonstrating that Scheme IV best reproduces exact results, especially the oscillatory structure at low temperatures.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the general FRG-DFT formalism, then applies it to the single-site Bose-Hubbard model, deriving the necessary SIC term. It then systematically compares four different truncation/closure schemes (I-IV) by benchmarking their predictions for thermodynamic observables against exact solutions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The functional renormalization group provides a nonperturbative and systematically improvable route to constructing density functionals for quantum many-body systems from microscopic Hamiltonians. Here we advance this program by benchmarking functional-renormalization-group density functional theory (FRG-DFT) against the exact thermodynamics of the single-site Bose-Hubbard model. This model provides an ideal testing ground because it is analytically solvable, yet remains subtle in the imaginary-time coherent-state path integral, where a naive continuum treatment generates a spurious self-interaction. We show that a careful Hubbard-Stratonovich derivation identifies the self-interaction correction term that must be included in the FRG-DFT flow to recover the exact thermodynamics. We then systematically compare several closures of the resulting hierarchy of flow equations for the free energy, chemical potential, and connected density correlators over broad ranges of density, temperature, and interaction strength. The benchmark shows that the free energy is comparatively robust, whereas the chemical potential and fluctuation observables provide much sharper diagnostics of the hierarchy closure. A maximum-entropy closure gives the most accurate overall description and reproduces even the low-temperature oscillatory structure of the connected two-density correlator. These results identify two general requirements for functional approaches to quantum thermodynamics: the renormalization group flow equation must retain the equal-time contact subtraction to avoid spurious self-interactions, and any closure of the hierarchy must preserve the statistical consistency of density correlators. This work provides a controlled foundation for deriving ab initio density functionals for quantum many-body systems across condensed-matter, ultracold-atom, and nuclear physics, as well as quantum chemistry.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31233"></a>
### [Global thermodynamics for heat-conducting fluids under weak gravity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31233v1)

**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31233v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.31233_figures/2605.31233_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 Schematic comparison of the chemical-potential profile in (a) equilibrium under gravity and (b) a heat-conducting steady state under gravity. **Summary.** This paper develops a global thermodynamic framework to analyze liquid-gas coexistence in fluids subjected to both heat conduction and weak gravity. It constructs a variational free-energy function, decomposing it to show that while the effective gravity dictates the primary phase ordering, a residual term is crucial for correctly calculating macroscopic thermodynamic observables. This work provides a general methodology for incorporating non-equilibrium driving forces into phase coexistence theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous application of global thermodynamics to non-equilibrium systems, particularly separating the ordering principle from residual non-equilibrium corrections, provides a powerful theoretical template for analyzing complex phase transitions in open quantum or classical systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the global thermodynamics of liquid-gas coexistence in heat-conducting fluids subjected to weak gravity.

**Main result.** The free-energy landscape is decomposed into an effective-gravity part determining the primary phase ordering, and a residual excess-latent-heat contribution necessary for deriving fundamental thermodynamic relations and observables.

**Method.** The authors employ a global variational free-energy function approach, extending equilibrium thermodynamics to incorporate non-equilibrium effects from heat conduction and gravity.

**Model / system.** The system is a fluid undergoing liquid-gas coexistence in a container under weak gravity and imposed temperature gradients. The analysis uses the van der Waals model for numerical illustration.

**Key observables.** Spatially averaged pressure, global chemical potential, and the variational free-energy landscape structure.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weak gravity ($mgL$), temperature difference ($\Xi$), and the dimensionless parameter $\varepsilon$ characterizing the weak coupling regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a sharp interface and operates in the linear response regime, neglecting explicit interfacial transport processes.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate variational free-energy landscapes in $(\xi_1, \xi_2)$ space, showing how temperature differences ($\Xi$) deform the barrier shape and how the ordering changes across different physical regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from setting up the global thermodynamic framework, deriving steady-state conditions via variational minimization, decomposing the free energy into effective-gravity and residual parts, and finally analyzing the resulting free-energy landscape structure.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study liquid-gas coexistence under gravity and heat conduction from the viewpoint of global thermodynamics. We construct a variational free-energy function for the fixed-global-temperature description and decompose it into two parts. The first has the same configurational form as the equilibrium weak-gravity free energy with gravity replaced by the effective gravity, and it determines the first-order configurational transition between the two separated liquid-gas arrangements. The second is a residual excess-latent-heat contribution that vanishes without heat conduction. Although it does not decide which separated liquid-gas arrangement is thermodynamically favored, this residual part is needed to derive the fundamental relation in the laboratory variables and to recover thermodynamic observables such as the spatially averaged pressure. The same residual contribution reshapes the barrier geometry, ridge/valley structure, and interfacial anomalies of the fixed-global-temperature free-energy landscape. Numerical examples based on the van der Waals model illustrate the resulting landscape structure, and estimates of experimental scales suggest a setup for detecting the effective-gravity inversion.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31262"></a>
### [Non-linear density scaling of spin noise reveals atomic correlations in warm vapors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31262v1)

**Summary.** The researchers used high-bandwidth spin noise spectroscopy on warm rubidium vapor to measure how spin noise changes with atomic density. They found a non-linear, quadratic increase in noise at high densities, which they attribute to resonant dipole-dipole interactions between atoms. By experimentally quenching these interactions, they confirmed that this non-linear scaling is indeed a signature of atomic cross-correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a direct, measurable spectroscopic signature (non-linear noise scaling) for many-body quantum correlations in a simple, controllable atomic system, extending SNS beyond simple magnetization measurements.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To experimentally characterize non-linear atomic correlations in warm alkali vapors by measuring the density dependence of spin noise.

**Main result.** The spin noise variance exhibits a quadratic, superlinear dependence on atomic density at high concentrations, which is attributed to resonant dipole-dipole interactions (DDI).

**Method.** High-bandwidth Spin Noise Spectroscopy (SNS) is employed to measure Faraday rotation fluctuations in a warm rubidium vapor.

**Model / system.** The system is a warm alkali vapor (rubidium) probed by a detuned laser, where the noise measurement probes spin correlations arising from resonant dipole-dipole interactions (DDI).

**Key observables.** Spin noise variance ($\langle \delta	heta^2 angle$) and the spin noise power spectral density (PSD).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Vapor density ($n$), detuning ($\Delta$), and input probe power ($P_p$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The non-linear scaling is assumed to arise from DDI, which is tested by quenching the interaction using an auxiliary resonant beam.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the non-linear (quadratic) scaling of spin noise variance vs. density, and demonstrate the suppression of this non-linearity and spectral distortions when the DDI is quenched.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the linear regime (non-interacting) and then presents evidence for a superlinear, quadratic scaling at high densities. The core argument is built by introducing and then experimentally suppressing the DDI via an auxiliary beam, confirming the correlation origin.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We experimentally demonstrate a non-linear dependence of the spin noise variance on atomic density in a warm alkali vapor. Implementing high-bandwidth spin noise spectroscopy (SNS) near the D2 transition of rubidium, a quadratic spin noise contribution is shown to arise at high densities, in contrast with the linear dependence valid in non-interacting ensembles. This non-linear scaling is shown to crucially depend on the residual optical excitation of the vapor by the probe beam, suggesting it stems from atomic cross-correlations due to resonant dipole-dipole interaction (DDI) in the vapor. We support this claim by introducing an additional experimental protocol to quench the ddi, resulting in a suppression of both the quadratic scaling of the spin variance and the distortions of the spin noise spectrum induced by the interaction. These results extend the applications of SNS to the characterization of many-body correlations in complex quantum systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31435"></a>
### [Can a spin-half particle ever give more than two spots in a Stern-Gerlach experiment? -- the subtle physics of effective Hamiltonians](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31435v1)

**Authors:** Noah Linden, Sandu Popescu, Anthony J. Short  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31435v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31435_figures/2605.31435_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: a) A standard Stern-Gerlach experiment for a spin-half particle. b) The same Stern- Gerlach experiment, but the spin passing through the device interacts with an external spin-half particle which remains outside the device and constrains the spins to be parallel. Note that the outer spots in the second figure are in same positions as in the first figure. c) Similar to figure b), but the spin-half particle inside the device is interacting with a spin-one particle outside.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31435_figures/2605.31435_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: a) shows a hypothetical three-layer material composed of spin 1</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper demonstrates that strong coupling between a spin-half particle and another spin can induce an effective spin-$s$ behavior. This allows the particle to yield $2s+1$ distinct outcomes in a Stern-Gerlach measurement, contrary to expectations based on its intrinsic spin. The analysis relies on projecting the system dynamics onto a low-energy subspace defined by the total spin quantum number.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics as it explores how strong, engineered interactions (like those in coupled quantum systems or artificial lattices) can induce emergent, higher-dimensional quantum behavior from fundamentally lower-dimensional constituents.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether a spin-half particle, when strongly coupled to another spin, can exhibit measurement outcomes in a Stern-Gerlach experiment corresponding to a higher effective spin value.

**Main result.** A spin-half particle can effectively behave as if it has a spin-$s$ character, leading to $2s+1$ distinct spots in the Stern-Gerlach measurement, despite its fundamental spin being $1/2$.

**Method.** The analysis uses the theory of effective Hamiltonians to approximate the dynamics generated by the full Hamiltonian, particularly in the limit of large energy gaps ($E_0$).

**Model / system.** The system involves a spin-half particle coupled to an arbitrary spin ($S_2$) via an interaction Hamiltonian ($H_0$). The measurement is modeled by coupling the system to a spatially varying magnetic field ($V_{	ext{SG}}$).

**Key observables.** The number of distinct spots observed in the Stern-Gerlach experiment ($2s+1$), the effective gyromagnetic ratio ($\gamma_{	ext{eff}}^1$), and the trace distance ($\mu(t)$) between state evolutions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling strength ($eta$), the energy scale ($E_0$), and the spatial confinement width ($z_0$) are crucial parameters governing the validity of the approximations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximating the evolution using effective Hamiltonians, assuming the coupling is strong enough to keep spins parallel, and that the measurement process can be bounded by a finite region.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares standard SG measurements with those involving external spin particles. Figure 2 illustrates a hypothetical three-layer material where strong inter-layer interactions constrain spins, effectively modeling a larger spin system.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the problem using the full Hamiltonian, then derives the effective Hamiltonian by projecting onto the total spin subspace ($\Pi_s$). It rigorously proves that the effective operators act like standard spin-$s$ operators, leading to the prediction of multiple spots. Finally, it analyzes the measurement fidelity using state distance metrics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that a spin-1/2 particle can behave as if it were spin-$s$, and generate $2s+1$ spots in a Stern Gerlach measurement (albeit with a smaller gyromagnetic ratio). This arises from some subtle properties of effective Hamiltonians and Hamiltonians with constraints. Examples of implications of the effect in condensed matter are discussed. We also give some simple non-perturbative bounds for a system subjected to strong constraints.

</details>

### [Asymptotic distinguishability of Haar-averaged measurement models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31168v1)

**Authors:** Ludmiła Marcinkowska, Łukasz Pawela, Marcin Markiewicz, Zbigniew Puchała  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31168v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31168_figures/2605.31168_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Discrimination protocol between the identity channel I and the Haar-random measurement channel Q(N) U . The maximally entangled state |ψ⟩is prepared across N subsystems. The channel Q(N) U acts collectively on these subsystems, followed by the joint measurement {Π0, Π1}.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31168_figures/2605.31168_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Equivalent representations of the collective model. The multipartite implementation using N copies of Q(1) U is equivalent to the collective channel Q(N) U acting on the product input state |0⟩⊗N, producing the classical outcome vector k = (k0, . . . , kN−1).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31168_figures/2605.31168_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Equivalent representations of the block model. The multipartite implementation using n1 copies of Q(1) U and n2 copies of Q(1) V is equivalent to two collective channels Q(n1) U and Q(n2) V acting independently on the corresponding subsystems, producing the classical outcome vectors k(1) and k(2). In the aggregate experiment, the observer retains only the total histogram obtained after forgetting the block assignment of the outcomes.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31168_figures/2605.31168_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Limiting aggregate total variation distance between P (N)(λ) and P (n1,n2)(λ), i.e. TVD∞(3, α), as a function of α.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.31168_figures/2605.31168_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Limiting aggregate total variation distance between P (N)(λ) and P (n1,n2)(λ), i.e. TVD∞(4, α), as a function of α.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work rigorously analyzes how distinguishable two measurement models are when the underlying randomness is either collective (shared unitary) or local (independent unitaries). By calculating the Total Variation Distance in various asymptotic limits, the authors show that the level of observation—whether aggregate or block-resolved—fundamentally changes the measurable distinguishability.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical tools developed for quantifying distinguishability between structured vs. unstructured quantum processes (shared vs. independent unitaries) are highly relevant for understanding resource quantification in quantum information theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper studies the asymptotic distinguishability between measurement models generated by a single collective unitary versus those generated by independent local unitaries.

**Main result.** The distinguishability, quantified by the Total Variation Distance (TVD), depends critically on the observation level: the aggregate statistics yield a nontrivial limit, while the block-resolved statistics show perfect distinguishability in certain limits.

**Method.** The authors use Weingarten calculus and combinatorial analysis to derive closed-form expressions and asymptotic limits for the TVD in various scaling regimes.

**Model / system.** The model involves Haar-random measurement-and-prepare channels acting on a composite system of $N=n_1+n_2$ subsystems of local dimension $d$. The comparison is between the collective channel $Q(N)_U$ and the block-separated channel $Q(n_1, n_2)_{U,V}$.

**Key observables.** Type-II error probability, Total Variation Distance (TVD) between aggregate and block-resolved histogram laws, and the block-resolved pair-of-histograms law.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis covers multiple asymptotic regimes: fixed-$N$, large-$d$; fixed-$d$, large-$N$; sparse joint-scaling ($N = o(\sqrt{d})$); and critical scaling ($N/\sqrt{d} 	o c$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis distinguishes between the aggregate (block-unresolved) and block-resolved statistics, and relies on asymptotic expansions derived from Haar measure integration.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the discrimination protocol using entangled testers and show the relationship between the collective and multipartite measurement models.

**Paper structure.** The paper first addresses channel discrimination (Type-II error) and then focuses on comparing the aggregate vs. block-resolved histogram laws using the TVD, deriving specific asymptotic limits for different scaling regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study discrimination problems generated by the same basic Haar-random measurement mechanism at two observational levels. First, we derive an explicit expression for the type-II error in the task of discriminating a Haar-random measure-and-prepare channel from the identity channel $\mathbb{I}$, using a coherence-sensitive entangled tester. Second, after passing to the induced classical measurement records, we compare two random measurement models: one induced by a single collective unitary of the form $U^{\otimes (n_1+n_2)}$ with $U\in U(d)$, and another induced by independent local unitaries $U_1^{\otimes n_1}\otimes U_2^{\otimes n_2}$. For the associated Haar-averaged aggregate histogram laws, in which the block of origin of each count is not retained, we obtain closed-form formulas and quantify their discrepancy through the total variation distance. We derive asymptotic expressions in the fixed-$N$, large-$d$ regime, the fixed-$d$, large-$N$ regime, the sparse joint-scaling regime $N=o(\sqrt d)$, and the critical scaling regime $N/\sqrt d\to c$. We also identify the block-resolved pair-of-histograms law, showing that the aggregate total variation distance is a coarse-grained lower bound on the distinguishability available when block labels are retained.

</details>

### [Rényi divergences and binary state discrimination error exponents for fermionic quasi-free states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31379v1)

**Authors:** Milán Mosonyi, Gábor Maróti-Zareczky  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31379v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31379_figures/2605.31379_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31379_figures/2605.31379_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31379_figures/2605.31379_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31379_figures/2605.31379_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.31379_figures/2605.31379_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops explicit formulas for various regularized Rényi divergences applicable to fermionic quasi-free states on infinite chains. By relating these divergences to the error exponents in quantum state discrimination, it provides a rigorous mathematical framework for quantifying distinguishability in complex quantum many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit calculation of divergences in the thermodynamic limit for fermionic systems provides powerful tools to analyze quantum correlations and distinguishability in many-body quantum matter models.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper quantifies the trade-off relations between error probabilities in binary quantum state discrimination, particularly for non-i.i.d. settings.

**Main result.** It derives explicit, closed-form formulas for various regularized Rényi divergences for a specific class of fermionic quasi-free states, establishing links between these divergences and the achievable error exponents.

**Method.** The analysis heavily relies on extending Szegő-type limit theorems from the theory of block Toeplitz operators to calculate the thermodynamic limit of quantum divergences.

**Model / system.** The system under study is a doubly infinite fermionic chain modeled by quasi-free states ($\omega_Q, \omega_R$) defined on the CAR-algebra. The states are characterized by single-particle symbols ($\hat{q}, \hat{r}$) acting on the torus.

**Key observables.** Error probabilities ($\epsilon_0, \epsilon_1$), error exponents ($d_r, s_r$), and various regularized Rényi divergences ($	ext{D}_{	ext{reg}}^{\alpha, \gamma}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The R\'enyi parameter $\alpha$, the internal dimension $d$ (number of modes per site), and the spectral properties of the symbols $\hat{q}(x)$ and $\hat{r}(x)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation requires the symbols $\hat{q}(x)$ and $\hat{r}(x)$ to be bounded away from 0 and 1, and for the symbols to be Lipschitz continuous on a non-degenerate interval.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining various Rényi divergences for quasi-free states (using limit theorems) to deriving explicit formulas for regularized divergences, and finally applying these results to establish the asymptotic error exponents for state discrimination.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The trade-off relations between the two types of error probabilities in binary i.i.d. quantum state discrimination can be expressed by single-copy formulas in terms of the Petz-type and the sandwiched Rényi divergences of the two states representing the two hypotheses. In the non-i.i.d. setting, the error exponents can usually be expressed in terms of regularized Rényi divergences, which do not admit explicit formulas in general. Here, we consider a class of states, translation-invariant and gauge-invariant quasifree states on doubly infinite fermionic chains, and give explicit formulas for a wide range of regularized Rényi divergences between such states, including $(α,z)$, log-Euclidean, maximal, measured, and the recently introduced integral Rényi divergences. We show that the case where there is a single mode at each lattice site becomes asymptotically classical, with all the different types of regularized Rényi divergences being equal, while in the case of multiple modes per site, non-commutativity persists under regularization, and for any fixed $α$, the regularized Rényi $(α,z)$-divergences give different regularized values for different $z$ parameters in general. We also generalize a previous construction from [Bunth, Maróti, Mosonyi, Zimborás, Lett.~Math.~Phys.~113:(7), 2023] to the case of multiple modes per lattice site to obtain a large class of states exhibiting super-exponential decay of the discrimination error probabilities.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31453"></a>
### [Solving models with generalized free fermions II: Path-product expansion and conserved charges](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31453v1)

**Authors:** Kohei Fukai, Balázs Pozsgay, István Vona  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31453v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31453_figures/2605.31453_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1 The claw graph K1,3, consisting of a center vertex connected to three mutually non-adjacent leaves.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31453_figures/2605.31453_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2 The two claw obstructions used in Lemma 3.3. In (a), an interior path vertex ℓi touched by j but not accompanied by either neighboring touched vertex would have three pairwise non-adjacent neighbors ℓi−1, ℓi+1, j. In (b), three touched path vertices no two of which are consecutive along the induced path are pairwise non-adjacent, so they form a claw with center j. The double-circled vertex marks the center of the forbidden claw in each panel. Black dotted path segments indicate omitted continuations of the induced path.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31453_figures/2605.31453_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5 The (2, 1) mixed-touch cancellation. The filled vertex is the Hamiltonian vertex of the commutator term: j in panel (a) and j′ in panel (b). Black dotted segments indicate omitted continuations of induced paths. Gray dashed edges indicate touching edges from the Hamiltonian vertex to the packed components. Yellow and blue halos mark the doubly touched side and the singly touched side, respectively. The braces label the path pieces LO, LE, L′, and L′ E used in the decompositions before and after the move. In the left panel the doubly touched component is denoted by L = LE-LO, where LE = L′ E-j′, while the singly touched component is denoted by L′. In the right panel the doubly touched...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31453_figures/2605.31453_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 6 Schematic one-component touch with spectators for c = 4. Only packing vertices and the Hamiltonian vertex are drawn; unrelated vertices and edges of the ambient graph are suppressed. The gray and red halos schematically indicate the closed neighborhoods of the spectator components and of the active component, respectively. Distinct halos may themselves overlap, but the packing condition Γ[La] ∩Lb = ∅for a ̸= b requires that no vertex of one packed component lie in the halo of another. Black path edges are the drawn induced paths, and the spectator components are carried along unchanged. The active packed path is drawn inside the red halo, while the filled black vertex is the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.31453_figures/2605.31453_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 7 Extended frustration graph Gχ for the Ising algebra, shown for the case M = 7. In general, the frustration graph G = PM is the path graph with M = 2L −1 vertices (white), where adjacent vertices correspond to anticommuting generators. The edge vertex χ (black) is connected to the simplicial clique Ks = {1}.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops a path-product expansion technique to explicitly determine the hidden free-fermion modes and local conserved charges in quantum spin systems defined on frustration graphs. By utilizing the Krylov basis generating function, the authors establish a direct link between graph structure and integrability, providing a unified framework for understanding conserved quantities in these models.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit, graph-theoretic construction of conserved charges provides a powerful, general tool for analyzing integrability in complex quantum spin models, applicable beyond standard lattice symmetries.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To derive an explicit, graph-theoretic structure for the hidden free-fermion modes and local conserved charges in quantum spin systems solvable by free fermions.

**Main result.** The free-fermion modes are shown to be expressible as path-product expansions along induced paths, and local conserved charges are determined by path products along induced paths with an odd number of vertices.

**Method.** The authors employ a path-product expansion derived from the generating function of the Krylov basis, bypassing the need for the transfer matrix or nonlocal charges.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on quantum spin systems defined on a frustration graph, particularly those belonging to the Even-Hole-Free and Claw-Free (ECF) classes. The Hamiltonian is governed by a graph Clifford algebra.

**Key observables.** Free-fermion modes, local conserved charges, and infinite-temperature dynamical correlation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The structure of the frustration graph (ECF or claw-free); the path length $|L|$ of induced paths.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The graph must be ECF for the primary mode derivation, but the charge construction extends to general claw-free graphs.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate claw obstructions and the structure of the extended frustration graph ($G_\chi$) used in the path-product expansion.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the framework by first introducing the graph-Clifford algebra, then deriving the path-product expansion for the free-fermion modes using the Krylov basis generating function, and finally applying this to derive explicit local and generalized conserved charges.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Free-fermion solvability in quantum spin systems is increasingly understood to be governed by a graph Clifford algebra defined from the frustration graph of the Hamiltonian. When the frustration graph belongs to certain classes, such as the even-hole-free and claw-free (ECF) class, the Hamiltonian is solvable by hidden free fermions: it admits a free-fermion solution although it does not reduce to a Majorana bilinear under the Jordan-Wigner transformation. However, unlike in the Jordan-Wigner case, where each mode is a linear combination of single Majorana fermions, the explicit operator structure of the hidden free-fermion modes -- and that of the local conserved charges -- has remained obscure. In this work, we derive a path-product expansion that expresses each free-fermion mode as a linear combination of path products along induced paths in the extended frustration graph. The expansion is obtained from the generating function of the Krylov basis and yields the modes directly, without using the transfer matrix or the nonlocal conserved charges as input. As an application, the mode decomposition computes infinite-temperature dynamical correlation functions for arbitrary ECF frustration graphs. We further obtain explicit expressions for local conserved charges as linear combinations of path products along induced paths; these charges apply beyond the free-fermion (ECF) class to more general claw-free frustration graphs. We also identify a unified family of generalized conserved charges that contains both the previously known nonlocal conserved charges and these local conserved charges as special cases. For Fendley's original FFD chain with homogeneous couplings and periodic boundary conditions, in a suitable basis, the structure of these local conserved charges exhibits the same Catalan-tree pattern as in the spin-$1/2$ XXX chain.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.30986"></a>
### [Dimensionality of a strongly interacting 2D-3D Fermi-Fermi mixture from the perspective of superfluid instability and excitation properties](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30986v1)

**Authors:** Haruka Takeda, Saki Hirai, Shumpei Iwasaki, Yoji Ohashi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30986v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30986_figures/2605.30986_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

Mean-ﬁeld BCS results for (a) superﬂuid order pa- rameter ∆at T = 0, and (b) superﬂuid phase transition tem- perature Tc, in a two-component 2D-3D Fermi-Fermi mixture with an s-wave pairing interaction. ǫF = k2 F/(2m) and kF is given in Eq.
