<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study examines how superfluidity behaves in a Fermi gas whose dimensionality is tuned between 3D-3D and 2D-3D. Using advanced many-body techniques, the authors find that pairing fluctuations suppress the critical temperature to zero in the 2D-3D limit. Crucially, they show that while the superfluid instability is dominated by the 2D component, the precursor pseudogap retains a 3D character, highlighting the observable dependence on dimensionality.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides detailed insights into how dimensionality fundamentally alters superfluidity and pairing mechanisms in ultracold atomic gases, distinguishing between mean-field predictions and fluctuation-dominated regimes.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the strong-coupling properties and superfluid instability of an attractively interacting Fermi-Fermi mixture as its dimensionality is tuned from 3D-3D to 2D-3D.

**Main result.** Pairing fluctuations suppress the superfluid transition temperature ($T_c$) to zero in the 2D-3D limit, which is governed by the lower-dimensional component. However, the pseudogap phenomenon retains a 3D character.

**Method.** The analysis employs the Self-Consistent T-matrix Approximation (SCTMA) and Generalized Random Phase Approximation (GRPA) to calculate superfluid properties and excitation spectra.

**Model / system.** The system is a two-component Fermi atomic gas with tunable s-wave pairing interaction, loaded in a mixed-dimensionality trap (2D in-plane, 3D along z).

**Key observables.** Superfluid transition temperature ($T_c$), superfluid order parameter ($\Delta$), Goldstone mode velocity ($\mathbf{v}_\varphi$), and single-particle spectral weight ($A_\sigma$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($U$), dimensional imbalance (2D-3D limit), and the pairing interaction strength.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of SCTMA is crucial for including pairing fluctuations, and the analysis relies on the Thouless criterion for determining $T_c$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the $T_c$ dependence, the vanishing $v_{\phi,z}$ component of the Goldstone mode velocity in the 2D-3D limit, and the pseudogap structure in the single-particle density of states.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical framework using SCTMA, then analyzing the superfluid instability via $T_c$ calculations, and finally examining the single-particle excitation spectrum, particularly the pseudogap, in the mixed-dimensional limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We theoretically investigate strong-coupling properties of an attractively interacting Fermi atomic gas, where the Cooper-pair formation occurs between atoms belonging to different dimensional bands. Including pairing fluctuations within the framework of the self-consistent $T$-matrix approximation (SCTMA), we examine how the BCS-type superfluid phase transition temperature $T_\mathrm{c}$ varies as one moves from the 3D-3D to the 2D-3D system, in the wide parameter region with respect to the strength of the pairing interaction. In the 2D-3D limit, we find that, while the mean-field BCS theory predicts $T_\mathrm{c}>0$ in the strong-coupling regime, $T_\mathrm{c}$ is remarkably suppressed down to zero by pairing fluctuations that are strongly enhanced by the mixed-dimensionality of the system. As the origin of this, we clarify that the lower-dimensional (2D) component dominates the superfluid instability, so that the vanishing $T_\mathrm{c}$ is the same phenomenon as that in the 2D-2D case. We also point out that this can already be seen in the mean-field level, when one examines the propagation of the Goldstone mode. On the other hand, we find that the pseudogap phenomenon, which is known as a precursor of Cooper-pair formation, exhibits a 3D character of the 2D-3D system. These results indicate that the dimensionality of a strongly interacting Fermi gas depends on what we observe.

</details>

### [Dually tunable YBCO coplanar waveguide resonators based on helium-ion-generated Josephson inductances](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30425v1)

**Summary.** This paper reports on the creation and characterization of highly tunable superconducting microwave resonators using YBCO thin films and Josephson junctions formed by helium ion irradiation. The key achievement is demonstrating that the resonators exhibit both periodic SQUID oscillations and a superimposed Fraunhofer-like modulation, allowing for independent control over resonance frequency and magnetic flux responsivity. This paves the way for advanced quantum sensing and circuit applications at elevated temperatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work directly addresses superconducting circuits using high-temperature superconductors (YBCO), which are central to the study of strongly correlated electron systems, and integrates quantum circuit elements (SQUIDs) into a measurable microwave platform.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to develop a flexible and reliable platform for superconducting microwave circuits capable of operating in high-magnetic-field and high-temperature regimes.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated strongly flux-tunable microwave resonators using YBCO coplanar waveguides integrated with helium-ion-generated Josephson junctions, exhibiting Fraunhofer-like modulation.

**Method.** The study involves fabricating YBCO CPW cavities, generating Josephson junctions via focused helium ion beam irradiation, and characterizing the resulting SQUID-resonator system using a Vector Network Analyzer (VNA) under applied magnetic flux.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a coplanar waveguide resonator made of YBCO, incorporating Josephson junctions (bJJs) forming SQUID loops. The analysis models the resonance frequency ($\omega_0$) and losses ($1/Q_{J0}$) as functions of the applied magnetic flux ($\Phi_b$) and temperature ($T_s$).

**Key observables.** Resonance frequency ($\omega_0$), linewidths ($\kappa$), critical junction current ($I_c$), and the Fraunhofer-like modulation envelope.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating temperatures up to 14 K; flux quantum ($\Phi_0$); ion dose ($D_{\ell}$); and the junction resistance ($R_J$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on fitting the data to established SQUID and Josephson junction models, though limitations regarding the simple model for $1/Q_{J0}$ are noted.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the SQUID flux-tuning curves showing periodic oscillations, overlaid with a broader Fraunhofer-like modulation envelope, and also show the temperature dependence of these characteristics.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from initial characterization of the YBCO platform and junction formation (dose dependence) to measuring the fundamental SQUID flux-tuning response, and finally analyzing the complex interplay between the periodic SQUID modulation and the superimposed Fraunhofer envelope, including temperature studies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconducting microwave circuits with and without Josephson inductances are the Swiss Army knife for many experiments and technologies from quantum information science to astrophysical particle detectors. Despite a large variety of existing circuit types, thin film materials and Josephson junction technologies, a flexible and reliable platform for high-magnetic-field and high-temperature applications is yet to be found. In this manuscript, we investigate coplanar waveguide cavities made of the high-temperature cuprate superconductor YBa$_2$Cu$_3$O$_7$ (YBCO), integrated with Josephson inductances and quantum interferometers that are generated by the controlled local irradiation of the YBCO with a focused helium ion beam. We obtain strongly flux-tunable microwave resonators, which not only display periodic interferometer oscillations of resonance frequency and decay rate, but also a superimposed Fraunhofer-like modulation pattern. The latter originates from the magnetic field tuning of the individual Josephson junction critical currents due to the out-of-plane junction barriers. It allows adjusting resonance frequency and flux responsivity independently of each other, potentially enabling tunable microwave circuits with low sensitivity to external magnetic-field noise over a broad range of frequencies. Finally, we investigate the temperature dependence of the cavities, show that they have promising characteristics up to 14$\,$K, and present a model for the junction-induced cavity losses.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical note tackles the complex physics of strong electron-boson coupling, bridging established theories like Migdal-Eliashberg superconductivity with modern models of quantum chaos, such as the SYK model. It develops sophisticated mathematical tools to analyze the self-energy and pairing mechanisms in these strongly correlated regimes. The work is significant for its attempt to unify descriptions of high-Tc superconductivity and quantum criticality using advanced field-theoretic techniques.

**Why it may be interesting.** The deep connection between superconductivity (Eliashberg theory) and quantum chaos models (SYK) provides a powerful, non-trivial theoretical lens for understanding emergent quantum phases in strongly correlated materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses subtle issues in strong phonon-like fermion-boson coupling and analyzes self-energy and gap functions in strongly correlated systems, extending concepts to supersymmetric generalizations of the SYK model.

**Main result.** The analysis provides simplified, coupled equations for the self-energy and gap function, showing how the ME approximation simplifies the Dyson-Schwinger equations, and establishes specific constraints for the SUSY-SYK model.

**Method.** The work employs advanced theoretical techniques including the Schwinger-Dyson equations, Migdal-Eliashberg approximations, algebraic ansatz solutions, and analysis using conformal field theory dimensions.

**Model / system.** The primary models are electron-phonon coupled systems (relevant for superconductivity) and the Sachdev-Ye-Kitaev (SYK) model, including its Yukawa and supersymmetric generalizations (YSYK, SUSY-SYK).

**Key observables.** Self-energy $\Sigma(\omega)$, pairing function $\Phi(\omega)$, condensation energy ($\delta F$), and the pairing susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling constants ($g$), frequency ($\omega$), and various dimensional exponents ($\Delta_\psi, \Delta_\phi$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis frequently relies on the Migdal-Eliashberg approximation (neglecting vertex corrections) and the large-N limit for simplification.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the framework for electron-phonon coupling via the ME approximation, then generalizing the self-energy analysis to NFL behavior using SYK/YSYK models, and finally applying these concepts to the highly constrained framework of SUSY-SYK models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This note addresses a number of subtle issues pertaining to the long-standing problem of strong phonon-like fermion-boson coupling. Among the central topics are the customary Migdal-Eliashberg approximation in the pertinent Schwinger-Dyson gap equation and its solutions. The previously gained insight is assessed by contrasting it against the various (non-)supersymmetric variants of the Yukawa-Sachdev-Ye-Kitaev model. Also, some previously discussed (pseudo-)holographic aspects of fermion pairing in such models are commented upon.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper derives the integral representation for the two-dimensional electron Green's function in the presence of a magnetic monopole field. It employs advanced mathematical tools, specifically the O(2,1) algebra and the operator method, to solve the governing master equation. The result provides a detailed spectral description of electron behavior in such topologically non-trivial magnetic environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection between topological defects (monopoles) and advanced algebraic structures like O(2,1) algebra, applied to fundamental quantities like the Green's function, provides deep insights into how topology influences electronic band structure in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to find the integral representation for the two-dimensional Green's function of an electron moving in the presence of a magnetic monopole field.

**Main result.** A full integral representation for the Green's function is derived, decomposing it into real and imaginary contributions involving Bessel functions.

**Method.** The methodology combines the operator method with the mathematical framework of the O(2,1) algebra, utilizing the Schwinger parametrization for the Green's function.

**Model / system.** The system models a nonrelativistic electron in 2D subjected to the magnetic field of a monopole, analogous to a 2DEG between solenoid ends. The governing equation is a master equation involving the electron's kinetic energy and the monopole field.

**Key observables.** The two-dimensional Green's function, G(rho, rho'|k).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic charge (related to $\Phi$), electron energy ($k$), and the geometry parameters ($ho, ho'$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the physical setup avoids the singularity at the center ($ho \sim a$).

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by setting up the master equation for the Green's function, applying the Schwinger parametrization, and then decomposing the solution using the properties of the O(2,1) algebra to yield the final integral form.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using the operator method and properties of $O(2,1)$ algebra, the integral representation for the two-dimensional Green's function of an electron in the field of a magnetic monopole is found. This representation is valid in all complex plane of the electron energy.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30419"></a>
### [New quantum information perspectives in the axion--photon and neutrino systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30419v1)

**Authors:** Aaditya Datar, Arun M. Thalapillil, Palak Thareja  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30419v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30419_figures/2605.30419_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Quantum information measures calculated for the axion–photon system, plotted as func- tions of the transition probability Pγ→a(z). Notice that all measures, except the capacity of entan- glement (which measures the fluctuation rather than the magnitude of the entanglement spectrum), follow a similar evolution—the measures, although providing complementary perspectives, are related to each other as they involve the same Schmidt weight in different functional forms. It is also impor- tant to emphasise again that the maximum transition probability attained depends on the values of α and β, and does not necessarily always span the full range [0, 1].</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30419_figures/2605.30419_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: An illustrative figure depicting the entanglement QSL T EE QSL in the (α, β) parameter space for the axion–photon system.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30419_figures/2605.30419_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: The entanglement QSL time T EE QSL plotted as a function of the evolution time T, over the respective full oscillation time periods for different values of β. In the plots α = 2 × 10−19 eV (top) and α = 9 × 10−19 eV (bottom), corresponding to the regimes α &lt; β and α &gt; β, respectively. The vertical lines correspond to the respective oscillation half-periods, where the transition probabilities are at their maxima. The diagonal, dashed line denotes a reference line T EE QSL = T. One observes that the entanglement bound is attained and saturated till some T, making the bound tight, and beyond that point the bound is weak with T &gt; T EE QSL.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work applies quantum information theory to study axion-photon mixing, treating it as a quantum oscillation problem analogous to neutrino oscillations. By analyzing entanglement measures and deriving an entanglement quantum speed limit, the authors establish fundamental, quantifiable limits on the rate and nature of the conversion process. This connects particle phenomenology directly to the fundamental principles of quantum information theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous application of quantum information theory tools, such as entanglement speed limits, to particle physics phenomena like axion-photon conversion provides a novel, fundamental constraint on particle physics models.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops a quantum information-theoretic framework to analyze axion-photon mixing, drawing parallels with neutrino oscillations.

**Main result.** It identifies characteristic thresholds for quantum information measures and derives an entanglement quantum speed limit that characterizes the intrinsic timescales of the conversion process.

**Method.** The analysis employs various quantum information measures (e.g., entanglement entropy, concurrence, discord) and compares multiple quantum speed limits (Mandelstam-Tamm, Margolus-Levitin, and entanglement-specific bounds).

**Model / system.** The system is the axion-photon pair coupled in the presence of a transverse external magnetic field, analyzed in the single-excitation sector. The dynamics are compared to the two-flavor neutrino oscillation model.

**Key observables.** Entanglement entropy, concurrence, negativity, quantum mutual information, discord, capacity of entanglement, and various quantum speed limits.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Axion mass ($\alpha$), mixing parameter ($eta$), detuning, and the external magnetic field strength ($B_{	ext{ext}}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the single-excitation sector, and comparisons between bases (interaction vs. propagation) highlight structural differences in entanglement measures.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of entanglement measures and the entanglement quantum speed limit on the mixing parameters ($\alpha$ and $eta$), showing distinct saturation and functional behaviors in different parameter regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically reviews axion-photon mixing, investigates various quantum information measures, and concludes with a detailed study and comparison of multiple quantum speed limits applicable to the system.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we broach a quantum information-theoretic treatment of axion--photon mixing. Motivated by an emerging class of quantum-enhanced axion searches, we analyse the two-level single-excitation sector of axion--photon oscillations, demonstrating how the coupled dynamics naturally generate bipartite axion--photon mode entanglement. We study in detail the ensuing aspects of entanglement entropy, concurrence, negativity, quantum mutual information and discord, and capacity of entanglement, and the corresponding neutrino analogues wherever novel and previously unaddressed. In particular, we highlight the characteristic features that connect maximal axion--photon entanglement to resonant or strong-mixing conversion, and the distinct thresholds for the extremal values attained by the quantum information measures. We study aspects of the Mandelstam--Tamm and Margolus--Levitin quantum speed limits for both the axion--photon and neutrino systems. While orthogonalisation occurs only at axion--photon resonance, or at maximal neutrino mixing, where the two bounds coincide, away from these limits, the Margolus--Levitin bound is saturated at maximal conversion, while the Mandelstam--Tamm bound is generally weaker. We also study an entanglement quantum speed limit for axion--photon conversion, that separates into detuning-dominated and magnetic-mixing-dominated regimes, and find that it is saturated for a period and then the bound becomes weak. The results in this work identify the quantum resources and limiting timescales intrinsic to axion--photon conversion, and connect axion phenomenology, neutrino oscillations and quantum information theory.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This theoretical study uses DFT to optimize Si/SiGe heterostructures for quantum computing by balancing two competing factors: valley splitting and spin coherence. The authors find that achieving high performance requires narrow quantum wells (3-4 nm) combined with precise isotopic purification to mitigate detrimental hyperfine coupling from nuclear spins.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly tackles the material science bottlenecks for solid-state qubits, providing quantitative guidelines on how structural parameters (like interface sharpness) and material composition (isotopic purification) must be controlled to realize functional quantum hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of co-optimizing spin coherence time and valley splitting in Si/SiGe heterostructures for use in semiconductor quantum devices.

**Main result.** Optimal performance requires balancing the competing effects of quantum well width, suggesting that 3-4 nm wide wells with specific isotopic concentrations can yield both large valley splittings (>500 ueV) and long coherence times (>15 uS).

**Method.** Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations were employed to model the electronic structure and calculate the hyperfine coupling responsible for spin dephasing.

**Model / system.** The system is based on Si/SiGe heterostructures, specifically modeling the confinement of electron spins in Si quantum wells (QWs) sandwiched between SiGe barriers.

**Key observables.** Valley splitting (E_v), spin dephasing time (T_2*), and the percentage of electronic wavefunction in the barriers (p_B).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quantum well width (t_QW), isotopic concentrations (e.g., 50 ppm of 73Ge and 29Si).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on DFT calculations, and the dephasing rate is calculated using the Fermi contact term approximation for hyperfine interaction.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how growth profiles and confinement lift valley degeneracy (Fig. 1), how wavefunction overlap with Ge nuclei increases in narrow wells (Fig. 2), and how interface quality affects T_2* (Fig. 3).

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical challenges (small E_v, hyperfine coupling) and then uses DFT to model the system. It analyzes the trade-off between narrowing the QW (which increases E_v but also increases coupling) and proposes optimal structural and isotopic parameters to maximize both metrics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Single electron spins can be used to encode and process information in semiconductor quantum devices. Progress has been hindered by materials challenges, such as the small energy splitting between low-lying valley states and hyperfine coupling to nuclear spins. Here we use density functional theory to optimize the valley splitting and spin dephasing time in realistic Si/SiGe heterostructures. Reductions in the Si quantum well width generally increase the valley splitting. However, in narrow quantum wells, a larger fraction of the electronic wavefunction resides in the SiGe buffer layers, which increases the hyperfine coupling with spinful $^{73}$Ge. Our work shows that Si/SiGe heterostructures with 3~--~4~nm wide quantum wells and $^{73}$Ge and $^{29}$Si concentrations of 50 ppm should support average valley splittings $E_{v}$~$>$~500~$μ$eV and spin dephasing times $T_2^*$ exceeding 15~$μ$s assuming an effective quantum dot area of 700 nm$^2$. In addition, sharper Si/SiGe interfaces in general result in larger valley splittings and longer spin dephasing times.

</details>

### [Fidelity bounds for spin-dependent kicks with pulsed lasers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31409v1)

**Summary.** This paper rigorously analyzes the fidelity of Spin-Dependent Kicks (SDKs) used in trapped-ion quantum computing. By moving beyond idealized assumptions, the authors determine that the primary source of error limiting gate fidelity is the finite duration of the laser pulses, not the ion's residual motion. The results provide actionable guidelines for achieving high-fidelity, sub-microsecond quantum operations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides crucial, quantitative design rules for implementing fundamental quantum gates (SDKs) in trapped ions by precisely quantifying the limitations imposed by realistic pulse laser technology.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize the control parameters required to maximize the fidelity of single-ion Spin-Dependent Kicks (SDKs) for implementing fast, high-fidelity two-qubit gates in trapped-ion quantum computing.

**Main result.** Finite pulse duration is found to be the dominant source of error, exceeding the contribution of secular motion by orders of magnitude for nanosecond-scale SDKs. Low infidelities below $10^{-3}$ are achievable with picosecond pulses.

**Method.** The analysis uses analytical methods and numerical simulations to model the evolution operator under pulsed Raman laser interactions, systematically relaxing approximations used in previous studies.

**Model / system.** The physical system is trapped ions with qubits encoded in hyperfine levels. The interaction is governed by a time-dependent Hamiltonian involving the trap frequency, qubit frequency, and pulsed laser fields, modeled as a train of short pulses.

**Key observables.** Fidelity of the SDK, state-averaged process fidelity ($ar{F}$), and state-averaged infidelity ($ar{I}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Raman frequency difference ($\delta$), pulse arrival times ($t_n$), Lamb–Dicke parameter ($\eta$), pulse width ($	au$), and total SDK time ($T_{SDK}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis moves beyond previous approximations by incorporating finite pulse width and considering the effects of ion secular motion, though the error from motion is found to be negligible compared to pulse duration errors.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the pulsed Raman SDK structure and energy level diagrams. Other figures compare infidelity vs. pulse number for different pulse widths, and show phase space pictures of SDK evolution.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first defining the ideal SDK operator, then analyzing the protocol by sequentially relaxing approximations (from large $N$ to finite $	au$ and motion), culminating in quantitative design rules based on the dominant error source.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Excitation of trapped-ion hyperfine qubits with fast optical Raman pulses enables faster-than-trap-period entangling gates with qubits of long coherence time for practical quantum computation. Achieving high-fidelity fast two-qubit gates requires high-quality spin-dependent kicks (SDKs), which form their fundamental building blocks. Here, we characterize the control parameters (including Raman frequency difference, pulse arrival times, Lamb--Dicke parameter, temperature, pulse width, and SDK time) that maximize the performance of single-ion SDKs for protocols compatible with performed experiments involving a small number of fast pulses. We demonstrate through analytical methods and numerical simulations that, within the model commonly used for infidelity optimization, finite pulse duration is the dominant source of error, exceeding the contribution of secular motion by orders of magnitude for nanosecond-scale SDKs. Low infidelities -- below $10^{-3}$ for schemes with $\gtrsim10$ fixed-amplitude, equispaced, picosecond pulses -- are achievable in SDK times on the order of nanoseconds. These results provide quantitative design rules for achieving competitive SDK fidelities with current pulsed-laser technology, laying the foundation for sub-microsecond trapped-ion quantum entangling operations.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study explores how quantum entanglement in top-antitop quark production can enhance the precision of measuring kinematic parameters like velocity and production angle. By calculating the Quantum Fisher Information Matrix and the concurrence, the authors demonstrate a direct link: strong quantum correlations signal regimes where quantum metrology offers superior measurement capabilities compared to classical methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the application is in particle physics, the core methodology—using quantum information measures (entanglement, QFIM) to enhance parameter estimation beyond classical limits—is directly applicable to quantum sensing and metrology problems in AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the interplay between quantum correlations (entanglement) and the precision limits achievable in simultaneously estimating multiple physical parameters ($eta$ and $\Theta$) during top-antitop quark pair production.

**Main result.** The simultaneous estimation strategy can yield a significant metrological advantage over individual estimations, and this advantage is strongly correlated with the degree of quantum entanglement present in the produced state.

**Method.** The authors utilized the spin density matrix formalism to model the system, deriving the Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM) to set the ultimate precision bounds, alongside calculating the concurrence to quantify entanglement.

**Model / system.** The physical system is top-antitop quark pair production ($g+g 	o tar{t}$) at high-energy colliders, modeled effectively as a bipartite spin-1/2 quantum state governed by relativistic parameters like velocity ($eta$) and production angle ($\Theta$).

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM), Concurrence, Estimation Variances ($\Deltaeta)^2, (\Delta\Theta)^2$, and the metrological advantage ratio $\Gamma(\Theta, eta)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Relativistic velocity ($eta$), production angle ($\Theta$), and the ultrarelativistic limit ($eta 	o 1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed at leading order in QCD, and the effective two-qubit description is valid due to the top quark's short lifetime.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of estimation variances on $\Theta$ and $eta$, showing optimal regimes, and plot the Concurrence versus $\Theta$ and $eta$, demonstrating entanglement revival in the ultrarelativistic limit.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives the quantum state using spin density matrices, calculates the QFIM for simultaneous estimation, compares this to individual estimations using the ratio $\Gamma$, and finally correlates the resulting metrological advantage with the calculated entanglement (concurrence).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the interplay between quantum correlations and multiparameter quantum estimation in top--antitop quark pair production through the gluon-fusion channel. Using the spin density matrix formalism, we construct an effective two-qubit quantum state governed by the relativistic parameters associated with the scattering process. Within the framework of quantum metrology, we derive the quantum Fisher information matrix for the simultaneous estimation of the relativistic velocity parameter and the production angle, and we analyze the corresponding quantum precision bounds. Our results reveal highly nontrivial estimation regimes strongly controlled by relativistic spin correlations and scattering geometry. We further characterize the produced state through the concurrence and demonstrate the existence of strong connections between entanglement structures and multiparameter estimation sensitivity. Finally, we discuss the experimental feasibility of probing these effects at the Large Hadron Collider through spin-correlation observables and reconstructed top--antitop density matrices. Our results identify top--antitop production as a unique relativistic platform for exploring quantum information theory and multiparameter quantum metrology in high-energy physics.

</details>

### [Non-Perturbative Renormalization Group for Ising-Nematic Criticality: A Closed-Form Nonlocal Ansatz](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30715v1)

**Authors:** Hyeon Jung Kim, Kyoung-Min Kim, Ki-Seok Kim  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30715v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30715_figures/2605.30715_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic representation of the antipodal patches on the Fermi surface and the low-energy scattering processes governed by the effective action.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30715_figures/2605.30715_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. The leading one-loop Feynman diagrams in the two- patch effective theory: (a) the one-loop fermion self-energy correction and (b) the one-loop boson polarization diagram, where solid lines represent dressed fermion propagators and wavy lines represent the nonlocal boson propagator.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30715_figures/2605.30715_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. The one-loop Yukawa vertex correction diagram.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30715_figures/2605.30715_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3 shows the one-loop correction to the Yukawa vertex. In the infrared limit, this correction vanishes, since the fermionic poles in the kx integration combine into a double pole whose residue gives no additional in- frared singularity. As a result, the one-loop vertex cor- rection does not introduce any additional infrared singu- larity, nor does it alter the leading scaling behavior of the Yukawa interaction. Thus, at this level of approximation, the Yukawa vertex may be consistently replaced by the coupling constant g in the low-energy effective action. The scaling structure is determined by requiring the three dominant terms in the dressed fermion propagator, namely</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30715_figures/2605.30715_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. The two-loop Feynman diagrams contributing to the boson self-energy: (a) the Maki-Thompson vertex-type cor- rection and (b) the diagram with a one-loop fermion self- energy insertion.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper applies a non-perturbative Renormalization Group technique to analyze the critical behavior of a metallic Ising-nematic quantum critical point. The authors calculate loop corrections up to three loops, finding that the system lacks a self-consistent fixed point for the critical exponent $a$. They conjecture that the required solution necessitates incorporating four-loop bosonic self-energy terms to achieve full theoretical consistency.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous, multi-loop RG approach to determine critical exponents in strongly correlated systems provides advanced theoretical tools applicable to understanding quantum phase transitions in materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to non-perturbatively determine the critical exponent $a$ characterizing the metallic Ising-nematic quantum critical point in two dimensions.

**Main result.** The three-loop analysis fails to yield a self-consistent fixed-point solution for $a$, suggesting that higher-order (four-loop) corrections are necessary to constrain the critical exponent.

**Method.** The authors employ a non-perturbative Renormalization Group (RG) analysis using a closed-form ansatz applied to a low-energy effective field theory.

**Model / system.** The model describes a metallic quantum critical point involving low-energy fermions coupled to a gapless bosonic nematic order parameter ($\phi$) in two dimensions.

**Key observables.** The critical exponent $a$, the fermion self-energy ($\Sigma$), the boson polarization ($\Pi$), and the Yukawa vertex correction ($\Gamma$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dynamical critical exponent $a$, and the anisotropic scaling dimensions $[k_0]=a+1$, $[k_x]=2$, $[k_y]=1$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of a closed-form RG ansatz and relies on matching conditions derived from scale invariance across multiple loop orders.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematic diagrams of scattering processes, one-loop and two-loop Feynman diagrams for self-energies and polarization, and numerical plots showing the lack of intersecting fixed points at three loops.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the RG framework by calculating loop corrections (self-energy, polarization, vertex) up to three loops, demonstrating structural asymmetries, and concluding that the fixed point requires higher-order, non-trivial bosonic counter-terms.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The two-dimensional metallic quantum critical problem is a long-standing puzzle that is widely believed to hold the key to resolving ubiquitous non-Fermi liquid behavior in strongly correlated electronic systems. In this study, we present a non-perturbative renormalization group (RG) analysis of the metallic Ising-nematic quantum critical point in two dimensions, formulated directly around an intrinsically nonlocal infrared (IR) boson propagator. Rather than treating the anomalous dynamical critical exponent $a$ as a fixed phenomenological parameter, we regard it as an intrinsic component of the fixed-point data to be determined from the internal consistency of the low-energy patch field theory under highly anisotropic scaling dimensions ($[k_0]=a+1$, $[k_x]=2$, $[k_y]=1$). While the leading two-loop diagrammatics vanish identically due to kinematic pole configurations, our three-loop evaluation reveals a profound structural asymmetry between the sectors: the fermion self-energy and Yukawa vertex receive non-vanishing logarithmic corrections, whereas the corresponding bosonic counter-term remains strictly zero. Consequently, we find that no self-consistent, intersecting fixed-point solution for the exponent $a$ exists within the three-loop truncation, failing to reproduce the physical value of $a \approx 1.85$ observed in quantum Monte Carlo simulations. We conjecture that the cross-linked topology of the four-loop boson self-energy diagrams is exactly marginal and yields the minimal, mandatory bosonic counter-term required to restore multi-sector self-consistency. Our framework establishes a rigid multi-loop matching scheme necessary to uniquely pin down the critical exponent, and uncovers a stable phase space for field anomalous dimensions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31160"></a>
### [Erasing photons from bright squeezed vacuum light via above-threshold ionization](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31160v1)

**Authors:** J. Rivera-Dean, T. Rook, G. Singh, P. Stammer, M. Khokhlova, E. Pisanty, C. Figueira de Morisson Faria

**Summary.** This paper shows that the process of Above-Threshold Ionization (ATI) driven by squeezed light can serve as a powerful, scalable mechanism analogous to photon subtraction in quantum optics. By detecting photoelectrons, the system filters the quantum state to generate large-amplitude optical Schrödinger cat states. These states exhibit clear non-classical features, allowing for the violation of Bell inequalities, thus opening strong-field physics for quantum state engineering.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges fundamental quantum optics concepts (cat states, photon subtraction) with high-intensity, non-linear strong-field physics (ATI), proposing a novel, scalable platform for generating macroscopic quantum states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is generating large-amplitude optical Schrödinger cat states, as standard photon subtraction protocols are limited in scalability and achievable macroscopicity.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that Above-Threshold Ionization (ATI) driven by bright squeezed light acts as a scalable, strong-field analogue of photon subtraction, enabling the generation of large-amplitude cat states that can violate a Bell inequality.

**Method.** The work employs a combination of quantum optics theory (photon subtraction formalism) and strong-field physics (ATI path integrals) to establish an analogy between the two processes.

**Model / system.** The system involves bright squeezed vacuum light interacting with atoms undergoing Above-Threshold Ionization (ATI). The theoretical framework models the post-selected optical state $|\Phi(\mathbf{p}_f, t)angle$ resulting from photoelectron detection.

**Key observables.** Wigner function negativity, violation of Bell inequalities (CHSH value), and the structure of the photoelectron probability amplitude $	ilde{c}(\alpha)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The squeezing strength ($r$), the detected photoelectron momentum ($\mathbf{p}_f$), and the finite momentum resolution ($\sigma_\mu$) are key parameters analyzed.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on semiclassical approximations (saddle-point methods) and assumes that the photoelectron detection acts as a highly selective, non-linear filter on the quantum state.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison between photon subtraction and photoelectron heralding, show the transformation of probability amplitudes, and display Wigner functions confirming cat-like features and Bell inequality violations.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first establishing the theoretical analogy between photon subtraction and ATI heralding. It then uses path integrals and semiclassical methods to derive the post-selected state, characterizes its non-Gaussian features, and finally tests its non-classicality by violating a Bell inequality while analyzing the impact of experimental noise.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

While the interface between strong-field physics and quantum optics offers a unique regime for combining extreme nonlinearity with quantum optical resources, its potential for generating non-classical states of light remains largely unexplored. Standard protocols for generating optical Schrödinger cat states, such as photon subtraction from squeezed light, are inherently limited in the achievable macroscopicity of the state and its scalability. In this work, we bridge this gap by demonstrating that above-threshold ionization driven by bright squeezed light provides a strong-field analogue of photon subtraction, where photoelectron detection acts as a high-intensity heralding mechanism, enabling the generation of large amplitude optical Schrödinger cat states. We characterize the resulting non-Gaussian features and show that they can be tuned via the detected photoelectron momentum, and study their robustness against the experimental imperfections arising from finite momentum resolution at the heralding step. Despite the noise, we show that the generated states can be manipulated to violate a Bell inequality, thereby highlighting their potential for foundational and practical applications. Our results establish strong-field processes as a scalable platform for macroscopic quantum state engineering, opening a route to quantum optics in previously inaccessible regimes.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30629"></a>
### [Search at bounded speed with immigration](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30629v1)

**Authors:** Hwai-Ray Tung, Sean D Lawley  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30629v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30629_figures/2605.30629_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Comparison of theory of section 3 with stochastic simulations of four search models. The black curves plot the relative error for approximations to the mean fFPT. The red curves plot the error for the approximations to the full distribution of the fFPT, where the error is the Kolmogorov-Smirnov distance (i.e. the maximum absolute difference between the cumulative distribution func- tions).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30629_figures/2605.30629_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Comparison of the mean fFPTs for the three “diffusion” processes of sections 5.1-5.3.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops rigorous mathematical results for estimating the time required for a search process to find a target when searchers arrive continuously (immigrate). It extends existing theory to account for searchers moving at a bounded speed, deriving exact distributions and moments in the fast immigration limit. The results are applied to canonical models like Brownian motion and run-and-tumble dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the core topic is stochastic search, the mathematical machinery involving convergence theorems, moment calculations, and the comparison between different physical diffusion models (BM vs. run-and-tumble) is highly relevant to non-equilibrium statistical physics and transport phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the search times for searchers that progressively enter or 'immigrate' into a domain at a constant Poissonian rate, specifically addressing the case where searchers move at a bounded speed.

**Main result.** In the fast immigration limit, the probability distribution and moments of the $k$-th search time ($T_k$) are determined, showing convergence to Erlang distributions.

**Method.** The analysis uses properties of inhomogeneous Poisson point processes and derives survival probabilities based on the short-time behavior of the single-searcher first passage time distribution.

**Model / system.** The models analyzed include 1D, 2D, and 3D run-and-tumble processes, Brownian motion, and linear PDMPs, all subjected to a constant immigration rate ($\lambda$).

**Key observables.** The $k$-th First Passage Time ($T_k$) and its moments ($E[T_k^m]$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Immigration rate ($\lambda$), system size ($L$), searcher speed ($v$), and the characteristic time scale ($t_{\min} = L/v$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is rigorously performed in the fast immigration limit ($\lambda 	o \infty$) and assumes the underlying single-searcher process ($	au$) has a specific short-time distribution.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the relative error of theoretical approximations against stochastic simulations for four different search models. Figure 2 compares the mean first passage time across three processes (BM, discrete, run-and-tumble) showing convergence/divergence regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops general theorems for the fast immigration limit, applies these theorems to various physical models (run-and-tumble, BM), and compares the theoretical predictions against numerical simulations, paying close attention to the breakdown of approximations in different parameter regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many biophysical search processes employ searchers which enter or "immigrate" into the domain progressively over time. Existing search time estimates can become unphysical for fast immigration since they imply that searchers move at infinite speed. In this paper, we investigate search times of immigrating searchers that move at bounded speed. In the fast immigration limit, we determine the full probability distribution and all the moments of the $k$th search time in terms of the early time probability distribution of a single searcher. We apply these rigorous mathematical results to several canonical models of stochastic search. We further analyze different models of "diffusion" and use these results to investigate when and how the minutiae of searcher dynamics affect search times. We compare our theory to numerical simulations.

</details>

### [(Non-)Traversable Quantum Phase Transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31472v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel geometric criterion to classify Quantum Phase Transitions (QPTs) as either 'traversable' or 'nontraversable'. Traversability is determined by whether the ground-state manifold has a finite geometric distance across the transition point, which is tested using counterdiabatic driving techniques. This classification offers a fundamental, dynamical understanding of QPTs, independent of local order parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, geometry-based diagnostic tool that transcends traditional classifications (like symmetry breaking or RG fixed points) for understanding the dynamical feasibility of quantum state preparation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop a geometric classification of Quantum Phase Transitions (QPTs) based on whether the transition can be dynamically connected (traversed) between the two phases.

**Main result.** A QPT is classified as 'traversable' if the geometric distance between the ground states in the thermodynamic limit is finite, allowing for exact counterdiabatic driving; otherwise, it is 'nontraversable'.

**Method.** The authors use the framework of counterdiabatic (CD) driving and analyze the geometry of the ground-state manifold using the fidelity susceptibility ($\chi_F$) to define a finite geometric length ($\ell$).

**Model / system.** The analysis applies to general many-body systems described by a tunable Hamiltonian $H(\lambda)$, with specific examples including the Transverse Field Ising Chain (TFIC) and the Ising Lipkin-Meshkov-Glick (LMG) model.

**Key observables.** Geometric distance ($\ell$), Fidelity Susceptibility ($\chi_F$), and the constant-speed CD driving schedule.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The thermodynamic limit ($L 	o \infty$), the divergence exponent $\alpha_F$, and the control parameter $\lambda$ near the critical point $\lambda_c$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on constructing a constant-speed parametrization $\lambda_{cs}(t)$ to tame the divergence of the adiabatic gauge potential in the thermodynamic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the constant-speed CD driving protocol, showing how it maintains unit fidelity across the transition, and provide a genealogy summarizing examples of traversable (blue) and non-traversable (red) QPTs.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the geometric concept of traversability, develops the mathematical machinery using CD driving and the fidelity susceptibility, derives the constant-speed parametrization, and applies this criterion to classify known QPTs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum phase transitions manifest as an abrupt change in the ground state of a many-body system; yet it is an open question whether this sudden change necessarily precludes a continuous dynamical connection between the two phases. We introduce a classification of quantum phase transitions based on this geometric aspect of the ground-state manifold, that differs from known classifications. By leveraging the framework of counterdiabatic driving, we explicitly construct schedules that dynamically connect one phase to another. This strategy allows us to uncover a large class of quantum phase transitions, where the states on both sides are separated only by a finite geometric distance in the thermodynamic limit. We term such transitions traversable, since exact counterdiabatic driving links the two phases via a finite dynamical protocol in the thermodynamic limit. We show that multiple known transitions fall into this class -- e.g., symmetry-breaking transitions obeying hyperscaling and discontinuous transitions with an enhanced continuous symmetry. We further show the existence of quantum phase transitions that cannot be crossed dynamically even with the help of nonlocal counterdiabatic driving, as they would require divergent amplitudes and frequencies. Geometrically, these nontraversable transitions correspond to an infinite distance separating the two phases of matter; we show that the class comprises continuous transitions exhibiting mean-field universality, and discontinuous transitions arising from the competition between metastable minima. Our geometric classification goes beyond the known taxonomy, is independent of local order parameters and renormalization group fixed points, and has direct implications for the complexity of state preparation and adiabatic quantum computation.

</details>

### [How To Track Qubits Through Space and Time (Or: Sailing in a Quantum Boat)](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30732v1)

**Authors:** James Bartusek, Zikuan Huang, Leo Orshansky, Henry Yuen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30732v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30732_figures/2605.30732_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>Figure 2: Non-localizability attack on 𝑓-BB84</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.30732_figures/2605.30732_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: The Non-collapsing Test.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30732_figures/2605.30732_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: The Collapsing Test.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30732_figures/2605.30732_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Compact state-generation diagram. 𝖦𝖾𝗇𝖠𝗇𝖼𝗁𝗈𝗋𝖲𝗍𝖺𝗍𝖾and 𝖣𝗈𝗆𝖺𝗂𝗇𝖤𝗑𝗍𝖾𝗇𝗌𝗂𝗈𝗇.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper advances quantum cryptography by proposing 'quantum localization' and 'trajectory verification' to overcome security flaws in existing position verification schemes. It constructs protocols using quantum anchor states to verifiably track quantum information through space and time. This work establishes stronger theoretical foundations for position-based cryptography by localizing both quantum states and computational functions.

**Why it may be interesting.** While highly abstract, the concepts of localizing quantum information and computational capabilities in space and time provide novel theoretical tools for understanding the physical constraints on quantum information processing and security.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing quantum position verification security definitions are insufficient because they fail to prevent distributed adversarial simulation across space and time.

**Main result.** The authors introduce and construct protocols for 'quantum localization' and 'trajectory verification,' providing stronger foundations for position-based cryptography.

**Method.** The work develops protocols using 'quantum anchor states' and generalizes concepts from unclonable cryptography to track quantum information through spacetime.

**Model / system.** The framework relies on quantum information theory, specifically utilizing bipartite entangled states and the concept of tracking quantum states or computations across a specified spacetime trajectory.

**Key observables.** Fidelity of the joint state between anchor and vessel registers, and the success probability ($\eta$) of the extraction process.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The security proofs are established in the classical oracle (ideal obfuscation) model, which is heuristically instantiated using post-quantum indistinguishability obfuscation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The security proofs rely on the existence of one-way functions and the ideal obfuscation model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 illustrates a non-localizability attack on the f-BB84 protocol involving three provers, demonstrating how entanglement can be nonlocally distributed.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the weakness in existing QPV to introducing stronger notions (quantum, state, functionality localization). It then constructs protocols for trajectory verification, details the mathematical machinery using quantum anchor states, and concludes by addressing public verifiability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

While quantum position verification aims to certify a prover's location using quantum information, existing security definitions only guarantee that part of the successful adversarial party is in the claimed location. This leaves open the possibility that a distributed team of adversaries can jointly simulate a prover in a way that defeats the intended meaning of ``being at a location'' in position-based cryptography.   We introduce stronger notions of position verification that we call quantum localization, which requires that there is a specified, unclonable state at the verified spacetime point -- and that this state can be found nowhere else. We show that quantum localization leads naturally to a meaningful notion of trajectory verification, in which quantum information is verifiably tracked through space and time. We construct quantum localization and trajectory verification protocols using quantum anchor states, which generalize coset states from unclonable cryptography. The security of our schemes is proven in the classical oracle (i.e. ideal obfuscation) model, which can be heuristically instantiated in the plain model using post-quantum indistinguishability obfuscation.   We also introduce and instantiate the concept of functionality localization, which guarantees that the adversary has the ability to compute a secret function at the verified spacetime point, and this function cannot be computed anywhere else. This raises the intriguing possibility of localizing computational capabilities in space and time.   More broadly, we believe our notions of quantum localization and our feasibility results provide stronger foundations for position-based cryptography.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces the Progressive Swapping to the Middle (PSM) protocol for quantum networks to improve long-distance qubit transmission. By comparing PSM against other established protocols, the authors demonstrate that PSM offers superior resource efficiency and link probability while maintaining high fidelity, especially when quantum memories are imperfect. This advances the practical feasibility of quantum networking.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly tackles the scalability and resource limitations of quantum communication networks, which is a core area of quantum information theory relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of efficiently transferring quantum bits (qubits) over long distances in quantum networks by optimizing entanglement distribution and swapping protocols.

**Main result.** The proposed Progressive Swapping to the Middle (PSM) protocol is shown to be highly resource-efficient, yielding better link probabilities and lower resource consumption compared to existing protocols like PS and BS, especially under imperfect memory conditions.

**Method.** The authors compare PSM against several established protocols (PS, BS, HD) by calculating key performance metrics like link probability, link fidelity, and minimum required resource consumption.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is a quantum network utilizing entangled photon pairs distributed over links, requiring Bell state measurements and teleportation for end-to-end qubit transfer. The analysis incorporates decoherence from imperfect quantum memories and fiber loss.

**Key observables.** Link probability (success rate), Link Fidelity ($F$), and Resource Consumption (minimum number of generated pairs required).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Memory decay time ($	au$), time-slot ($\Delta$), and the swap success probability ($p_{swap} \approx 0.9$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes exponential decay for memory and fiber loss, and compares protocols based on metrics derived for imperfect quantum memories.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare performance metrics (fidelity, success probability, resource consumption) of PSM against other protocols (BS, HD, PS) as a function of increasing route length ($l_r$ or $L$).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the PSM protocol, derives its performance metrics, and systematically compares it against parallel (BS, HD) and sequential (PS) protocols using quantitative analysis of link fidelity, success probability, and resource cost.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The distribution of entangled pairs of photons on the links composing a quantum network, combined with Bell state measurements and teleportation, is the basic apparatus to transfer quantum bits (qubits) over long distances. Entanglement distribution establishes an end-to-end entangled pair while consuming intermediate pairs on links and holding them for a certain time period. The technical literature identifies two main kinds of protocols, parallel and sequential ones, the latter having an advantage in resource consumption over the former. In this paper, we introduce an efficient swapping protocol called Progressive Swapping to the Middle (PSM) as it combines the existing Progressive Swapping (PS) protocol from both extremities of a path that meet in the middle where the received pairs are swapped. We compare PSM with two parallel protocols and PS; in our evaluation, we take into account imperfect memories and fidelity degradation. We demonstrate that PSM yields a much better link probability than PS while keeping a reasonable link fidelity, and shows an advantage in resource consumption over other protocols.

</details>

### [Entanglement distribution protocols under imperfect fidelity and quantum memory conditions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31347v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes and analyzes the Locally Heralded Distribution (LHD) protocol for generating long-distance entanglement over quantum repeaters. By incorporating realistic noise models for quantum memory and fiber loss, the authors demonstrate that LHD significantly improves upon existing protocols like Blind Swapping, making it a more viable candidate for future quantum internet infrastructure.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit modeling of combined fiber loss and memory decoherence, alongside the comparative analysis of repeater protocols, provides crucial theoretical benchmarks for building quantum networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of establishing long-distance quantum entanglement necessary for a quantum Internet, specifically accounting for photon fragility and imperfect quantum memory.

**Main result.** The proposed Locally Heralded Distribution (LHD) protocol is shown to outperform previous methods (Blind Swapping and Globally Heralded Distribution) by achieving higher link success probabilities.

**Method.** The authors develop and compare several entanglement distribution protocols (BS, GHD, LHD) by deriving formulas for link success probability and end-to-end fidelity under realistic noise models.

**Model / system.** The model involves distributing entangled photon pairs over segmented links connected by repeater nodes, utilizing quantum memories whose fidelity and loss are explicitly modeled over time.

**Key observables.** End-to-end entanglement fidelity, link success probability, and the comparison between different protocol performance metrics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Memory lifetime ($	au$), coherence time ($	au_{coh}$), fiber attenuation ($\alpha$), and the number of links ($l_r$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis incorporates both fiber-induced and memory-induced decoherence, and assumes deterministic write-in/read-out operations for quantum memories.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the path schemes and compare link probabilities (BS, LHD, GHD) and link fidelities as a function of increasing route length ($l_r$).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem, details the physical models for decoherence and loss, develops the LHD protocol, and concludes by benchmarking LHD against established protocols using simulation results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The rapid development of quantum computers and sensors urges for the development of a quantum Internet capable of transmitting quantum bits over long distances. Photons used for quantum data transfer are fragile over time and sensitive to their environment, so that they cannot be directly used over long distances. To remedy this problem, long distance paths are segmented into shorter links and entangled pairs of photons are distributed over these links and swapped to create end-to-end entangled pairs over long distances, eventually used for teleportation. In this paper, we develop an existing protocol taking account of fidelity and imperfect memories. We shorten the execution time and thus increase its link success probability creating the so-called Locally Heralded Distribution (LHD). It turns out that the proposed protocol outperforms some previous protocols. We benchmark through simulation the performances of protocols considered in this paper by using a blind entanglement protocol as a baseline.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces the Hamming quantum kernel (HQK) to overcome the exponential concentration problem plaguing standard quantum kernels in Support Vector Machines. By utilizing the full measurement statistics weighted by Hamming weight, the HQK maintains high classification accuracy even as the number of qubits increases significantly. This demonstrates a scalable and robust method for quantum feature mapping.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the focus is on machine learning kernels, the underlying challenge—extracting robust, scalable information from quantum measurements—is central to quantum information processing and quantum characterization.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The standard fidelity quantum kernel suffers from exponential concentration when scaling to large quantum systems, limiting its practical use in Support Vector Machines (QSVMs).

**Main result.** The proposed Hamming quantum kernel (HQK) successfully mitigates this concentration issue, showing superior and stable performance compared to the standard fidelity kernel and classical kernels for systems with 15 or more qubits.

**Method.** The HQK is a classical post-processing method that utilizes the full measurement statistics of quantum circuits, weighted by the Hamming weight, rather than relying on a single fidelity value.

**Model / system.** The work uses QSVMs trained on two types of data: classical MNIST digits (reduced via PCA) and synthetic data generated from quantum circuits. The core model involves comparing the performance of three kernels: RBF (classical), FQK, and HQK.

**Key observables.** Test accuracy, kernel matrix values, and the theoretical distinguishability $D(P_0, P_1)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of qubits ($n$, ranging from 2 to 27), the HQK weighting parameter ($\lambda$), and the RBF bandwidth ($\gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The HQK assumes that similar data points lead to measurable shifts in the Hamming distributions, and the analysis relies on the ability to perform full measurement statistics.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare test accuracy vs. dimensionality ($n$) for MNIST and quantum data, consistently showing HQK outperforming FQK and RBF at large $n$. One figure illustrates the concentration issue in FQK versus the informative kernel from HQK.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of FQK concentration, details the HQK formulation using full measurement statistics, and then evaluates the method by comparing its performance against RBF and FQK across multiple classification tasks (MNIST, synthetic quantum data) as the system size increases.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum support vector machines are classification algorithms that rely on quantum-generated kernels. The fidelity quantum kernel commonly used in quantum support vector machines suffers from exponential concentration as system size increases, preventing an efficient scaling beyond fewqubit systems. We introduce the Hamming quantum kernel, a classical post-processing method that is based on the same measurement outcomes as the fidelity quantum kernel. However, it avoids the exponential concentration problem by using the full measurement statistics rather than a single fidelity value. We evaluate the approach on both classical data (MNIST) and synthetic data generated from quantum circuits, using systems ranging from 2 to 27 qubits. Throughout the simulations, the Hamming quantum kernel outperforms the fidelity quantum kernel whenever 15 or more qubits are used. Furthermore, for synthetic quantum data, our method consistently outperforms the classical Gaussian kernel. This demonstrates that the Hamming quantum kernel improves the expressivity and robustness at larger qubit scales without requiring any additional quantum ressources.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31519"></a>
### [Entanglement in quantum channel discrimination: sometimes less is more](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31519v1)

**Authors:** Kristin Sundal Lien, Marco Túlio Quintino  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31519v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31519_figures/2605.31519_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Maximal success probability of discrimination between the four Pauli channels. Success probability of dis- crimination increases with increasing entanglement.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31519_figures/2605.31519_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Maximal success probability of discrimination between Werner-Holevo channels. Success probability of discrimination increases with increasing entanglement.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31519_figures/2605.31519_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Maximal success probability of discrimination between two measurement channels. Success probability of discrimination decreases with increasing entanglement. The top horizontal (green) dot-dashed line is the maximal suc- cess probability with separable states, the bottom horizontal (orange) dashed line is the maximal success probability with separable states, and the (blue) filled curve is the optimal success probability with a fixed value of entanglement entropy.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31519_figures/2605.31519_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: The figure shows three different types of strategies for measuring the channel Φ. The simplest strategy, shown in (a), uses no ancilla. The most general strategy, shown in (b), uses a bipartite input state ρAA′ that may or may not be entangled with the auxiliary system A′. In (c), a maximally entangled input state ϕ+ is used, and we effectively measure the Choi state J(Φ)</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.31519_figures/2605.31519_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 6: The eigenvalues of the unitary W = diag(−1, 1, 1, .., 1) on the complex unit circle. We see that the origin is within the convex hull of the eigenvalues, but the centroid approaches one as the dimension d increases.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work explores the limits of entanglement in quantum channel discrimination, proving that entanglement is not always beneficial. By introducing the MEWC/MEBC framework, the authors demonstrate that for certain channel pairs, using maximally entangled states actively reduces the maximum achievable success probability compared to using separable input states.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous theoretical framework for understanding when entanglement, typically viewed as a resource, fails to provide an advantage in quantum information tasks, which is crucial for designing practical quantum protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates quantum channel discrimination, specifically identifying scenarios where the use of maximally entangled input states is suboptimal or actively detrimental to the discrimination success probability.

**Main result.** It establishes that for certain pairs of channels (MEWC pairs), the optimal input state must be separable, and excessive entanglement can significantly reduce the maximum achievable discrimination probability.

**Method.** The analysis employs concepts like the Diamond Norm ($\|\cdot\|_\diamond$) and the ME-norm ($\|\cdot\|_{ME}$), introducing the Maximal Entanglement Worst Case (MEWC) and Best Case (MEBC) classifications to systematically characterize the role of entanglement.

**Model / system.** The study models the task of distinguishing between two or more quantum channels ($\Phi_i$), using the Choi-Jamiołkowski isomorphism to translate the problem into analyzing the difference map $\Delta\Phi$. The analysis compares performance using separable vs. maximally entangled input states.

**Key observables.** Success probability of discrimination ($P^*_s$), the M-operator ($M(\Delta\Phi)$), and the relationship between the diamond norm and the ME-norm.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The entanglement entropy of the input state, the dimension of the Hilbert space ($d$), and the structure of the difference map $\Delta\Phi$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often focuses on the uniform prior probability case and compares the optimal strategy (using the diamond norm) against the maximally entangled strategy (using the ME-norm).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the success probability for discriminating Pauli channels, showing an increase with entanglement, but also show cases where the probability decreases with increasing entanglement, favoring separable states.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops from general channel discrimination theory, introduces the MEWC/MEBC concepts, derives mathematical bounds relating different norms, and applies these concepts to specific examples like Pauli and measurement channels to prove when entanglement is detrimental.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement is known to be a powerful resource that improves performance in various quantum information and computational tasks. A standard example of such a phenomenon is the possibility of perfectly discriminating all four Pauli operations in a single shot via the superdense coding protocol. While entanglement is often a powerful resource for quantum channel discrimination, this is not necessarily the case. In this work, we identify scenarios in which the maximally entangled state is a bad choice of input state and, more generally, show that excessive entanglement can reduce channel discriminability dramatically. To do so, we present an explicit pair of unitary channels which are perfectly discriminable without entanglement, but for which any strategy with maximally entangled input states is $ε$-close to a blind uniform guessing strategy. To develop a systematic approach, we introduce the concepts of Maximal Entanglement Worst Case (MEWC) and Maximal Entanglement Best Case (MEBC) pairs of channels, and present conditions for a pair of channels to be MEWC or MEBC. With these conditions, we show that the optimal input states for discriminating MEWC pairs of channels are necessarily separable, and provide non-trivial examples of measurement channels for which entanglement necessarily reduces the maximum probability of discrimination.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.30978"></a>
### [Parity-induced generalized Brillouin zone without non-Hermitian skin effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30978v1)

**Authors:** Alexander Felski  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30978v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<sub>Fig. 3. Clearly, this behavior is no longer captured by the conventional band spectrum (10) found under peri- odic boundaries. It marks an acute parity-induced sen- sitivity to the boundary conditions, reminiscent of the non-Hermitian skin effect. Moreover, and curiously in light of the absence of a closed-loop spectral structure under periodic boundaries, the analysis within the generalized Brillouin zone formal- ism identifies a consistent standing-wave construction for the description of bulk states at kj ∈C with Rekj ∈(0,π) satisfying</sub>
