**Summary.** This work investigates spectral features in non-Hermitian quantum systems, specifically challenging the notion that complex quasimomenta and generalized Brillouin zones always imply the non-Hermitian skin effect. By analyzing the SSH* model, the authors prove that these complex features can instead originate from parity-induced even-odd effects inherent to finite chain lengths, even when the system remains fundamentally delocalized.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a crucial theoretical distinction between two superficially similar phenomena—spectral sensitivity due to finite-size parity effects versus the true non-Hermitian skin effect—which is vital for interpreting experimental measurements in open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To demonstrate that spectral sensitivity and the formation of a generalized Brillouin zone (GBZ) can occur in non-Hermitian systems due to parity-induced even-odd effects, even when the conventional non-Hermitian skin effect (NHSE) is absent.

**Main result.** The study shows that the complex quasimomentum structure and GBZ deviation can arise purely from finite-size parity effects in the SSH* model, independent of the NHSE, while wavefunctions remain delocalized.

**Method.** The analysis employs the generalized Brillouin zone formalism, analyzing the Hamiltonian's spectrum for finite chains of odd and even lengths using symmetry classification (Altland-Zirnbauer classes).

**Model / system.** The primary model is the SSH* tight-binding Hamiltonian, which is a non-Hermitian generalization of the Su-Schrieffer-Heeger model, studied on finite chains of length N.

**Key observables.** Eigenvalue spectra, generalized Brillouin zone structure, Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR), and the comparison between open vs. periodic boundary conditions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chain length N (odd vs. even), the coupling strengths t and delta, and the thermodynamic limit (N -> infinity).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on constructing specific compound states to satisfy eigenvalue relations and distinguishing between parity-induced effects and the true NHSE.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate eigenvalue spectra comparing periodic and open boundary conditions for both the Hatano-Nelson model (showing NHSE) and the SSH* model (showing parity-induced GBZ deviations).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically contrasts systems exhibiting NHSE with the SSH* model, deriving the GBZ for finite chains, and finally separating the observed spectral sensitivity into contributions from parity effects versus the NHSE.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Acute spectral sensitivity to boundary conditions and the formation of a generalized Brillouin zone associated with complex quasimomenta are features frequently attributed to systems with non-trivial non-Hermitian topology, showcasing the non-Hermitian skin effect. We show that, away from the thermodynamic limit, these features themselves are not uniquely tied to this phenomenon; they can similarly arise as parity-induced even-odd effects in non-Hermitian systems without skin effect. Despite an underlying generalized Brillouin zone description, wavefunctions remain delocalized. In addition, the effect can arise in skin-effect models as entirely separate distinguishable feature

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30588"></a>
### [Pure State Transformations under Block Coherence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30588v1)

**Authors:** Dipayan Chakraborty, Priyabrata Char, Indrani Chattopadhyay, Debasis Sarkar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30588v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30588_figures/2605.30588_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Schematic diagram of the block incoherent unitary U1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30588_figures/2605.30588_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Schematic diagram of the block incoherent unitary U2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30588_figures/2605.30588_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Achievable target state region for a fixed input prob- ability vector ˜p</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30588_figures/2605.30588_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Input state region for a fixed target probability vector ˜q</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30588_figures/2605.30588_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4 shows the achievable target state region from the fixed input probability vector</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a resource theory framework for 'block coherence' by analyzing pure state transformations under three generalized incoherent operations: PBIO, SBIO, and BDCO. It proves that for SBIO and BDCO, the ability to transform one pure state to another is mathematically equivalent to a majorization condition on their respective block probability vectors. This advances the understanding of how coherence resources constrain quantum operations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous mathematical framework for quantifying and characterizing quantum resources (block coherence) by establishing precise, necessary, and sufficient conditions for state transformations under generalized incoherent dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the necessary and sufficient conditions for deterministically transforming one pure quantum state to another using specific types of incoherent quantum operations.

**Main result.** For Strictly Block Incoherent Operations (SBIO) and Block Dephasing Covariant Operations (BDCO), pure state conversion is completely characterized by the majorization relation between the input and output block probability vectors.

**Method.** The authors employ resource theory concepts, utilizing majorization relations and constructing explicit Kraus representations to prove the equivalence between the physical process and the mathematical ordering condition.

**Model / system.** The system is a quantum state $|\psiangle$ defined on a Hilbert space $H$ with a fixed orthogonal decomposition $H = \sum_{\alpha} H_\alpha$. The operations studied are PBIO, SBIO, and BDCO, which generalize standard coherence models.

**Key observables.** Block probability vectors ($	ilde{p}$ and $	ilde{q}$), and the resulting state transformation $|\psiangle 	o |\phiangle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The nondegeneracy condition for PBIO, and the block structure defined by the orthogonal decomposition.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a fixed orthogonal decomposition of the Hilbert space and relies on establishing necessary and sufficient conditions for the transformation.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the structure of block incoherent unitaries and compare the achievable state transformation regions under DIO versus BDCO.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the conversion criteria for PBIO, then establishes the majorization criterion for BDCO and SBIO, proving equivalence and constructing the required Kraus operators for the converse direction.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Block coherence provides a natural generalization of standard quantum coherence by treating superpositions across different subspaces as a resource. This work studies deterministic pure-state conversion under three free operations: physically block incoherent operations (PBIO), strictly block incoherent operations (SBIO), and block dephasing covariant incoherent operations (BDCO). For PBIO, we prove that, under a natural nondegeneracy condition on the active Kraus branches, any deterministic conversion from one pure state to another must be implemented by a block incoherent unitary. When the nondegeneracy requirement is removed, the condition becomes more general. It demands that the blockwise action of every active branch reproduce the target block structure with a common proportionality factor across all output blocks. For SBIO and BDCO, we show that deterministic pure-state transformation is completely characterized by the majorization relation between the input and output block probability vectors. The converse proof is constructive, yielding an explicit Kraus representation for every admissible BDCO transformation. In the rank-one limit, these conditions reduce to the known pure-state transformation criteria for physically incoherent operations (PIO), strictly incoherent operations (SIO), and dephasing covariant incoherent operations (DIO) in the standard resource theory of coherence. Using the majorization condition, a maximally block-coherent state with uniform block weights is also identified as a universal pure-state resource under BDCO and SBIO. We have also provided geometric numerical illustrations comparing the state transformation power of BDCO and DIO for a fixed input state, fixed output state and mutual convertibility scenarios.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31203"></a>
### [Rigorous extension of semilocal collinear functionals to noncollinear DFT using $SU(2)$ rotations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31203v1)

**Summary.** This work develops a rigorous, first-principles method to generalize standard collinear exchange-correlation functionals to the noncollinear regime required for accurate DFT in magnetically frustrated systems. By employing $SU(2)$ rotations in a $u(2)$ matrix framework, the authors provide a mathematically consistent way to calculate magnetic properties like local torques and hyperfine couplings. This advances the theoretical tools available for studying strongly correlated magnetic materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on solid-state magnetism, the rigorous mathematical framework for handling non-collinearity via group theory ($SU(2)$) and gradient expansions is highly relevant to any theoretical work involving complex, symmetry-broken quantum states or spin dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the lack of a rigorous, first-principles connection between established semilocal collinear exchange-correlation (XC) functionals and their necessary noncollinear generalizations required for accurate DFT calculations in magnetically complex materials.

**Main result.** A locally exact relationship between collinear and noncollinear XC functionals is derived using a $u(2)$ matrix representation and implemented via numerically robust $SU(2)$ rotations.

**Method.** The authors derive the relationship by expanding the energy functional using a gradient expansion formalism in the $u(2)$ space, transforming collinear derivatives to noncollinear space via unitary rotations.

**Model / system.** The theory is applied to noncollinear magnetism, which is crucial when spin-orbit coupling or geometric frustration is present. Demonstrations include calculating local magnetic torques in the $	ext{Cr}_3$ cluster and hyperfine couplings in Uranium.

**Key observables.** Local magnetic torque structure, hyperfine coupling constants ($A$), and magnetic moments ($\langle \hat{S}^2 angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The degree of noncollinearity, the ratio of transverse to longitudinal magnetization gradients ($\alpha_{\perp}/\alpha_{\parallel}$), and the small deviation parameter $\epsilon$ for regularization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary approximation is that the exact noncollinear functional can be represented in a locally collinear parameterization, which is only strictly true if certain coefficient tensors commute with gradient variables.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the local magnetic torque structure for $	ext{Cr}_3$ using various noncollinear approximations (NC-SU(2), SF, etc.) and different XC functionals (BLYP, OLYP, KT3). Table II compares calculated hyperfine coupling constants for U and $	ext{U}^+$ across multiple methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the mathematical framework (using $u(2)$ representation and $SU(2)$ rotations) to deriving the energy-invariant relationship, applying this formalism to calculate local magnetic torques in $	ext{Cr}_3$, and finally extending the methodology to calculate relativistic properties like hyperfine couplings in Uranium.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the presence of spin-orbit coupling and in geometrically frustrated materials, a noncollinear treatment the magnetization density is essential. However, in density functional theory most exchange--correlation functional approximations were originally developed for locally collinear magnetization. Many practical approaches to noncollinear DFT have emerged over the past decade. However, a first-principles connection between widely used semilocal collinear functionals and their noncollinear generalizations remains lacking. In this work, a locally exact relation between collinear and noncollinear exchange--correlation functionals is derived at the level of gradient expansions within a $u(2)$ matrix representation of the energy functional. Within this framework, collinear semilocal variables naturally acquire distinct dependencies on transverse and longitudinal magnetization gradient components. The widely used Scalmani--Frisch scheme emerges as a first-order approximation. The transformation of collinear functional derivatives to noncollinear space is implemented through numerically robust $SU(2)$ rotations. A consistent description of local magnetic torques is demonstrated for the prototypical spin-frustrated Cr$_3$ cluster. The approach further extends to fully nonlocal functionals and provides a direct route towards numerically stable relativistic response calculations. The influence on magnetic properties in presence of spin-orbit coupling is illustrated through calculations of hyperfine couplings in the high-spin ground states of uranium and the uranium ion.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31207"></a>
### [Cover time statistics of one-dimensional Brownian motion under stochastic resetting](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31207v1)

**Authors:** Anirban Ghosh, Sanjib Sabhapandit  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31207v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31207_figures/2605.31207_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Brownian motion (BM) with reflecting boundary con- dition at x = 0 and x = L, which are shown in blue. The black region indicates the space already covered by the walker up to time t, starting at x0 = L/2. The Orange region is yet to be covered. The Brownian particle is resetting at L/2 with a rate r. The cover time tc is the first time at which the entire domain becomes black.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.31207_figures/2605.31207_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 shows an excellent agreement between the analyt- ical prediction and numerical simulations.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes how stochastic resetting modifies the time required for a Brownian particle to explore an entire confined space (the cover time). The authors find that there is an optimal resetting rate that minimizes this search time, providing a quantitative tool for optimizing exploration processes in confined physical systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of optimizing search times via external driving (resetting) is highly relevant to open quantum systems, particularly in quantum computation or quantum sensing where initialization or measurement resets are common.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how stochastic resetting affects the statistics of the cover time for a one-dimensional Brownian motion confined to a finite interval.

**Main result.** The mean cover time exhibits a non-monotonic dependence on the scaled reset rate, achieving a minimum at an optimal rate $\rho^*$, demonstrating that resetting can efficiently optimize search processes.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving exact analytical expressions for the cover time distribution and its moments using Laplace transforms and residue calculus.

**Model / system.** The system is a single Brownian particle undergoing diffusion on the line segment $[0, L]$ with reflecting boundaries. The dynamics are modified by introducing stochastic resetting to the center of the interval at a constant rate $r$.

**Key observables.** Cover time ($	ext{t}_c$), mean cover time $\langle 	ext{t}_c angle$, and the probability density function (PDF) of $	ext{t}_c$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Diffusion constant $D$, interval length $L$, and the scaled reset rate $\rho = rL^2/4D$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The resetting process is modeled as Poissonian, and the mathematical derivations rely heavily on Laplace transforms and residue calculus for solving the associated renewal equations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows the scaled mean cover time decreasing, reaching a minimum at $\rho^*$, and then increasing with the scaled resetting rate $\rho$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the non-resetting case, then introduces the stochastic resetting mechanism, and finally derives the full cover time distribution and its asymptotic limits using advanced mathematical techniques.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the effect of stochastic resetting on the statistics of the cover time of a one-dimensional Brownian motion with a diffusion constant $D$, confined to a finite interval of length $L$. The cover time $t_c$, defined as the minimum time required for the particle to visit every point of the interval at least once, exhibits a non-monotonic dependence on the scaled reset rate $ρ= rL^2/4D$. The scaled mean cover time $4D\langle t_c\rangle/L^2$ initially decreases with increasing $ρ$, attains a minimum at an optimal value $ρ^*$, and then increases with $ρ$, indicating an optimal resetting rate that minimizes the search duration. Furthermore, we derive an exact analytical expression for the cover time distribution, including its asymptotic limits, which agree well with numerical simulations. These results demonstrate that stochastic resetting serves as an efficient mechanism for optimizing cover-time processes in confined geometries.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper addresses the failure of the standard tight-binding model for coupled optical waveguides when mode overlap is significant. The authors propose using Löwdin orthogonalization to generate an orthonormal basis, which corrects the Hamiltonian and restores the model's accuracy. This improved Löwdin-TB method accurately predicts complex coupling effects, such as negative next-nearest-neighbor hopping phases, previously missed by standard approximations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous mathematical tool (Löwdin orthogonalization) to correct a fundamental approximation in coupled-mode theory, which is essential for accurately modeling light-matter or light-waveguide interactions in complex photonic structures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The standard tight-binding approximation for light propagation in waveguide arrays fails when waveguides are closely spaced because the underlying assumption of mode orthogonality breaks down.

**Main result.** The proposed Löwdin-TB method successfully restores the standard eigenvalue problem and accurately reproduces exact beam propagation simulations, capturing crucial physical effects like negative next-nearest-neighbor coupling.

**Method.** The authors introduce a modified tight-binding framework using Löwdin orthogonalization to construct an orthonormal basis from the non-orthogonal guided modes.

**Model / system.** The system is a linear array of coupled single-mode optical waveguides, whose dynamics are governed by the paraxial wave equation. The model is analyzed using a tight-binding Hamiltonian approach.

**Key observables.** Fidelity (comparison to BPM), Spectral Distance (S), Completeness Distance (C), and Frobenius Distance (F) are used to quantify the accuracy of the approximation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Waveguide separation distance ($d$), number of waveguides ($M$), and the overlap matrix elements ($\langle\psi_n|\psi_mangle$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The standard TB method assumes guided modes are mutually orthogonal, which is the primary flawed assumption addressed by the work.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the breakdown of standard TB fidelity versus BPM for increasing waveguide count, and plot the modified Hamiltonian elements ($H_{n,m}$) showing the emergence of negative NNN coupling phases in the Löwdin approach.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the failure of standard TB due to non-orthogonality, then introduces the Löwdin orthogonalization procedure, and finally validates the new method by comparing its results against exact beam propagation simulations using multiple quantitative error metrics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many advancements in optics have relied on the tight-binding approximation, which simplifies the description and prediction of complex system behaviors. This approximation describes the dynamics of the total light field by examining the coupling between the guided modes of individual single-mode substructures -- also known as coupled mode theory. However, the underlying assumption, that the guided modes of individual waveguides form an orthogonal basis, breaks down when waveguides are brought into close proximity or when larger arrays are considered. In this work, we systematically analyze the consequences of this non-orthogonality and show that it leads to a generalized eigenvalue problem involving an overlap matrix, causing a fundamental mismatch between the standard TB model and solutions of the paraxial wave equation. To resolve this issue, we introduce a modified TB framework based on the Löwdin orthogonalization, which constructs an orthonormal basis from the non-orthogonal guided modes while minimally altering their physical shape and preserving their symmetry properties. The resulting Löwdin-TB method restores the standard eigenvalue problem and yields excellent agreement with exact beam propagation simulations across a wide range of system sizes and waveguide separations. Furthermore, it captures important physical effects, such as enhanced long-range coupling and nontrivial hopping phases, that are absent in the standard approach.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31602"></a>
### [Twin Algebras: Condensable Algebras beyond Anyons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31602v1)

**Authors:** Yuhan Gai, Sakura Schafer-Nameki, Alison Warman  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31602v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31602_figures/2605.31602_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Partial Hasse diagram for Z(Vecω G32). The algebras are detailed in Table IX. The top most algebra is the identity algebra. The bottom row are the Lagrangian algebras, that correspond to topological boundary conditions. Twin alge- bras are (Aω 4 ,Aω 5 ), (Aω 6 ,Aω 11), (Aω 8 ,Aω 9 ), (Aω 7 ,Aω 10), (Aω 15,Aω 16).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31602_figures/2605.31602_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Part of the Hasse diagram for Z(Vec(Z2×Z2)⋉Z8) that connects to the twin algebras A4 = A(H1, 1, 1, 1) and A5 = A(H2, 1, 1, 1). The algebra A14, A21, A24 are condensable algebras that contain both twins. The algebras are detailed in table VIII. The top most algebra is the identity algebra. The bottom row are the Lagrangian algebras, that correspond to topological boundary conditions.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work classifies 'twin condensable algebras' in 2+1D topological orders, which are generalizations of standard anyon models. By introducing the concept of twin algebras—those with the same anyon content but different algebraic structures—the authors identify new candidates for quantum phase transitions. This provides a powerful mathematical tool for distinguishing between physically distinct symmetric phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of distinct phases with isomorphic ground state spaces but inequivalent order parameters is highly relevant for understanding exotic quantum phases in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to classify and characterize 'twin condensable algebras' in 2+1 dimensional non-chiral topological orders, which go beyond simple anyon descriptions.

**Main result.** Twin algebras exist, representing distinct symmetric phases that share the same anyon content but possess inequivalent algebra structures, leading to phase transitions beyond Landau theory.

**Method.** The classification uses the framework of Symmetry Topological Field Theory (SymTFT) applied to group-theoretical topological orders, parameterizing algebras by subgroups and cocycles.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on symmetric gapped phases in 2+1 dimensions, analyzed using the structure of the Drinfeld center Z(Vec_omega^G) for finite group symmetries G.

**Key observables.** The primary observables are the condensable algebras themselves, characterized by their algebra structure, and the resulting order parameters $\mathcal{O}_a$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The classification depends on parameters $(H, N, \gamma, \epsilon)$, and the existence of Gassmann triples $(G, H_1, H_2)$ is crucial for identifying twins.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to group-theoretical symmetries $	ext{Z}(	ext{Vec}^{\omega}G)$, and the identification of twins relies on algebraic structures like Gassmann triples.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the SymTFT for S-symmetric phases, showing the relationship between symmetry and physical boundaries, and the structure of the algebras.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically defines condensable algebras, introduces the concept of twin algebras, develops classification criteria using algebraic invariants (like Gassmann triples), and demonstrates physical consequences regarding phase transitions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Condensable algebras in 2+1d non-chiral topological orders characterize gapped boundary conditions and interfaces. Applied to the Symmetry Topological Field Theory, they allow classification of symmetric gapped phases and impose sharp constraints on possible phase transitions. A condensable algebra is specified not only by its underlying set of anyons, which end on the boundary or interface, but also by its algebra structure. We introduce the concept of twin condensable algebras, which have the same anyon decomposition, but inequivalent algebra structure. We revisit the classification of condensable algebras in $\mathcal{Z}(\text{Vec}_G^ω)$, i.e. in group-theoretical topological orders for finite groups $G$ with anomaly $ω$. In this context we are able to identify twin algebras that arise from different mechanisms, such as subgroup data, SPT cocycles, and symmetry actions. In particular, we construct infinite families of examples of twins from so-called Gassmann triples, and exhibit cases in which the reduced topological orders are inequivalent despite having identical anyon content. Physically, twin algebras describe distinct symmetric phases that have isomorphic spaces of ground states, but inequivalent order parameters. Such twin phases never exhibit relative spontaneous symmetry breaking, and can be used to construct phase transitions without hidden symmetry breaking, which are intrinsically beyond Landau transitions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31118"></a>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>FIG. 5. The heat capacity Cp divided by temperature T vs T in various ﬁelds applied parallel b-axis. Open (closed) sym- bols is in cooling down (warming up) cycle. The upper inset enlarges the region around the double transition in zero ﬁeld and from a second sample (#2). The lower inset presents the ﬁt to the data using Eq. 1.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work investigates the complex magnetic behavior of CeRhSn2, a material with a geometrically frustrated structure. By combining detailed measurements of magnetization, specific heat, and resistivity with DFT calculations, the authors resolve conflicting reports to establish a clear sequence of magnetic transitions. They conclude that the material exhibits a ferromagnetic ground state, while attributing the large electronic specific heat coefficient to weak Kondo hybridization.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a detailed, multi-faceted investigation into magnetic ordering in a heavy fermion material, directly addressing the interplay between crystal structure, magnetic frustration, and electronic correlations, which is central to strongly correlated electron physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to resolve conflicting reports regarding the ground state of the intermetallic compound CeRhSn2, which is complicated by a sawtooth-like arrangement of Ce sites suggesting magnetic frustration.

**Main result.** The study establishes three successive magnetic transitions (AFM at 3.65 K, FM at 1.7 K, and a final transition at 1.5 K) and concludes that the system possesses a ferromagnetic ground state, despite initial signs of frustration.

**Method.** The research combines extensive experimental measurements (magnetization, specific heat, resistivity) on high-quality single crystals with ab initio Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the intermetallic compound CeRhSn2, which has an orthorhombic structure and exhibits complex magnetic anisotropy due to its crystal structure and electronic interactions.

**Key observables.** Magnetization (M), specific heat divided by temperature (Cp/T), resistivity (rho), and magnetic transition temperatures (TN, TC).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sommerfeld coefficient (gamma = 76.5 mJ/mol·K^2), magnetic anisotropy (b-axis easy axis), and critical temperatures (3.65 K, 1.7 K, 1.5 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The authors assume that the observed magnetic transitions and thermodynamic anomalies provide definitive evidence for the magnetic ground state, while acknowledging that the enhancement of gamma might be due to Kondo hybridization rather than frustration.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the crystal structure showing two inequivalent Ce sites and the sawtooth pattern; magnetization curves show field-dependent transitions; and Cp/T vs. T plots reveal anomalies corresponding to the magnetic ordering temperatures.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of literature review/problem statement, detailed experimental characterization across multiple physical properties (M, Cp, rho), analysis of field and temperature dependencies to pinpoint multiple transitions, and finally, theoretical calculations to confirm the magnetic ground state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Conflicting reports exist on the ground state of the intermetallic compound CeRhSn$_2$. This can be rooted in the sawtooth-like arrangement of two inequivalent Ce sites in the unit cell, which suggests potential geometric magnetic frustration. To resolve, we conducted a comprehensive study on high-quality single crystals of CeRhSn$_2$ by means of magnetization ($M$), specific heat ($C_p/T$), and resistivity ($ρ$). The system exhibits strong magnetic anisotropy, confirming the $b$-axis as the easy magnetic axis. We establish three successive transitions, an AFM order at $T_{N} = 3.65$K, a first-order FM order at $T_{C} = 1.7$K and final transition, at $T = 1.5$K. The transition temperatures are highly field-directional dependent: in a magnetic field, the lowest transition is immediately suppressed while $\mathbf{H} \parallel b$ rapidly merges $T_{C}$ and $T_{N}$ into a single second-order transition. Conversely, $\mathbf{H}\parallel c$ suppresses the FM order and reduces $T_{N}$. Additional ab initio calculations affirm the FM ground state of CeRhSn$_2$. The observation of an enhancement of the Sommerfeld coefficient ($γ= 76.5$mJ/mol$\cdot$K$^2$) may arise from geometric frustration, but it is most consistently attributed to weak Kondo hybridization as frustration cannot be conclusively established through our data.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Twin Phases: Phase Transitions Without Hidden Symmetry Breaking](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31601v1)

**Authors:** Alison Warman, Yuhan Gai, Sakura Schafer-Nameki  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31601v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31601_figures/2605.31601_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Three twin gapped phases: the physical boundaries are the twin Lagrangian algebras LS(1) 3 and LS(2) 3 . Changing</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31601_figures/2605.31601_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Hasse diagram for Dω(G). The algebras are ordered by increasing quantum dimension from top to bottom, and are detailed in Tab. II. The top most algebra is the identity algebra. The bottom row are the Lagrangian algebras, that correspond to topological boundary conditions. The twin algebras are Lagrangians twins A20 and A21 and non-maximal twins A15, A16 and A9, A10. The details of the algebras are in table II.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces the concept of 'twin phases' to challenge the assumption that all 1+1 dimensional phase transitions must involve spontaneous symmetry breaking. Using advanced tools from Topological Field Theory, the authors construct a model based on the $	ext{GL}(2, 3)$ symmetry group, demonstrating a stable, second-order phase transition between two distinct phases without any hidden symmetry breaking.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of twin phases offers a new mechanism for understanding continuous phase transitions in quantum matter that bypasses the standard SSB paradigm, which is relevant for understanding exotic quantum critical points in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To provide counterexamples to the conjecture that all 1+1d phase transitions are Spontaneous Symmetry Breaking (SSB) transitions, even after gauging.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate the existence of 'twin phases' and stable, second-order phase transitions between them that are intrinsically beyond Landau transitions and do not involve hidden SSB.

**Method.** The analysis utilizes the Symmetry Topological Field Theory (SymTFT) framework to classify gapped phases and constructs interpolating Hamiltonians between distinct topological orders.

**Model / system.** The study uses a theoretical framework based on a finite group symmetry $G = 	ext{GL}(2, 3)$ in 1+1 dimensions, modeling the system via lattice Hamiltonians $H(\lambda)$ that interpolate between two distinct gapped phases.

**Key observables.** Order parameters (OPs) belonging to the same generalized charge, and the critical point $\lambda = 1/2$ in the interpolating Hamiltonian.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The mixed anomaly $\omega \in H^3(G, U(1))$ is crucial for stabilizing the transition; the transition occurs at $\lambda = 1/2$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the SymTFT description for gapped phases and relies on the structure of condensable algebras.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the SymTFT for twin gapped phases; Figure 2 shows the three twin gapped phases; Figure 3 is a Hasse diagram of condensable algebras, highlighting the twin Lagrangian algebras $A20$ and $A21$.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework using SymTFT, analyzes the symmetry structure of $G=	ext{GL}(2,3)$ via its character table and anomalies, constructs the twin phases using specific Lagrangian algebras, and finally models the transition using an interpolating Hamiltonian $H(\lambda)$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce the concept of twin phases for a symmetry $\mathcal{S}$, defined as inequivalent phases, whose order parameters are part of the same generalized charge under $\mathcal{S}$. Stable, direct transitions between such twin phases are never spontaneous-symmetry-breaking transitions, even after (partially) gauging the initial symmetry $\mathcal{S}$: they are phase transitions without hidden symmetry breaking. We illustrate this with an (anomalous) finite group symmetry in 1+1d, which exhibits such intrinsically beyond Landau transitions.

</details>

### [Using graph neural networks to predict many-body interactions in amorphous materials](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30822v1)

**Summary.** This work introduces NequIP, an equivariant GNN, to overcome the limitations of pairwise potentials in modeling amorphous materials like PGNs. By training on high-energy configurations sampled via DFT, the network accurately predicts the system's complex potential energy landscape. This allows for the simulation of equilibrium structures, revealing local icosahedral ordering, which is crucial for understanding glassy dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The ability to use ML potentials trained on high-energy, non-equilibrium data to predict low-energy, equilibrium states is a powerful paradigm for simulating complex, glassy systems where traditional sampling methods fail.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Accurately modeling the complex, high-dimensional, and rugged potential energy landscape (PEL) associated with many-body interactions in amorphous materials, which are often restricted to computationally cheaper pairwise-additive frameworks.

**Main result.** The proposed GNN, NequIP, successfully learns the complex PEL by training on out-of-equilibrium data and accurately predicts the system's equilibrium structure, matching experimental observations.

**Method.** The authors employ an equivariant message-passing Graph Neural Network (GNN) trained on energy data derived from classical Density Functional Theory (DFT) simulations of out-of-equilibrium configurations.

**Model / system.** The system studied is a model soft glass composed of solvent-free polymer-grafted nanoparticles (PGNs). The interactions are governed by the polymer free energy, leading to strong, angular-dependent many-body forces.

**Key observables.** Potential energy landscape (PEL), equilibrium structure (e.g., icosahedral-like local order), pair distribution function, and static structure factor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Grafting density ($\sigma_g$), core volume fraction ($\phi_c$), and the GNN hyperparameters ($l_{	ext{max}}=3, n_l=3$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core relaxation is assumed to be much slower than individual polymer relaxation, allowing for a Born-Oppenheimer-like approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the computational workflow (out-of-equilibrium sampling $ightarrow$ GNN training $ightarrow$ equilibrium prediction). They compare structural metrics (like bond order parameters and Voronoi volumes) and energy predictions between the GNN, DFT, and experimental results.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the physical model (PGNs and polymer-mediated interactions), describes the computational framework (DFT sampling of high-energy states), introduces the NequIP GNN architecture, and validates its predictive power by running GNN-driven Monte Carlo simulations to reach equilibrium.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many-body interactions govern the complex behavior of many amorphous materials, from metallic glasses to biological tissues, yet are often replaced by pairwise additive frameworks for computational efficiency. Here, we use classical density functional theory (DFT) to study a model soft glass of solvent-free polymer-grafted nanoparticles (PGNs), where the absence of solvent forces grafted chains to uniformly fill the interstitial space, generating strong angular-dependent many-body interactions between the cores. We show that NequIP, an equivariant message-passing graph neural network (GNN), learns the high-dimensional, rugged potential energy landscape of the system and reproduces classical DFT energies across a range of PGN design parameters at four orders of magnitude lower cost. Systematic analysis of GNN hyperparameters offers physical insights into the range, anisotropy, and effective body order of interactions. GNN-driven Monte Carlo simulations reveal locally favored icosahedral-like structures at equilibrium, and strikingly, recover equilibrium structures in agreement with experiments, despite the network being trained only on high-energy, out-of-equilibrium configurations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31209"></a>
### [$q$-Exponential Random Graphs: higher-order networks from simple constraints](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31209v1)

**Authors:** David Dobáš, Diego Garlaschelli, Petr Jizba  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31209v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This work develops $q$-Exponential Random Graph Models by replacing the standard Shannon entropy maximization with generalized $q$-entropies. This generalization allows the model to capture higher-order network correlations, such as tunable clustering and phase transitions, using only simple constraints. It shows that the non-Shannon parameter $q$ acts as a control knob for these complex structural features.

**Why it may be interesting.** The framework provides a powerful, information-theoretic tool to model complex network structures that exhibit non-trivial correlations (like high clustering at low density) without needing to explicitly add higher-order constraints to the model Hamiltonian.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper generalizes the Maximum Entropy Principle (MEP) for network ensemble construction from the standard Shannon entropy to a broader class of $q$-entropies (Uffink entropies). It seeks to model complex networks in a more agnostic way than the standard Boltzmann-Gibbs framework.

**Main result.** The resulting $q$-Exponential Random Graphs ($q$-ERGs) and $q$-Exponential Configuration Models ($q$-CMs) exhibit higher-order structural dependencies, such as tunable clustering and phase transitions, even when only simple constraints (like total link count or degree sequence) are imposed.

**Method.** The methodology involves maximizing generalized entropy functionals (like Tsallis entropy) subject to expected structural constraints, leading to $q$-exponential probability distributions. This is analyzed through mathematical derivations, saddle-point approximations, and MCMC simulations.

**Model / system.** The model is the random graph ensemble, generalizing the Erdős–Rényi and Configuration Models. The core mathematical structure is the $q$-exponential distribution, $P_q(G) \propto \exp_{2-q}(-\sum 	ilde{	heta}_i c_i(G))$, where $q$ controls the deviation from the standard Shannon case.

**Key observables.** Link density, degree-resolved clustering coefficient $\langle C angle(k)$, average nearest-neighbor degree $\langle k_{nn} angle(k)$, and the order parameter $S$ (fraction of nodes in the largest connected component).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The entropic parameter $q$ (or $r = 1-q$), which controls the degree of non-extensivity and the strength of higher-order correlations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that maximizing generalized entropies provides a more agnostic description of physical systems than maximizing Shannon entropy. The analysis relies on consistent generalizations of the Maximum Entropy Principle.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how the non-Shannon parameter $r$ independently controls higher-order properties like clustering ($\langle C angle$) and nearest-neighbor degree ($\langle k_{nn} angle$) in the $q$-CM, while the $q$-ER model shows enhanced critical fluctuations near the percolation threshold.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the mathematical necessity of generalized entropies, then applying this framework to generalize the ER and CM models, deriving the $q$-exponential forms, and finally analyzing the resulting structural properties, phase transitions, and the independent control offered by the parameter $r$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exponential Random Graphs (ERGs) are among the most widely used network models, derived as principled least-bias graph ensembles that maximize Shannon entropy under constraints on the expected values of given structural properties. However, it has been recently (re)discovered that, in the absence of additional information privileging Shannon entropy, the most agnostic inferential construction should maximize the broader class of Uffink entropies. The resulting entropy-maximizing distribution changes from the exponential (Boltzmann-Gibbs) to the so-called q-exponential one. Since maximizing Shannon entropy may produce an unjustified independence between degrees of freedom, here we investigate how the most popular ERGs with independent edges (namely, the Erdos-Renyi and configuration models) generalize to higher-order q-Exponential Random Graphs with dependent edges in the non-Shannon case, while keeping their defining constraints (number of links and degree sequence, respectively) unchanged. We find features, such as a phase transition between sparse and dense regimes, that are absent in the original ERGs but typical of higher-order networks, plus novel phenomena such as richer assortativity and clustering profiles, which allow for the coexistence of link sparsity and triadic closure. These results show that higher-order networks do not necessarily require higher-order constraints, as they naturally arise from simpler ones in a framework that is even more agnostic than Shannon's.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30872"></a>
### [A Phase Space Signature of Quantum Roaming in Chesnavich's Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30872v1)

**Authors:** Stephen Wiggins  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30872v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30872_figures/2605.30872_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Classical reference structures and radial diagnostic boundaries used to define the projected</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30872_figures/2605.30872_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Primary quantum-roaming resonance. Left: configuration-space density |ψ(r, θ)|2 with</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30872_figures/2605.30872_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Quantum signatures of the primary resonance. Left: angular-channel weights, showing</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30872_figures/2605.30872_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Selection of the primary quantum-roaming resonance from the computed CAP resonance</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30872_figures/2605.30872_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5 compares radial marginal densities for the primary, secondary, and representative</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates quantum roaming in the $\mathrm{CH}_4^+$ ion-molecule reaction using a two-dimensional model. By computing resonance states with a Complex Absorbing Potential, the authors analyze the wavefunctions using phase-space diagnostics. They successfully identify a primary resonance whose localization in phase space—characterized by specific radial and angular distributions—serves as a controlled quantum analogue of the classically observed roaming mechanism.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges classical reaction dynamics (roaming) with quantum mechanics by providing a rigorous, diagnostic-based identification of a quantum resonance state that mirrors a complex classical trajectory feature. This methodology is highly relevant for understanding non-adiabatic quantum dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental quantum question of whether a single quantum resonance state can carry a recognizable signature of the classically defined 'roaming' region in chemical reactions.

**Main result.** The authors identify a specific resonance state whose wavefunction and phase-space diagnostics are localized in the region between the inner and outer transition-state structures, providing a controlled quantum analogue of classical roaming.

**Method.** Quantum resonance states are computed using a Complex Absorbing Potential (CAP) method, and their wavefunctions are analyzed using phase-space diagnostics like radial Husimi projections and angular-momentum channel weights.

**Model / system.** The study uses Chesnavich's two-degree-of-freedom model for the ion--molecule reaction $\mathrm{CH}_4^+ightarrow\mathrm{CH}_3^+ + \mathrm{H}$. This model contains the necessary structures (inner and outer transition-state bottlenecks) to exhibit classical roaming dynamics.

**Key observables.** Projected roaming probability ($P_{	ext{roam}}$), outer probability ($P_{	ext{outer}}$), radial Husimi projection peak location ($p_{r,H}=0$), and angular-momentum weights.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis is performed at a fixed reference energy ($E_{	ext{ref}}=0.5$) and involves tuning the CAP parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The definition of 'quantum roaming' is operational: it requires the state to be localized in the classically defined roaming region, and the diagnostics are analogues of classical phase-space signatures.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the classical reference structures (TTS, FR1, OTS), the radial Husimi projection showing a peak at intermediate radius with zero radial momentum, and comparisons of probabilities locating the primary state in the projected roaming region.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the classical roaming concept, details the quantum resonance calculation using CAP, and then systematically analyzes the resulting wavefunctions using multiple phase-space diagnostics to pinpoint the state matching the classical description.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Roaming reactions occur when a molecule enters a near-dissociation region, avoids immediate separation, and later forms products by a pathway not controlled by the conventional tight transition-state bottleneck. Classical studies have shown that roaming is best understood in phase space: inner and outer transition-state structures, together with their invariant manifolds, organize trapping, return, and dissociation. The corresponding quantum question is less settled. Can a single quantum resonance carry a recognizable signature of the classical roaming region? We address this question in Chesnavich's two-degree-of-freedom model for the ion--molecule reaction $\mathrm{CH}_4^+\rightarrow\mathrm{CH}_3^+ + \mathrm{H}$. Resonance states are computed with a complex absorbing potential and analyzed using diagnostics designed to mirror the classical phase-space picture: radial probability weights derived from the tight and outer transition-state structures, radial Husimi projections, angular-momentum channel weights, and coherent-state probes of the classical periodic orbits. One resonance is distinguished from the rest of the computed resonance ensemble. Its wavefunction is concentrated in the projected region between the inner and outer transition-state structures, its radial phase-space distribution is centered at intermediate radius with nearly zero radial momentum, and its angular structure is consistent with a standing rather than a directed rotating component. We interpret this state as a phase-space-localized quantum analogue of classical roaming. The result provides a controlled example in which quantum roaming is identified directly from a resonance wavefunction and its phase-space diagnostics, rather than only from product-state or scattering signatures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.30429"></a>
### [Attention-based optimizer for symmetry finding](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30429v1)

**Authors:** Shreya Banerjee, Vinodh Raj Rajagopal Muthu, Charlie Nation, Rick P. A. Simon, Francesco Martini, Alessandro Ricottone, Federico Cerisola, Luca Dellantonio  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30429v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30429_figures/2605.30429_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Schematic diagram for the attention-based optimization framework to find a Pauli symmetry Sp of the input Hamiltonian H. H is first embedded into its tableau representation Ht [49, 50] in the input embedding layer, and then is passed through a linear row-wise projection layer [51, 52]. The underlying machine-learning model is a Set-Transformer [51, 53] (light-blue box with dashed border in left), consisting of Encoder-Decoder stacks and an activation module. The encoder (light-orange) contains stacked Set Attention Blocks (SAB) [51] (two in the figure). The Decoder (dark green) includes a Pooling Multi-head Attention layer (PMA) [54], followed by a SAB, layer-norm [55], and a linear...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30429_figures/2605.30429_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: (a): Benchmark on random Hamiltonians with 10 qubits and increasing R. a(1) shows the average (light- green) and minimum (dark-green) times taken to find a symmetry. a(2) showcases the performance of the ACE module. We plot the average (light-green) and minimum (dark-green) contexts N needed to find a symmetry, rescaled by the full context (red dashed line) 2R. The orange dashed line reports the initial number of Pauli-Strings ≈R2. a(3) presents the probability of failure pf (red) of the attention-based framework and the number of parallel starts ns (green stars) required by the optimizer to achieve a 90% success rate, as well as the number of GPUs nGPU (green triangles) to enable...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces an attention-based optimization framework that uses a Set-Transformer to automatically discover Pauli symmetries of quantum Hamiltonians. By minimizing a loss function derived from the commutation relation, the method achieves state-of-the-art performance on physical models like the Ising and Toric codes. This represents a significant integration of machine learning into the field of quantum many-body physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the structure of quantum Hamiltonians by using deep learning to discover fundamental symmetries, which is crucial for simplifying and understanding complex many-body physics models.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is to automatically find the Pauli symmetries of a given quantum Hamiltonian, merging automated symmetry detection with machine learning techniques.

**Main result.** The proposed attention-based optimizer successfully finds symmetries for physical Hamiltonians (Ising, Toric code) with near-deterministic probability, significantly outperforming state-of-the-art deterministic methods.

**Method.** The framework uses a Set-Transformer architecture to encode correlations among Pauli-Strings, optimizing a loss function based on the commutation relation between the Hamiltonian and the candidate symmetry.

**Model / system.** The method is applied to various quantum systems, including random Pauli Hamiltonians, the transverse-field Ising model (1D and 2D), and the Toric code. The Hamiltonian is represented in a tableau form.

**Key observables.** Commutation loss ($	ext{C}_{	ext{com}}$), failure probability ($p_f$), required computational resources (parallel starts, GPUs), and the resulting symmetry vector $S_p$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The rank ($R$) of the Hamiltonian, the system size ($n_q$), and regularization coefficients ($\alpha_{com}, \alpha_{zp}, \alpha_{bin}, \alpha_{lin}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of $\sin^2(rac{\pi}{2} x)$ as a differentiable proxy for modulo 2 arithmetic introduces a highly multi-modal loss landscape, requiring multiple optimization starts.

**Figures summary.** Figures benchmark the optimizer against deterministic methods on random and physical Hamiltonians, showing speedups in time and iterations required to find symmetries.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the ML framework (Set-Transformer, loss function, ACE module) and then validates it by benchmarking performance across random, 1D Ising, and 2D Ising/Toric code Hamiltonians, comparing results against established deterministic algorithms.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Finding symmetries is crucial for understanding physical models. In this work, we present an optimization framework that searches Pauli symmetries of Hamiltonians, merging the fields of machine learning with automated symmetry finding. Built on a Set-Transformer architecture, our framework uses self-attention to encode the pairwise and higher-order correlations among the Pauli-Strings. The relations are then decoded as a candidate, which is further optimized with a custom commutation-based objective, and mapped to a symmetry of the input Hamiltonian. We apply our method to random Pauli Hamiltonians, periodic one and two dimensional transverse-field Ising model and the Toric code. We show that for physical Hamiltonians (Ising and Toric), our framework succeeds with near-deterministic probability while providing substantial advantage compared to state-of-the-art strategies. For random Pauli Hamiltonians, we estimate the required computational resources, specifically the number of parallel starts and the number of GPUs, to find a symmetry with high success probability under fixed design specifications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31525"></a>
### [High-rate and computationally-efficient seedless extractors for device-independent quantum cryptography](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31525v1)

**Authors:** Simone Lin, Cameron Foreman, Lluis Masanes  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31525v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31525_figures/2605.31525_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Key rate R versus CHSH violation (8we −4) in the large-n regime.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel, efficient proof technique for constructing seedless randomness extractors essential for device-independent quantum cryptography. By employing a truncation method, the authors overcome previous limitations regarding the number of required experimental rounds. This results in a protocol that achieves the optimal key generation rate, advancing the practical security guarantees of quantum key distribution.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides rigorous, advanced theoretical tools (truncation methods, spectral analysis) for quantifying security in quantum communication protocols, which is fundamental to quantum information theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the need for high-rate and computationally-efficient seedless randomness extractors for device-independent (DI) quantum cryptography.

**Main result.** The authors present a new proof technique using a truncation method that achieves the optimal key rate of one bit per singlet for one-party DI randomness generation.

**Method.** The core method involves developing a new proof technique based on truncation to estimate protocol parameters, circumventing the need for many rounds previously required when bounding Bell inequality expectations.

**Model / system.** The protocol is set in the context of DI quantum cryptography, utilizing two non-communicating quantum devices (Alice and Bob) whose security relies on violating a Bell inequality (like CHSH). The analysis involves bounding the trace distance between actual and ideal key states.

**Key observables.** Bell Violation (CHSH inequality), Key Rate (bits per singlet), Trace Distance ($\epsilon$), and the raw key vector $\mathbf{a}_r$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The asymptotic key rate, the error parameter $\epsilon$, and the threshold winning frequency $w_e$ for the CHSH game.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The security proof relies on bounding the trace distance using spectral decomposition and a truncation method, which is shown to be effective for the CHSH setting.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the positive region for the key rate $R$ as a function of the CHSH violation ($8w_e - 4$).

**Paper structure.** The paper develops proofs for several lemmas, including those related to state dilation (Lemma 1), spectral decomposition of measurement operators (Lemma 2), bounding the trace distance via truncation (Lemma 3), and finally establishing the key rate bound using linear algebra and probability tools (Lemma 7).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Device-independent (DI) quantum cryptography provides secure cryptography with minimal trust in, or characterisation of, the used quantum devices. An essential component of DI protocols is the use of randomness extractors for privacy amplification, but these typically require an initial seed of randomness that introduces a potential vulnerability. To solve this problem, the security of seedless extractors was proven in Quantum 9, 1654 (2025). The core idea was to use the Bell violation of the raw data, rather than its min-entropy, as the extractor promise. However, the large fluctuations in the Bell inequality used required many rounds to precisely estimate the Bell violation, consuming substantial randomness and making the protocol very inefficient. In this work, we present a new proof technique based on a truncation method that allows the user to estimate the protocol parameters with an asymptotically vanishing fraction of rounds and, as a consequence, achieves the optimal rate of one key bit per singlet. Notably, we prove this result using seedless extractors that can be implemented efficiently.

</details>

### [Intrinsic locality dimension of quantum codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31441v1)

**Authors:** Yimin Lu, Esther Xiaozhen Fu, Zi-Wen Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31441v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3. **Summary.** This work introduces the intrinsic locality dimension, based on the Assouad dimension, as a unifying geometric parameter for quantum error-correcting codes. It demonstrates that this dimension characterizes code properties, generalizing existing bounds for topological and lattice codes. The theory provides a versatile mathematical foundation for understanding the fundamental geometric limits of fault tolerance.

**Why it may be interesting.** The geometric framework provides a powerful, abstract tool to constrain the fundamental capabilities of quantum error correction, linking algebraic structure to geometric measure theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a general, intrinsic geometric characterization of quantum error-correcting codes that is independent of the background geometry.

**Main result.** The intrinsic locality dimension, defined via the Assouad dimension, serves as a fundamental parameter unifying code properties and generalizing known bounds for code parameters and fault-tolerant gates.

**Method.** The authors employ concepts from fractal geometry, specifically the Assouad dimension, to analyze the connectivity graphs associated with stabilizer codes.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on stabilizer quantum codes, analyzing their connectivity graphs, which can be derived from topological structures, fractal sets, or lattice constructions.

**Key observables.** Intrinsic locality dimension ($eta$), code parameters ($k, d, n$), and the Assouad dimension of the connectivity graph.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The intrinsic dimension $eta$ can take non-integer values; the analysis depends on the structure of the underlying manifold or lattice.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the existence of good triangulations for topological codes and quasi-convexity for fractal constructions to establish dimension equivalences.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Assouad dimension concept using covering numbers and show examples of fractal structures (like the Sierpiński gasket) whose dimensions are calculated.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the intrinsic locality dimension using the Assouad dimension, applies this to specific code types (topological, fractal, lattice), derives general bounds, and concludes with implications for thermal stability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum error-correcting codes are a cornerstone of quantum computing, with broad and profound connections to physics and mathematics. In this work, we introduce the notion of intrinsic locality dimension of stabilizer codes that is independent of any background geometry and naturally incorporates flexible architectures and accommodates noninteger values, drawing on mathematical machinery from fractal geometry and geometric measure theory. Important scenarios include topological codes and algebraic codes such as bivariate-bicycle-type codes. We show how the intrinsic dimension serves as a fundamental organizing parameter that unifies code properties. In particular, we prove general limitations on code parameters and compatible fault-tolerant logical gates induced by the intrinsic dimension, generalizing the Bravyi--Poulin--Terhal and Bravyi--König bounds for regular topological codes, respectively. Furthermore, we discuss implications on thermal properties, presenting a conditional no-go result for self-correcting quantum memories in dimension $3-ε$ for any $ε>0$. Our theory lays a versatile and unifying mathematical foundation for studying the fundamental capabilities and geometric implementations of quantum error correction and fault tolerance.

</details>

### [Optimization of multisite reactions in complex compartmentalized media](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31418v1)

**Authors:** T. V. Mendes, T. Guérin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31418v1>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work analyzes how confining a reaction target within a compartment characterized by sub-diffusion can optimize the rate of multisite chemical reactions. By deriving scaling laws for mean first passage times, the authors show that the resulting reaction time can be minimized at an optimal compartment size, demonstrating kinetic acceleration despite local transport slowing.

**Why it may be interesting.** The core concept of kinetic optimization via geometric confinement is relevant to understanding reaction rates in complex biological environments, which often exhibit fractal or disordered structures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how the geometry and dynamics of a complex, compartmentalized medium influence the kinetics of multisite chemical reactions.

**Main result.** The presence of a crowded compartment can accelerate the reaction rate compared to an unconfined system, provided the target is weakly reactive, by optimizing the search process.

**Method.** The authors derive analytical expressions for mean first passage times and reaction times by adapting arguments from random walk theory in fractal and diffusive media.

**Model / system.** The model involves a random walker searching for two or more activation events (dual-site reactions) within a compartment exhibiting fractal sub-diffusion, contrasting this with classical 3D diffusion outside.

**Key observables.** Mean reaction time ($\langle T angle$), mean first passage time ($\langle 	au^*_r angle$), and the speed-up factor relative to the no-compartment case.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Compartment size ($R$), reaction probability ($p$), time window ($\Delta$), and diffusion exponents ($d_w, d_f$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the weakly reactive limit ($p \ll 1$) and the regime where the time window $\Delta$ is much smaller than the characteristic escape time ($\Delta \ll \langle 	au_\infty angle$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the optimization of $\langle T angle$ with respect to compartment radius ($R$) and the comparison of the acceleration factor ($\langle T angle / \langle T angle_{n.c.}$) versus reactivity ($p$).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from calculating mean first passage times in confined/fractal media to deriving the reaction time for multisite events, culminating in showing the optimal size $R_m$ that minimizes $\langle T angle$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In complex media, transport and geometric properties deeply influence the kinetics of random encounters between reactants. Here, we consider the situation where a random walker, moving in a regularly diffusing medium, has to reach and activate a target located inside a compartment characterized by fractal (obstructed) sub-diffusion. We focus on dual-site reactions, which end when two activation events occur within a given time window. Each activation event happens with a finite probability whenever the random walker visits the target. For weakly reactive targets, we demonstrate that the reaction time can be minimized for an optimal compartment size and can even be accelerated when compared to the same system without compartment. Our analytical predictions are validated through simulations of a random walker on a cubic lattice, where some sites inside the compartment are obstructed at the critical percolation threshold. Our theory illustrates the fact that adding a crowded compartment around a target, even if it slows down the motion in its vicinity, can accelerate the kinetics of complex reactions, especially for weakly reactive targets.

</details>

### [Real-Time Quantum Error Correction System Stack: Architecture, Algorithms, and Engineering Practice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30765v1)

**Authors:** Yaojian Chen, Chun-Yang Luan, Peilin Zheng, Xianghong Zeng, Jia-Yi Hou, Zhuo Fu, Yirong Jin, Fei Wang, Guangwen Yang, Dingshun Lv  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30765v1>

<sub>Figure 3 shows the mean per-round decode latency in mode at five physical error rates,</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>FIG. 4. Window mode: per-round mean decode latency vs. code distance at different p. Each</sub>

**Summary.** This white paper addresses the engineering gap preventing scalable quantum computation by analyzing the real-time constraints of Quantum Error Correction (QEC). It proposes a detailed, multi-layer system architecture and benchmarks decoders to show that system latency, not just algorithmic speed, is the primary bottleneck. The work is vital for guiding the development of fault-tolerant quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a crucial systems-level roadmap for quantum computing hardware, detailing the necessary classical control stack and timing budgets required to make fault-tolerant computation practical.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is transitioning Quantum Error Correction (QEC) from theoretical feasibility to a scalable, real-time systems engineering challenge for Fault-Tolerant Quantum Computing (FTQC).

**Main result.** The bottleneck for scalable QEC is not purely algorithmic but resides in system-level engineering constraints, particularly managing tail latency and end-to-end data path coordination.

**Method.** The authors propose a six-layer reference architecture and benchmark major decoder algorithms (e.g., MWPM, BP+OSD) across different code types (Surface, qLDPC) to quantify real-time readiness.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as a multi-stage data path: syndrome acquisition, classical decoding, error inference, and correction application, which must operate within strict time budgets.

**Key observables.** QEC round time, tail latency (p99), sustained decode throughput ($\mu$), and the accumulated decoding backlog $B(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The operational regime shifts between streaming (Clifford gates) and synchronization barriers (non-Clifford gates like T gates), which dictate the required feedforward latency.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes a uniform depolarizing noise model, though the paper notes that real-device effects like leakage and crosstalk are omitted in current benchmarks.

**Figures summary.** The notes reference a multi-stage data path diagram (Figure 1) and tables comparing various decoders and platform-specific timing constraints.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the QEC cycle, identifies the shift from algorithmic to engineering bottlenecks, proposes a layered reference architecture, and benchmarks performance across different physical platforms and code families.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum error correction (QEC) is transitioning from physical feasibility demonstrations to systems engineering challenges. Google has achieved below-threshold performance on distance-5/7 surface codes, while Riverlane and Rigetti have demonstrated hardware-integrated low-latency feedback loops. These milestones indicate that the core challenge of real-time decoding has shifted from algorithmic capability to system-level engineering. However, a substantial engineering gap remains between laboratory demonstrations and scalable fault-tolerant quantum computing (FTQC). This white paper addresses three questions: (1) Where are the real bottlenecks in real-time QEC: beyond average decoder speed, the constraints lie in QEC round time, tail latency, and end-to-end data path coordination; (2) How mature are mainstream decoder algorithms: we benchmark the major decoders for both surface codes and quantum low-density parity-check (qLDPC) codes, evaluating their real-time readiness; (3) What system stack do we propose: a six-layer reference architecture from syndrome acquisition to logical operations, with interface definitions and latency budget models. Our results quantify the gap between current decoder performance and real-time requirements, and identify the architectural choices needed to close it.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31139"></a>
### [Shallow Electronic State Preparation for Quantum Chemistry with Quantum Monte Carlo Pre-Selection](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31139v1)
