**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) pre-screening technique to construct shallow, hardware-efficient quantum circuits for molecular electronic structure calculations. By using Givens rotations, the method significantly reduces circuit depth compared to standard UCC ansätze, enabling chemical accuracy on current noisy quantum devices. This provides a crucial, tunable trade-off between computational power and hardware feasibility for quantum chemistry.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the gap between theoretical quantum chemistry requirements and the physical limitations of near-term quantum hardware by proposing a resource-aware ansatz construction technique.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is developing quantum algorithms for molecular electronic structure simulation that are shallow enough to run reliably on current, noisy quantum hardware.

**Main result.** The proposed QMC-Givens ansatz provides a significantly shallower and more hardware-efficient alternative to conventional Unitary Coupled Cluster (UCC) methods, especially in the presence of noise.

**Method.** The authors combine Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) pre-screening to identify crucial wavefunction components with the use of Givens rotation ansätze within the Variational Quantum Eigensolver (VQE) framework.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on simulating the electronic structure of small molecules ($	ext{LiH}$, $	ext{BeH}_2$, $	ext{N}_2$) using fermionic operators mapped to qubits. Benchmarking was performed on the Quantinuum System Model H1.

**Key observables.** Ground state energy, circuit depth, Hardware Quantum Credits (HQC) cost, and the Spearman rank correlation coefficient ($ho$) quantifying parameter importance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The trade-off between circuit expressivity (depth) and circuit depth is a key parameter space explored, tailored for current high-noise devices.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The controlled Givens ansatz is used for its relative ease of classical implementation; furthermore, the final analysis suggests that zero-initialization might outperform QMC-initialization for certain systems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the correlation between early determinant spawning and final amplitudes (Fig. 1, 2), compare energy error vs. parameters (Fig. 3), and compare initialization strategies (Fig. 4).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QMC-Givens method as an improvement over UCC for quantum chemistry. It details the QMC pre-screening procedure, benchmarks the ansatz on molecular systems using hardware metrics (HQC), and concludes by demonstrating the practical advantages of shallow circuits on noisy hardware.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum computers hold great promise for molecular simulation, but noise remains a fundamental obstacle. We introduce a Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) pre-screening procedure that constructs compact, physically motivated Givens rotation ansätze tailored to realistic quantum hardware. By identifying the most important wavefunction contributions early in a QMC simulation, we build circuits that are shallower that conventional alternatives while preserving number symmetry. Benchmarked on Quantinuum System Model H1, QMC-prescreened circuits outperform more complex ansätze under realistic noise conditions. The method offers a practical path toward chemical accuracy on quantum devices, by providing an adjustable trade-off between expressivity and circuit depth to generate shallow circuits suited to current high-noise devices, as well as deeper, more expressive circuits that can be deployed on future lower-noise devices.

</details>

**Summary.** This review details how Second Harmonic Generation (SHG) acts as a symmetry-resolved optical technique to probe hidden order parameters in quantum and functional materials. By analyzing polarization-resolved rotational anisotropy (RA-SHG), researchers can map out the symmetry-allowed components of the nonlinear susceptibility tensor. This methodology provides a crucial link between fundamental group theory and the measurable electronic and magnetic states of complex condensed matter systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection between symmetry group theory, nonlinear optics, and emergent electronic order parameters provides a powerful, non-perturbative diagnostic tool for studying symmetry breaking in strongly correlated systems, which is highly relevant to modern condensed matter theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is identifying the symmetry of ordered phases in quantum and functional materials, as these phases are defined by the symmetries they break.

**Main result.** The paper establishes that Second Harmonic Generation (SHG) spectroscopy, particularly polarization-resolved rotational anisotropy (RA-SHG), serves as a powerful, symmetry-resolved optical probe to map out these hidden order parameters.

**Method.** The methodology combines rigorous quantum mechanical derivations of nonlinear susceptibility tensors ($\chi$) with group-theoretical constraints (Landau theory) to predict symmetry-allowed SHG responses.

**Model / system.** The analysis focuses on condensed matter systems, including polar, magnetic, and electronic materials, using the nonlinear optical process of SHG ($\omega 	o 2\omega$). The theoretical framework involves multipole expansions (ED, MD, EQ) and symmetry analysis within point groups like $C_{4v}$.

**Key observables.** Nonlinear susceptibility tensor components ($\chi_{ijk}$), SHG intensity as a function of rotation angle ($\varphi$), and the symmetry-allowed coupling terms related to order parameters ($\phi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Crystallographic and magnetic point group symmetries, polarization states (P/C, SS/SP/PS/PP), and the order parameter magnitude ($\phi$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quantum mechanical formalism is rigorous but may struggle with materials exhibiting strong electronic correlations or complex orbital hybridization; the symmetry analysis relies on the invariance principles of the parent point group.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the multipole sources (ED, MD, EQ) for SHG radiation and schematically depict the various geometries for RA-SHG measurements (normal vs. oblique incidence).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from the basic theoretical background of SHG sources (multipole expansion) to the quantum mechanical derivation of susceptibility tensors, followed by a comprehensive review of RA-SHG applications across various symmetry-breaking phenomena, and concluding with future research directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Second harmonic generation (SHG) has evolved from a probe of noncentrosymmetric crystals into a symmetry-resolved optical method for identifying order parameters in quantum and functional materials. In particular, polarization-resolved rotational anisotropy (RA) measurements of SHG can connect nonlinear susceptibility tensors to the crystallographic and magnetic point groups of the underlying materials. This capability is especially powerful when the ordered state is weak, spatially confined, multipolar, magnetic, or hidden from conventional linear probe techniques. In this review article, we provide a comprehensive overview of RA-SHG studies across a broad range of condensed matter systems. We begin with basic theoretical background for the multipole origins of SHG radiation, the construction of nonlinear susceptibility tensors, and group-theoretical framework connecting tensor components to order parameters. We then review the applications of RA-SHG to polar materials, magnetic orders, and other hidden electronic materials. Finally, we outline challenges and future research directions for using SHG to reveal, image, and control hidden, intertwined, and nonequilibrium phases in quantum and functional materials.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel, denser planar surface code architecture by efficiently packing twist defects into a 2D hex grid. This advancement boosts the encoding rate by 4.5x, leading to substantial reductions in the required physical qubits and computational time for simulating complex electronic structures, such as nitrogen fixation catalysts.

**Why it may be interesting.** The focus on optimizing resource scaling for quantum simulation, particularly by improving the encoding rate of topological codes, directly impacts the feasibility timeline for quantum chemistry simulations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is achieving utility-scale quantum simulation for electronic structure problems while drastically reducing the required physical quantum resources.

**Main result.** The authors propose a dense packing of surface code twist defects, achieving an estimated encoding rate up to 4.5x that of standard surface codes, leading to significant improvements in resource estimates.

**Method.** The work develops new stabilizer measurement cycles and padding-free lattice surgery protocols to implement a denser, more efficient planar surface code architecture.

**Model / system.** The system is a quantum computer utilizing the surface code for fault-tolerant quantum error correction, implemented on a regular 2D hex grid. The target application is ground state phase estimation for electronic structure problems.

**Key observables.** Encoding rate (up to 4.5x improvement), minimum physical quantum volume (1.3 mega-qubit-hours), and resource trade-offs across space, time, and qubit count.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise model: one- and two-qubit $10^{-3}$ error uniform depolarizing model. Time scales: $1\mu$s cycle time, $10\mu$s reaction time. Target: Chemical accuracy for FeMoco catalyst.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes an ideal outer decoder for lattice surgery and uses circuit-level noise modeling; the comparison to prior art relies on specific resource estimates (e.g., Toffoli gate counts).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the Pareto frontier of physical resources for ground state phase estimation, comparing the new dense-packing approach against prior art across 'Compact', 'Balanced', 'Efficient', and 'Fast' regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first identifying the resource bottleneck of standard surface codes, then introducing the dense-packing twist defect strategy, detailing the optimized lattice surgery protocols, and finally applying the resulting resource estimates to benchmark utility-scale quantum simulation tasks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a quantum code implementable on a regular $2$D hex grid with an estimated encoding rate up to $4.5\times$ of that of a rotated surface code patch using circuit-level noise in a one- and two-qubit $10^{-3}$ error uniform depolarizing model. Our approach is based on yoking a dense packing of surface code twist defects, enabled by new stabilizer measurement cycles with an optimal four layers of nearest-neighbor two-qubit gates, almost no distance-reducing hook errors, and efficient decoding. We demonstrate a space-efficient architecture for computing on densely packed logical qubits, including new padding-free lattice surgery protocols in an optimal bounding box of $2d^2$ data and measurement qubits per patch. Assuming a $1μ$s surface code cycle time and a $10μ$s reaction time, these developments enable chemically accurate ground state phase estimation of a broad class of `utility-scale' electronic structure simulation problems such as the $108$ spin-orbital FeMoco-based nitrogen fixation catalyst in under a month with $89$k noisy superconducting qubits. We elucidate a Pareto frontier of space-time trade-offs and find a minimum physical quantum volume of $1.3$ mega-qubit-hours. These correspond to a $36\times$ space and $6.6\times$ spacetime improvement, respectively, over our previous state-of-the-art minimum-Toffoli resource estimates (Phys. Rev. X 15, 041016).

</details>

### [Ambiguity problem of the Bootstrap Method in Quantum Mechanics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30536v1)

**Authors:** Takeshi Morita, Worapat Piensuk, Pushkar Soni

**Summary.** This paper investigates the ambiguity problem inherent in applying the bootstrap method to quantum Hamiltonians with mixed potentials. The authors demonstrate that the method fails for such potentials but propose and test several resolutions, including imposing additional positivity constraints. These resolutions successfully recover the correct energy spectrum for specific cases, providing a more robust theoretical framework.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work tackles a fundamental theoretical limitation of a powerful mathematical tool (the bootstrap method) used to determine quantum spectra, offering systematic ways to resolve ambiguities arising from operator structure.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The bootstrap method for quantum mechanics suffers from an ambiguity problem, failing to yield the correct energy spectrum when the potential contains mixed functional forms (e.g., polynomial and exponential terms).

**Main result.** The ambiguity can be resolved by imposing additional positivity conditions, such as $M_{\cosh} \succeq 0$, which successfully reproduce the true energy spectrum for certain potentials.

**Method.** The authors use the bootstrap method, which constrains energy eigenvalues by enforcing the positive semi-definite condition ($M \succeq 0$) on a constructed bootstrap matrix derived from expectation values.

**Model / system.** The analysis focuses on quantum mechanical Hamiltonians, specifically comparing the anharmonic oscillator ($H_{AHO}$) with a mixed potential $H_{mixed} = rac{1}{2} \hat{p}^2 + (\hat{x} - L)^2 + \cosh(\hat{x})$.

**Key observables.** Energy eigenvalues ($E$) and expectation values ($\langle \hat{x} angle$, $\langle e^{\hat{x}} angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The potential shift parameter $L$, and the size parameters ($K_x, K_p$) used in the bootstrap matrix construction.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the energy spectrum must satisfy the positivity condition on the bootstrap matrix; resolutions involve imposing additional constraints like parity or specific positivity inequalities.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the allowed regions in the $(E, \langle \hat{x} angle)$-plane for both the anharmonic oscillator (convergence shown) and the mixed potential (failure/resolution shown using different constraints).

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the failure of the bootstrap method for mixed potentials, then proposes and analyzes three resolutions: Taylor expansion, imposing parity, and imposing an additional positivity condition, culminating in the successful resolution for $L=0$ using the combined constraints.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The bootstrap method for quantum mechanics is a powerful tool for computing the energy eigenvalues of a Hamiltonian. However, we point out that this method suffers from an ambiguity problem: it fails to yield the correct spectrum when the potential contains different types of functions, such as polynomial and exponential terms. Similarly, the bootstrap method may break down when evaluating the expectation values of operators of different types. This issue can arise in a wide range of systems, including statistical models and matrix models. We propose three possible resolutions to this problem.

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes the phase transitions of the Simplicial Ising Model on hypergraphs with varying structural disorder. By developing a cavity method framework, the authors show that size and degree heterogeneities fundamentally alter the transition type, leading to complex double transitions. The findings highlight the intricate interplay between these structural features in collective ordering phenomena.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed analysis of phase transitions driven by structural disorder (heterogeneity) in interacting systems is a core topic in statistical mechanics, providing theoretical tools applicable to complex network dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how structural heterogeneity, specifically in group size and node degree, influences the collective phase transitions in systems governed by higher-order interactions.

**Main result.** Size heterogeneity sharpens the transition via large-group unanimity, while degree heterogeneity softens it; the coexistence of both can lead to complex double transitions and hysteretic regions modulated by cross-order degree correlations.

**Method.** The study develops a cavity-method framework and applies it to the Simplicial Ising Model (SIM) on heterogeneous hypergraphs, utilizing the Effective Medium Approximation (EMA) near the critical point.

**Model / system.** The system is the Simplicial Ising Model (SIM) defined on heterogeneous hypergraphs. The Hamiltonian involves higher-order interactions ($\delta_{\{S_i\}_{i\in\partial_a}}$) that enforce that all spins within a hyperedge must be identical.

**Key observables.** Magnetization ($m$), phase transition types (Continuous (C), Discontinuous (D), Double (C+D)), and Cross-Order Degree Correlation (CODC).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Size heterogeneity ($\gamma_q$) and degree heterogeneity ($\gamma_k$), and the cross-order correlation parameter ($\alpha$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the cavity method, which is exact only on locally treelike hypergraphs, and uses the Effective Medium Approximation (EMA) near the critical point.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the phase diagrams showing the emergence of (C+D) regions in planes defined by heterogeneity parameters ($\gamma_q$ vs $k$, $\gamma_k$ vs $q$) and the effect of CODC ($\alpha$ vs $T$).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the general framework of the SIM on hypergraphs, derives cavity equations, applies EMA, and then systematically analyzes the distinct roles of size and degree heterogeneities, culminating in the effect of cross-order correlations on the phase diagram.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Higher-order interactions admit richer structural heterogeneity than pairwise networks. To understand how heterogeneity impacts collective phenomena we develop a framework based on the cavity method and apply it to the simplicial Ising model on heterogeneous hypergraphs. Unlike in homogeneous structures, group size and node degree play fundamentally different roles: size heterogeneity sharpens the transition via large-group unanimity, while degree heterogeneity softens it as hubs cooperatively seed ordering with non-hubs. Under either type of heterogeneity, continuous--discontinuous double transitions can arise, where the symmetry-breaking continuous transition is driven by pairs or by hubs, respectively. When both heterogeneities coexist, cross-order degree correlations further modulate the phase diagram, with anticorrelation delaying the group-driven discontinuous jump and broadening the hysteretic region. Our results reveal the intricate interplay between size and degree heterogeneities in collective phenomena beyond pairwise interactions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.30967"></a>
### [Evaluating higher-order product formulae for molecular ground-state energy estimation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30967v1)

**Summary.** This paper rigorously compares the resource costs (gate count and circuit depth) of different higher-order product formulae for simulating molecular ground-state energies. Using hydrogen chains as benchmarks, the authors demonstrate that the optimal formula depends on the required accuracy, proposing a new fourth-order method that achieves superior performance over existing state-of-the-art formulae near chemical accuracy.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the practical resource estimation for quantum chemistry simulations, providing concrete guidelines on selecting the most efficient quantum circuit decomposition method for molecular energy calculations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper evaluates and compares the computational cost of various deterministic higher-order product formulae used to estimate the ground-state energy of molecules via quantum simulation.

**Main result.** A newly constructed fourth-order product formula was shown to outperform established eighth-order formulae in minimizing both the total gate count and the RZ-layer depth near chemical accuracy.

**Method.** The cost is evaluated by estimating the total gate count and the depth of RZ-rotation layers required to achieve a target energy error ($\epsilon_E$) using perturbative analysis.

**Model / system.** The benchmarks use one-dimensional hydrogen chains ($	ext{H}_2$ to $	ext{H}_{15}$) modeled by a Hamiltonian approximated via product formulae for the time-evolution operator.

**Key observables.** Total gate count ($F$), depth of RZ-rotation layers ($D_{	ext{RZ}}$), and the energy error ($\epsilon_E$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target energy error ($\epsilon_E = 1.6 	imes 10^{-4} 	ext{ Ha}$), formal order of the product formula ($p$), and system size ($N$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis uses a perturbative method to estimate eigenvalue error, and the layer depth estimate neglects the depth contribution from the Clifford basis-change circuit.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the total gate count and RZ-layer depth for different formulae across H-chain sizes, and show the scaling relationships between these costs and system size.

**Paper structure.** The paper first analyzes the phase estimation error scaling, then details the cost metrics (gate count and layer depth) derived from product formulae approximations, compares various orders (2nd, 4th, 8th, 10th), and concludes by proposing and validating a new optimized fourth-order formula.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We evaluate deterministic higher-order product formulae for molecular ground-state energy estimation. Motivated by recent fault-tolerant architectures in which non-Clifford operations may be generated more locally and cheaply than in conventional assumptions, we re-examine such formulae as practical candidates for quantum chemistry. Using one-dimensional hydrogen chains from $\mathrm{H}_2$ to $\mathrm{H}_{15}$ as benchmarks, we estimate both the total gate count and the depth of $R_Z$-rotation layers required to reach a target energy error. To make this comparison feasible at larger system sizes, we use a perturbative method to estimate the eigenvalue error induced by each product formula and thereby evaluate the cost of the corresponding phase-estimation procedure. Among the previously considered formulae, the eighth-order construction introduced by Morales et al. [M. E. S. Morales et al., "Greatly improved higher-order product formulae for quantum simulation," arXiv:2210.15817v2 (2024)] minimizes both cost metrics in the benchmark at a chemically relevant target error. We also find that increasing the formal order does not automatically reduce the total cost: near chemical accuracy, the tenth-order formula introduced in the same work can be less efficient than the eighth-order one. Motivated by this observation, we construct a new fourth-order formula; it achieves the lowest total gate count among the formulae considered for all H-chain instances near chemical accuracy and over much of the 0.1-10 mHa target-error window for most instances, while also reducing the $R_Z$-layer depth. These results clarify how deterministic higher-order product formulae should be selected for molecular ground-state energy estimation.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a generative, energy-based framework to optimize quantum data embeddings for supervised learning, moving beyond fixed circuit designs. It uses an LLM to search for optimal quantum circuits and refines them with learnable biases. Crucially, it provides a theoretical bound linking the achievable quantum classification error to the 1-Wasserstein distance of the input data, offering a diagnostic tool for embedding optimization.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous connection drawn between the achievable quantum classification error and the classical Wasserstein distance provides a powerful, data-geometry-informed diagnostic tool for quantum algorithm design.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is improving the performance of supervised quantum machine learning (QML) when using classical data inputs by optimizing the quantum data embedding structure.

**Main result.** The proposed generative framework successfully optimizes embedding structures, and the achievable performance gains are diagnosable using the Wasserstein distance of the underlying classical data geometry.

**Method.** The authors use an energy-based generative learning framework, employing a Large Language Model (LLM) to synthesize and optimize quantum circuit sequences, guided by a fidelity-based surrogate objective.

**Model / system.** The system involves mapping classical inputs $x$ to quantum states $ho_{\Phi}(x)$ via an embedding circuit $\Phi$. The optimization targets the structure of $\Phi$ itself, moving beyond fixed ansatzes.

**Key observables.** QKSVM classification accuracy, the trace distance $D_{tr}(ho_+(\Phi), ho_-(\Phi))$, and the 1-Wasserstein distance $W_1(\hat{P}_+(x), \hat{P}_-(x))$ between class-conditional distributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The fidelity-based surrogate objective $\ell_{ij}(\Phi)$ and the geometric bound derived from the Wasserstein distance.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The process separates discrete architecture search (LLM/EGAS) from continuous parameter refinement (learnable bias function), and the performance is bounded by the geometry of the classical input space.

**Figures summary.** Figures demonstrate the EGAS training process, the reduction in surrogate energy ($\Delta E$) upon refinement, and split-wise comparisons of QKSVM accuracy versus classical baselines.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops from establishing the fundamental limit on class separation via the trace distance, proposing the generative search (EGAS) using fidelity loss, and finally refining the best structures using learnable biases, all while providing a geometric diagnostic via the Wasserstein distance.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many practically relevant applications of quantum machine learning involve classical data, for which performance depends critically on how inputs are embedded into quantum states. Yet the use of a fixed embedding circuit ansatz remains standard practice. We propose an energy-based generative learning framework that synthesizes gate sequences to optimize embedding structures and refine data-tailored parameters, using a fidelity-based surrogate objective to guide the search toward improved class distinguishability. Empirically, the method improves classification performance across diverse settings, while also revealing datasets where architecture search within the present embedding family yields only limited additional gains. We explain this saturation by deriving bounds on the achievable empirical risk in terms of the Wasserstein distance in the input space, showing that classical data geometry provides an \emph{a priori} diagnostic for regimes in which substantial gains from embedding optimization are unlikely. The results establish a practically useful and theoretically motivated framework for searching effective quantum data embeddings through generative optimization, with the attainable gains diagnosed through the geometry of the underlying classical data.

</details>

**Summary.** This work investigates the sources of variation in the critical current of Al/AlO_x/Al Josephson junctions essential for quantum circuit scaling. Through model-based statistical analysis, the authors pinpoint film thickness fluctuations as the main cause of variation. They demonstrate that optimizing the deposition angle to 30 degrees significantly improves junction uniformity, providing crucial guidelines for nanofabrication.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on superconducting materials, the rigorous statistical modeling of geometric variation and the quantitative link between deposition parameters (angle) and device uniformity are highly relevant to engineering quantum devices where nanoscale precision is paramount.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge addressed is achieving uniform critical current across Josephson junctions, which is critical for scaling superconducting quantum circuits.

**Main result.** The analysis shows that fluctuations in the Al film thickness are the dominant source of critical current variation, and using a 30-degree deposition angle significantly improves junction uniformity.

**Method.** The study combines statistical analysis of room-temperature tunnel resistance with a model-based approach that decomposes the total variance into contributions from different physical sources.

**Model / system.** The system is the Al/AlO_x/Al Josephson junction fabricated using the Dolan-bridge technique. The model relates the critical current variation to the variance of the junction's tunnel resistance, decomposing this variance based on geometric factors and film thickness fluctuations.

**Key observables.** Critical current (I_C), room-temperature tunnel resistance (R_N), relative standard deviation (r.s.d.) of I_C, and the geometric factor f_2(theta, N_L).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Deposition angle (theta), junction width (W), number of deposited layers (N_L), and the size of the measurement region (e.g., 1.5 mm).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the variance can be decomposed into statistically independent components, and that thickness fluctuations of different layers are uncorrelated.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate cross-sectional schematics of the junctions, show normalized RMS variation vs. junction width, and present resistance histograms for different sized measurement regions demonstrating decreasing r.s.d. with smaller regions.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the need for uniform I_C, models the variance of the junction resistance as a sum of independent components (geometry-independent, thickness-related, etc.), derives a geometric factor quantifying thickness effects, and then uses statistical analysis on fabricated junctions to identify thickness variation as dominant and quantify the improvement achieved by specific deposition angles.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Achieving uniform critical current across Josephson junctions is essential for the large-scale integration of superconducting quantum circuits. In this work, we statistically analyzed the variation of the critical current of Al/AlO${\rm _x}$/Al junctions using room-temperature tunnel resistance statistics, and identified the dominant contribution among the modeled sources of the variation based on their dependence on geometry and deposition conditions of junctions. Our model-based analysis reveals that fluctuations in the Al film thickness play the dominant role among the modeled contributing factors. Based on this analysis, we found that, in Dolan-bridge double-angle deposition, adopting a deposition angle of 30-degree for bilayer junctions significantly improves uniformity, yielding a relative standard deviation of 1.2% (0.5%) across a 9.75 mm (1.5 mm) square region.

</details>

### [More efficient Clifford+T synthesis for small-angle rotations and application to Trotterization](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31544v1)

**Authors:** Marius Bothe, Christoph Sünderhauf, Michael J. Witham, Earl T. Campbell, Nick S. Blunt

<sub>Figure 3. Regions of the complex plane for selecting the under- and over-rotation in the mixed diagonal approximation of Ref. [19]. The target angle is θ, and γ = arcsin( p</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31544_figures/2605.31544_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Schematic overview of methods for various regimes of θ and δ = λ −1. (a) shows the preferable method in each regime. <details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>Figure 5. Example region, defined in Eq. (51), for selecting the over-rotation with quasi-probabilities. Each unitary with upper left entry u = x+iy inside the shaded region has a sample complexity λ, Eq. (43), within the desired target λmax. **Summary.** This work significantly improves the theoretical understanding of synthesizing rotation gates for fault-tolerant quantum computing. By developing angle-dependent resource estimation formulas, the authors demonstrate that the T gate cost for small rotations scales favorably with $	heta^2$, rather than remaining high and independent of the angle. This has major implications for the resource requirements of quantum simulation algorithms like Trotterization.

**Why it may be interesting.** The findings provide concrete, resource-aware improvements for compiling quantum simulation circuits, directly impacting the feasibility estimates for fault-tolerant quantum computation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the high resource overhead associated with synthesizing arbitrary-angle rotation gates ($Z_	heta$) using Clifford+T gates in fault-tolerant quantum computation.

**Main result.** They show that for small rotation angles ($	heta$), the T gate cost can be significantly reduced from an angle-independent scaling to $	ilde{O}(	heta^2/\delta)$, and they develop scalable quasi-probability methods for resource estimation.

**Method.** The authors employ advanced techniques involving quasi-probability and probability mixtures of quantum channels, alongside optimizing the Linear Combination of Channels (LCC) representation for the rotation gate.

**Model / system.** The focus is on synthesizing the $RZ(	heta) = e^{i	heta Z}$ rotation gate using a Clifford+T gate set, which is critical for implementing quantum algorithms like Trotterization.

**Key observables.** T gate cost (resource estimate), average T gate count, and the total error ($\epsilon_{	ext{total}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rotation angle $	heta$, error parameter $\delta$, and the Trotter step size $t$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on comparing quasi-probability mixtures ($\lambda^{-1}$) against standard probability mixtures ($\epsilon_{\diamond}$), and assumes the applicability of the LCC representation.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare average T gate counts across different regimes ($	heta$ vs. $\delta$), illustrating the crossover from angle-independent to angle-dependent scaling, and showing the advantage of quasi-probabilities over standard methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from initial LCC representations to probability/quasi-probability mixtures, derives angle-dependent resource formulas, and applies these results to show cost reductions for Trotterization circuits.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Clifford+T synthesis of rotation gates is an important routine in fault-tolerant quantum compilation. While Clifford+T synthesis is scalable, it has a high overhead of tens of T gates per rotation in practice, translating to high resource estimates for many fault-tolerant algorithms. However, these well-known results, including those using probabilistic mixtures [Quantum 7, 1208 (2023)], are independent of the rotation angle $θ$, requiring $O(\log 1/δ)$ T gates. We show that it is possible to do much better for small angles, reducing the T cost to $\tilde O(θ^2/δ)$, and returning to existing $O(\log1/δ)$ results in the worst case. This is particularly important since many algorithms, such as Trotterization, are dominated by small-angle rotations. Further, we perform a detailed theoretical and numerical study of quasi-probabilities, which can further reduce the total T cost of large circuits by orders of magnitude with only a small overhead in sample complexity. We also develop a scheme based on quasi-probability mixtures of Clifford+T fallback channels. We derive new $θ$-dependent formulas that can be used for resource estimation of fault-tolerant quantum algorithms. As an application of our results, we show that the gate cost of Trotterization circuits compiled to a Clifford+T gate set is constant in the small Trotter step size limit, and can be reduced by orders of magnitude even for large step sizes. The cost of fault-tolerant Trotterization for a variety of applications should be re-examined in light of these results. Our work dispels the widely-stated claim that Clifford+T rotation synthesis has a high cost independent of $θ$, and further develops a scalable quasi-probability method for rotation synthesis. We also expect our results to bring forward useful early fault-tolerant quantum computing by reducing required magic state resources.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31374"></a>
### [Multi-point functions of a full-plane two-state fuzzy $4$-Potts model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31374v1)

**Authors:** Hong-Bin Chen, Jiaming Xia

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31374_figures/2605.31374_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>

<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper analyzes the critical behavior of the fuzzy 4-Potts model on the plane. It proves that the model's multi-point correlation functions converge to explicit, conformally covariant forms characteristic of Coulomb gas theories. The work establishes the continuum limit of the magnetization field, providing deep insights into the scaling properties of lattice statistical models near criticality.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous connection between lattice statistical mechanics models (like the Potts model) and continuous field theories (like the GFF/CFT) is fundamental to understanding critical phenomena in many physical systems, including those relevant to quantum many-body physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study focuses on determining the scaling limits of multi-point spin correlation functions for the full-plane two-state fuzzy 4-Potts model.

**Main result.** The even multi-point spin correlation functions converge to explicit conformally covariant Coulomb-gas type neutral charge sums, and the rescaled magnetization field converges in law to a continuum field $\Phi_0$.

**Method.** The proof combines advanced techniques including the Baxter-Kelland-Wu coupling, the convergence of the six-vertex height function to the Gaussian Free Field (GFF), and a rigorous charge-completion mechanism.

**Model / system.** The model is the full-plane two-state fuzzy 4-Potts model, derived from assigning spins to open clusters of a critical q=4 random-cluster configuration, which is equivalent to the single-spin projection of the isotropic Ashkin-Teller model at its Potts point.

**Key observables.** Multi-point spin correlation functions, the rescaled magnetization field ($\Phi_\delta$), and their limiting moments ($M_n(h)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The critical point ($p_c = 2/3$), the scaling factor $f(\delta) \sim \delta^{1/8}$, and the limit $\delta 	o 0$ (the continuum limit).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the convergence of the six-vertex height function to the GFF and the ability to establish convergence in law via moment matching in Sobolev spaces.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by first establishing the convergence of even multi-point functions to a Coulomb-gas sum (Theorem 2.2), then defining the continuum limit of the magnetization field ($\Phi_\delta 	o \Phi_0$ in $H^{-s}$), and finally completing the proof by deriving necessary combinatorial and scaling relations (A, B, C, D) to confirm the final scaling limit (Proposition 3.1).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the full-plane two-state fuzzy $4$-Potts model, obtained by assigning independent balanced $\{\pm1\}$ spins to the open clusters of a critical $q=4$ random-cluster configuration. This model corresponds exactly to the single-spin projection of the isotropic Ashkin-Teller model at its Potts point. We prove that, after proper normalization, all even multi-point spin correlation functions converge to explicit conformally covariant Coulomb-gas type neutral charge sums. As a consequence, we prove convergence in law of the rescaled magnetization field and identify the moments of the limiting field. The proof combines the Baxter-Kelland-Wu coupling, convergence of the six-vertex height function to the Gaussian free field, and a charge-completion mechanism: an enlarged discrete sum over charge assignments with total charge in $4\mathbb Z$ produces a combinatorial cancellation of connection patterns, while only the neutral charge sector survives in the scaling limit.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Ni-O hybridization-driven electronic reconstruction across the superconducting dome in an infinite-layer nickelate](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30752v1)

**Authors:** Chi Sin Tang, Shengwei Zeng, Xing Gao, Zhaoyang Luo, Xiongfang Liu, Zhi Shiuh Lim, Saurav Prakash, Ping Yang, Caozheng Diao, Xinmao Yin, Changjian Li, Huajun Liu, Mark B. H. Breese, A. Ariando

**Summary.** This experimental study investigates the electronic structure of infinite-layer nickelates across their superconducting dome using X-ray Absorption Spectroscopy. The researchers found that the superconducting state correlates with a doping-induced, orbital-selective crossover in Ni-O hybridization. This work provides a key orbital-resolved mechanism linking electronic structure changes to the superconducting properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** The direct link established between measurable spectroscopic signatures (orbital hybridization) and macroscopic superconducting behavior provides a powerful experimental handle for understanding correlated electron physics in unconventional superconductors.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand how the electronic structure of infinite-layer nickelates evolves with hole doping, specifically linking these changes to the superconducting dome.

**Main result.** A doping-dependent orbital-selective crossover in Ni-O covalency, evidenced by spectral weight redistribution, was found to coincide with transport anomalies like the Hall coefficient sign reversal and the reduction of the superconducting critical temperature.

**Method.** The research utilized X-ray Absorption Spectroscopy (XAS) at the O K-edge and Ni L-edge, combined with temperature-dependent measurements and X-ray Linear Dichroism (XLD).

**Model / system.** The physical system is the infinite-layer nickelate $	ext{La}_{1-x}	ext{Ca}_x	ext{NiO}_2$, which serves as a cuprate analogue for studying high-temperature superconductivity.

**Key observables.** O K-edge spectral weight redistribution (especially features 'a' and 'b'), Ni $L_3$-edge intensity, Hall coefficient sign, and superconducting critical temperature ($T_c$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hole doping concentration ($x$), temperature ($T$), and the doping range $0.18 \le x \le 0.27$ where superconductivity occurs.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed spectral weight changes directly reflect orbital reorganization and covalency changes in the Ni-O bonds, and that the pre-peaks are intrinsic electronic features.

**Figures summary.** Figures summarize the doping dependence of O K-edge spectra across the superconducting dome, showing spectral weight transfer; they also present temperature-dependent measurements and XLD results correlating electronic structure changes with $T_c$.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically maps the doping and temperature dependence of electronic states using XAS, correlating the observed orbital reorganization (spectral weight transfer) directly with measurable transport properties (Hall coefficient, $T_c$) across the superconducting dome.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconductivity in infinite-layer nickelates has drawn wide interest as a cuprate analogue, yet how the electronic structure evolves with hole doping remains unsettled. Here we map the doping- and temperature-dependent unoccupied states of the La-based infinite-layer nickelate La1-xCaxNiO2 using O K-edge and Ni L-edge x-ray absorption spectroscopy. Superconductivity occurs for 0.18<=x<=0.27. Near x~0.20-0.23, low-energy spectral weight redistributes: Ni3d-dominated states decrease while O2p-hybridized states increase, indicating an orbital-selective crossover in Ni-O covalency. This crossover coincides with a sign reversal of the Hall coefficient and precedes the reduction of the superconducting critical temperature at higher doping. By directly linking transport anomalies and the superconducting dome to a measurable Ni-O orbital reorganization, our results provide a key step toward a unified, orbital-resolved phase diagram for infinite-layer nickelates and a practical route to engineer superconductivity via hybridization control.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31537"></a>
### [Nonperturbative renormalization of Haldane pseudopotentials from the exact two-electron spectrum](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31537v1)

**Authors:** G. -Q. Hai, M. T. Matsubara, L. Cândido, B. G. A. Brito  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31537v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31537_figures/2605.31537_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Renormalized pseudopotentials V ∗ |m| (solid lines) and conventional Haldane pseudopotentials V|m| (dash lines) as functions of interaction strength γB for m = 0, −1, −2, ..., −9. The exact pseudopotentials remain systematically smaller than the LLL-projected values due to dynamical correlation effects associated with higher-Landau-level virtual processes. The shaded region corresponds to V ∗ |m| &gt; 1 or equivalently Erel 0m &gt; 3/2 where the lowest relative branch enters the higher- sublevel energy window. Red semi-dots indicate the threshold values γc B(m) = p</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31537_figures/2605.31537_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Renormalization of the short-range pseudopotential difference relevant to the Laughlin ν = 1/3 state. The figure compares the conventional LLL difference δLLL 13 = V1 −V3 (black-solid line), the renormalized difference δ∗ 13 = V ∗ 1 −V ∗ 3 (red-solid line), and the dynamical correction δ∆= |∆1 −∆3| (blue-dashed line) as functions of γB.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31537_figures/2605.31537_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Exact dynamical correction ∆|m| (solid lines) and perturbative Landau-level-mixing correction κ2V2(m) from Ref. [8] (dash lines) as functions of the interaction strength of γB for m=0,-1,-2,..., -9. At small γB, the exact results agree quantitatively with perturbation theory. At larger γB, substantial deviations emerge. Red semi-dots indicate the threshold values γc B(m).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work establishes a nonperturbative framework to calculate effective electron interactions in the fractional quantum Hall regime by going beyond the standard Lowest Landau Level approximation. By using the exact two-electron spectrum, the authors derive renormalized pseudopotentials that incorporate dynamical correlations from higher Landau levels. This shows that strong interactions significantly modify the effective interaction hierarchy, a crucial finding for understanding strongly correlated materials like ZnO/MgZnO heterostructures.

**Why it may be interesting.** The methodology of deriving effective interactions from the exact spectrum, rather than relying on perturbative expansions, provides a powerful, nonperturbative tool applicable to strongly correlated electron systems where simple approximations fail.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a nonperturbative description of effective electron-electron interactions (Haldane pseudopotentials) in the fractional quantum Hall regime by accounting for virtual transitions to higher Landau levels.

**Main result.** The exact renormalized pseudopotentials ($V^*|m|$) show a systematic, negative dynamical correction ($\Delta|m|$) compared to the standard Lowest Landau Level (LLL) approximation, which is crucial in strongly interacting systems.

**Method.** The authors solve the relative-motion problem using the exact two-electron spectrum in a magnetic field, defining renormalized pseudopotentials directly from the exact eigenenergies.

**Model / system.** The system is two interacting electrons confined to a 2D plane under a perpendicular magnetic field, analyzed via the relative-motion Hamiltonian which separates into center-of-mass and relative-motion sectors.

**Key observables.** Renormalized pseudopotentials ($V^*|m|$), dynamical correction ($\Delta|m|$), and the difference between short-range interaction scales ($\delta^*_{13}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimensionless interaction strength ($\gamma_B$), and characteristic thresholds ($\gamma_c^B(m)$) marking the onset of strong Landau-level mixing.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The conventional pseudopotentials are only valid in the LLL approximation; the exact formulation is necessary when Landau-level mixing is nonperturbative.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the LLL pseudopotentials ($V|m|$) against the exact renormalized values ($V^*|m|$) across various angular momenta ($m$) and interaction strengths ($\gamma_B$), highlighting the magnitude of the correction $\Delta|m|$.

**Paper structure.** The paper contrasts the conventional LLL projection method with the exact spectrum approach, deriving the renormalized pseudopotentials and quantifying the dynamical correction $\Delta|m|$ which reveals the breakdown of perturbation theory in the strong-mixing regime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Haldane pseudopotentials $V_{|m|}$ provide the effective interaction parameters governing correlated states in the fractional quantum Hall regime. In conventional formulations, these quantities are obtained by projecting the Coulomb interaction onto relative-angular-momentum states within the lowest Landau level, thereby neglecting virtual transitions to higher Landau levels. Here, we formulate a nonperturbative description of effective interactions directly from the exact two-electron spectrum in a magnetic field. By solving the relative-motion problem beyond the lowest-Landau-level approximation, we define renormalized pseudopotentials $V^*_{|m|}$ from the exact eigenenergies and introduce dynamical corrections $Δ_{|m|}=V^*_{|m|}-V_{|m|}$. The corrections remain systematically negative and depend strongly on both interaction strength and relative angular momentum, reflecting dynamical correlation effects associated with higher-state virtual admixture. The exact results reproduce the perturbative Landau-level-mixing limit at weak coupling while exhibiting substantial deviations in the strong-mixing regime, signaling the breakdown of low-order perturbative expansions. In particular, the short-range interaction channels relevant to Laughlin-type correlations undergo strong renormalization, leading to substantial modification of the effective interaction hierarchy in strongly interacting systems such as ZnO/MgZnO heterostructures. The present formulation establishes a microscopic framework for incorporating nonperturbative Landau-level-mixing effects into effective interaction theories of quantum Hall systems.

</details>

### [Quantum State Preparation via Neural Network Encoding in Quantum Machine Learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31006v1)

**Authors:** Kevin W. Aoun, Florian J. Kiwit, Carlos A. Riofrío, Samer Saab, Charbel Al Bateh, Joe Tekli, Andre Luckow

(c) Fashion-MNIST, FRQI ansatz. (d) Fashion-MNIST, center-sequential ansatz. Both architectures show consistent improvement with depth. F-MNIST converges to lower infidelity than MNIST at equivalent depth, though the FRQI ansatz at D ∈{6, 8} (Nparams = 468 and 624) exhibits some training instability on both datasets.</sub>

**Summary.** This work tackles the computational bottleneck of preparing quantum states from classical data by training a classical neural network to output the necessary parameters for a fixed quantum circuit. By shifting optimization offline, the method achieves state preparation with high fidelity and a massive speedup, demonstrating a scalable pathway for near-term QML applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on data loading rather than fundamental quantum dynamics, the efficiency gains and the use of classical ML to control quantum circuits are highly relevant to developing practical quantum algorithms for physical simulations or data analysis.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge addressed is the 'state preparation bottleneck' in Quantum Machine Learning (QML), which involves the prohibitive computational cost of loading high-dimensional classical data into a quantum state.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated a scalable method by training a classical neural network to map input data directly to quantum circuit parameters, achieving high fidelity (up to 0.992) on unseen images with a massive runtime speedup (over 5000-fold).

**Method.** They propose training a classical Convolutional Neural Network (CNN) encoder to generate the continuous parameters ($	heta$) for a fixed Parameterized Quantum Circuit (PQC), bypassing the need for iterative optimization for every data instance.

**Model / system.** The system involves encoding classical image data (MNIST/Fashion-MNIST) into quantum states using two types of PQCs: a Center-sequential circuit and an FRQI-native circuit. The encoding relies on amplitude encoding principles.

**Key observables.** Fidelity (Linfid), Peak Signal-to-Noise Ratio (PSNR), and Structural Similarity Index (SSIM) are used to quantify state preparation quality and image reconstruction accuracy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth ($D \in \{2, 4, 6, 8\}$), number of qubits ($n=11$ for $32 	imes 32$ images), and the number of trainable weights in the CNN encoder ($\approx 749,000$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes that the data structure allows for approximation by quantum states, and the core assumption is that offloading optimization to an offline training phase is beneficial.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the direct state preparation pipeline ($x 	o E(x) 	o 	heta 	o |\psiangle$), compare the architectural designs of the two PQCs, and show image reconstructions comparing the proposed FRQI encoding against other methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper first identifies the state preparation bottleneck, proposes the CNN-to-PQC parameter mapping, details the two ansatz architectures (Center-sequential vs. FRQI-native), and validates the method on MNIST and Fashion-MNIST using multiple reconstruction metrics and runtime comparisons.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A central challenge in quantum machine learning is the state preparation bottleneck that describes the prohibitive computational cost of loading high-dimensional classical data into a quantum state. Although amplitude encoding can represent $2^n$-dimensional data using only $n$ qubits in principle, preparing arbitrary states remains computationally expensive, typically requiring variational optimization of a parameterized quantum circuit for each individual data instance. In this work, we propose a method that avoids iterative optimization by training a classical neural network to map input data directly to the continuous parameters of a fixed quantum circuit. We demonstrate the generation of quantum image states with high fidelity on data not seen during training. Since all optimization is performed once during training, the resulting model encodes new inputs in a single inference step, providing a scalable pathway for data loading in near-term quantum algorithms. We validate our method on the MNIST and Fashion-MNIST datasets, achieving fidelities up to 0.992 on unseen images and reducing the per-data-instance runtime by more than 5000-fold.

</details>

**Authors:** Yifan Sun, Boyuan Sun, Jiameng Tian, Xiangdong Zhang

<sub>Figure 3: The learning process of a QNN vs a classical NN, in a generalized perspective of data flow. By using a QNN (the left panel), the data is encoded by quantum states (in the subspace 1), and transformed to other states (in the subspace 2). The training is done by computing the loss function of the output states, and updating the parameters of the QNN. By using a classical NN (the right panel), the data is encoded by the states of digital electrical circuit states. The size of the state space when data is processed often changes. The training is also done by computing the loss function of the output states, and updating the model parameters.</sub>

The roles of the basic types of the QNN for those learning paradigms are also illustrated, as indicated by blue arrows. The quantum operations on states, such as applying quantum algorithms or performing measurements, can be employed as a quantum version of alignment and reward feedback, as indicated by red arrows. combine into more complex architectures for these learning tasks.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30724_figures/2605.30724_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Schematic of a QTL framework. A QNN-based agent interacts with an environment by observing a state 𝑠𝑡, selecting an action 𝑎𝑡according to its policy, and receiving a reward 𝑟𝑡as the environment transitions to a new state 𝑠𝑡+1 with probability 𝑝(𝑠𝑡+1|𝑠𝑡, 𝑎𝑡). After collecting a set of transitions (𝑠𝑡, 𝑎𝑡, 𝑟𝑡, 𝑠𝑡+1), the agent is updated using a suitable optimization algorithm.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This survey provides a comprehensive review of Quantum Neural Networks (QNNs) and Quantum Machine Learning (QML). It details various QNN architectures and critically analyzes the optimization challenges, such as barren plateaus, while comparing gradient-based and gradient-free training methods. The paper maps out the current state-of-the-art for leveraging quantum computation in artificial intelligence.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a comprehensive overview of how quantum principles (superposition, entanglement) are being mapped onto computational models for ML, which is highly relevant for understanding quantum information processing paradigms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for more powerful machine learning strategies to solve complex data analysis, pattern recognition, and optimization tasks.

**Main result.** The paper surveys various Quantum Neural Network (QNN) architectures and discusses the systematic challenges in training them, such as the barren plateau problem.

**Method.** It reviews multiple QNN approaches (e.g., QCNNs, QBMs) and compares optimization techniques like Parameter-Shift Rules, Quantum Natural Gradient Descent, and gradient-free methods like SPSA.

**Model / system.** The core model is the Quantum Neural Network (QNN), which processes data encoded into quantum states (qubits) via unitary operations. These are compared against classical NNs, highlighting the Hilbert space feature mapping.

**Key observables.** Learning accuracy, training time, resource requirements, and the cost function L(theta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of qubits (p), learning rate (eta), and the structure of the unitary gates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that QNNs can be effectively trained, despite challenges like the barren plateau problem and significant classical overhead for state preparation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the timeline of QNN advancements, the structural comparison between QNNs and classical NNs, and the generalized learning process flow for both paradigms.

**Paper structure.** The review is systematic, first establishing the need for QML, then detailing various QNN types, followed by a deep dive into the mathematical and computational methods for training these quantum models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Machine learning holds fundamental computational significance due to the increasing demand for efficient solutions to complex tasks in data analysis, pattern recognition, and optimization, which are essential for addressing the multifaceted challenges of modern society. As the volume of data proliferates at an unprecedented rate, the need for more powerful machine learning strategies becomes increasingly evident. Quantum neural networks (QNNs) represent an emerging and transformative research field that seeks to harness the unique principles of quantum mechanics to enhance the capabilities of machine learning algorithms. This survey examines various QNN approaches, including fully connected QNNs, quantum convolutional neural networks, equivariant QNNs, quantum Hopfield networks, quantum Boltzmann machines, quantum reservoir computing, and composite networks for quantum reinforcement learning, quantum generative learning, and quantum transfer learning. We summarize the relevant investigations on their performance, including learning accuracy, training time, and resource requirements, etc. Each QNN type has unique strengths and weaknesses, offering diverse solutions for different applications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31582"></a>
### [Richardson-Gaudin states of non-zero seniority III: The Perfect-Pairing limit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31582v1)

**Authors:** Paul A. Johnson  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31582v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31582_figures/2605.31582_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Single and double excitations from the PP reference |ω⟩: solid edges represent</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31582_figures/2605.31582_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: (a) Symmetric bond dissociation of linear H8 in the STO-6G basis: EN2 computed</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31582_figures/2605.31582_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Symmetric bond dissociation of linear H8 in the STO-6G basis: comparison of</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>
