**Summary.** This work develops a computationally efficient method to calculate correlation energies for strongly correlated electron systems by simplifying the Richardson-Gaudin states to the Perfect-Pairing limit. By applying second-order perturbative corrections (EN2) within this simplified framework, the authors achieve accuracy comparable to expensive full CI methods. This provides a powerful, scalable tool for studying bond dissociation and electronic structure in correlated materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The systematic reduction of complexity from full CI/CASSCF to the PP limit while maintaining high accuracy is a significant algorithmic advance for treating strong correlation, providing a computationally tractable alternative for molecular simulations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the computational challenge of treating strongly correlated electrons, which are poorly described by simple single Slater determinants, especially when the active space is large.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that simplifying the Richardson-Gaudin states to the Perfect-Pairing (PP) limit allows for the calculation of high-accuracy correlation energy corrections (EN2) with substantially reduced computational cost.

**Method.** The core method involves using the PP state as a reference wavefunction and applying second-order Epstein-Nesbet (EN2) perturbative corrections, alongside analyzing excitation energies via VBS gaps and pair amplitudes.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using the Coulomb Hamiltonian, decomposed into seniority-conserving channels. The theoretical framework builds upon Richardson-Gaudin states and utilizes the Valence-Bond Subsystem (VBS) approximation to simplify the calculation.

**Key observables.** Dissociation energy curves (e.g., N2), excitation energies (singles, doubles, pair-transfers), and correlation energy corrections ($	ext{EN2}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The VBS Gap ($\omega_\alpha$), the Perfect-Pairing (PP) limit, and the seniority quantum number (focusing on non-zero seniority III).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the PP approximation yields results comparable in quality to the full Richardson-Gaudin state approach, and that the EN2 correction can be accurately calculated perturbatively.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate dissociation curves (e.g., N2) comparing PP, EN2, and EN2d corrections, and show the structure of single and double excitation channels from the PP reference state.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the need for simplified correlated methods (PP limit) to deriving the necessary mathematical machinery (VBS gaps, generalized Fock matrices) for calculating the EN2 correction, and finally applying this to molecular dissociation problems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Strongly correlated electrons can be treated with a configuration interaction of Slater determinants grouped by number of unpaired electrons with exponential cost. The first two papers in this series demonstrated that single reference methods built from Richardson-Gaudin states gave results of similar quality at polynomial cost. In this contribution, the states are simplified substantially yielding the perfect-pairing state as a reference along with its low-lying excitations. The states are much simpler, the computational cost is substantially reduced, and there is no sacrifice in numerical accuracy. Second-order Epstein-Nesbet perturbative corrections for the valence electrons are similar in quality to the complete active space self-consistent field.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.30977"></a>
### [Software Platform for Hybrid Pseudo-Random Sequence Generation and Predictability Analysis Based on LFSR and Mersenne Twister](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30977v1)

**Authors:** Ali Abdolrahimi Zarnagh, Ali Motazedifard  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30977v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30977_figures/2605.30977_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1 Structure of LFSR generation process</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30977_figures/2605.30977_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2 MT generation algorithm pseudo code</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30977_figures/2605.30977_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3 LFSR structure breaking algorithm pseudo code</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30977_figures/2605.30977_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4 Block diagram of the method used for breaking the LFSR structure</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30977_figures/2605.30977_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5 MT structure breaking algorithm pseudo code</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work introduces a software platform to systematically analyze and benchmark various random number generators, including LFSRs and Mersenne Twister. It demonstrates that these classical generators are fundamentally predictable by reconstructing their internal states using advanced statistical and machine learning techniques. The findings strongly advocate for the use of quantum random number generators due to their inherent, non-algorithmic source of true randomness.

**Why it may be interesting.** While not directly focused on quantum dynamics, the paper's rigorous comparison between deterministic algorithmic randomness and non-deterministic quantum randomness provides a strong theoretical justification for the necessity of quantum sources in high-security quantum information protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental problem of generating and analyzing the predictability of random bit sequences, comparing classical pseudo-random generators (LFSR, MT) against truly random quantum sources.

**Main result.** Classical PRNGs are shown to be deterministic and predictable using statistical and AI-based reconstruction techniques, whereas quantum random sequences offer superior, inherent unpredictability for security applications.

**Method.** The authors developed a software platform to generate, analyze, and attempt to 'hack' the internal states and parameters of LFSRs and MTs, contrasting these methods with testing quantum random number streams.

**Model / system.** The models analyzed are classical PRNGs, specifically Linear Feedback Shift Registers (LFSRs) and the Mersenne Twister (MT). The comparison benchmark is the physical process generating quantum random numbers (QRNGs).

**Key observables.** Sequence complexity, resistance to prediction, match-point rate (for prediction success), and the statistical distribution of reconstructed states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** LFSR feedback polynomial coefficients, MT state vector size (e.g., 624 states), and the required input bit length for successful state reconstruction.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The predictability of PRNGs relies on exploiting underlying algorithmic patterns, and the analysis assumes quantum randomness is fundamentally non-deterministic.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the structural diagrams of LFSR and MT generation processes, alongside pseudo-code and block diagrams detailing the specific algorithms used for structure breaking and state estimation.

**Paper structure.** The paper is structured around developing a comprehensive software platform with two modes: Generation Mode (implementing LFSR, MT, and hybrid combinations) and Hacking Mode (applying statistical, algebraic, and deep learning techniques to estimate internal states).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Generating reliable random and pseudo-random sequences is important in many electronic and signal processing systems, such as secure communications, radar, spread-spectrum methods, and autonomous platforms. Although true and quantum random number generators provide stronger unpredictability, classical pseudo-random number generators, including Linear Feedback Shift Registers (LFSRs) and the Mersenne Twister (MT), are still widely used because they are efficient and easy to implement. This work introduces a user-friendly software platform for generating, analyzing, and evaluating the predictability of pseudo-random bit sequences. The software supports two main functions: generating sequences using classical PRNGs and hybrid combinations, and analyzing input sequences through statistical measures and data-driven methods. In particular, hybrid LFSR-MT structures are studied to examine how they affect sequence complexity and resistance to prediction. The platform also includes machine-learning and deep-learning tools to investigate when deterministic PRNGs may remain partially predictable, even when their structure becomes more complex. The results show that algorithmic random sequence generators have inherent limitations in terms of unpredictability, which supports the use of quantum random sequences in security-critical applications. A comparative study between classical LFSR-MT sequences and quantum random sequences shows that quantum randomness offers higher unpredictability due to its non-deterministic physical origin. The potential use of quantum random sequences in jamming applications is also discussed, highlighting their improved robustness against prediction-based attacks. Overall, the proposed software provides a practical tool for analyzing, comparing, and benchmarking random sequence generators in modern electronic, sensing, and quantum-enabled communication systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31248"></a>
### [Trainable Quantum Spectral Models for Partial Differential Equations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31248v1)

**Authors:** Gabriel Mejia, Eileen Kuehn, Melvin Strobl, Achim Streit  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31248v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31248_figures/2605.31248_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Compact schematic of the richer spectral architecture Bϵ. Trainable diagonal spectral layers D(θ(ℓ)) are interleaved with forward and reverse entangling mixers, which introduce controlled coupling between spectral modes. The parameter β(ϵ) controls the mixer strength.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31248_figures/2605.31248_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. HHL-inspired model. In the spectral basis, each mode |k⟩controls a sequence of ancilla rotations RY (θk). Post-selection of the ancilla induces the learned spectral filtering, followed by the inverse transform Q to recover the solution state in physical coordinates.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31248_figures/2605.31248_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. Training loss evolution Ltrain = median( 1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31248_figures/2605.31248_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5. Mode-wise spectral error Ek = median(|ˆuθ,k −ˆuk|2). All spectral models, except the richer spectral model with ϵ &gt; 0.5, accurately recover dominant modes and suppress spurious high-frequency components.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.31248_figures/2605.31248_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 6. Mode-wise gradient power Gk. All spectral models, except the richer spectral model with ϵ &gt; 0.5, exhibit better sensitivity on the gradients of modes of the true solution.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using trainable Quantum Spectral Models (QSMs) to solve PDEs by learning the inverse operator in a spectral basis rather than the physical basis. By structuring the quantum circuit based on the PDE's natural basis, the models achieve faster convergence and higher fidelity compared to generic quantum circuits. This highlights that incorporating physical structure into quantum algorithms is crucial for practical scientific computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a structured, physically informed quantum ansatz for scientific computing, suggesting that incorporating prior knowledge (like spectral decomposition) into quantum algorithms can overcome limitations in raw expressibility.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing trainable quantum spectral models (QSMs) to efficiently solve linear Partial Differential Equations (PDEs) by learning the inverse differential operator in a spectral basis.

**Main result.** Operator-aware spectral representations significantly outperform generic quantum circuits, showing faster convergence and more stable gradients for solving PDEs.

**Method.** The approach involves mapping the PDE problem into a spectral basis and designing parameterized quantum circuits (QSMs) that operate on this transformed representation, rather than the physical basis.

**Model / system.** The models are tested on linear PDEs, including the Poisson and Helmholtz equations, and variable-coefficient versions. The quantum circuit learns the inverse operator $A^{-1}$ via a parameterized unitary $M_	heta$ acting in the spectral domain.

**Key observables.** Mean Squared Error (MSE), Fidelity ($F$), mode-wise spectral error ($E_k$), gradient variance ($	ext{Var}(\partial 	heta_i L)$), and convergence speed.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The mixing parameter $\epsilon$ in richer spectral models, which controls the trade-off between expressibility and trainability, with an optimal regime found around $\epsilon \approx 0.5$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The performance gains are observed even when the chosen spectral basis does not perfectly diagonalize the underlying differential operator.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the training loss and fidelity improvements of spectral models over generic circuits, show mode-wise spectral error concentration in spectral models, and compare gradient variance across different model architectures.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically introduces QSMs, compares various architectures (diagonal, richer spectral, HHL-inspired) against HEA baselines, analyzes the expressibility vs. trainability trade-off using the mixing parameter $\epsilon$, and validates performance on multiple benchmark PDEs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work studies trainable quantum spectral models (QSMs) for solving linear partial differential equations (PDEs). Instead of learning solutions directly in physical space, QSMs learn the inverse differential operator in a spectral representation, embedding prior knowledge of the equation's natural basis.   We systematically study the expressibility and trainability of several QSM architectures, ranging from near-diagonal to fully parameterized unitaries. In particular, we introduce a family of richer spectral models that interpolate between purely diagonal operators and fully mixing unitaries through a parameterized mixer controlled by $ε$. Our results reveal an intermediate regime, typically around $ε\approx 0.5$, where models achieve the best tradeoff between expressibility and trainability. Beyond this threshold, increased circuit complexity degrades convergence without improving accuracy.   Among the architectures considered, models inspired by the inverse step of the Harrow-Hassidim-Lloyd (HHL) algorithm achieve the fastest training convergence while maintaining high solution fidelity. Numerical experiments on the (variable-coefficient) Poisson and Helmholtz equations show that trainable operations in the spectral basis outperform standard variational quantum circuits acting directly in the computational basis. These advantages appear through faster convergence, more stable gradients, and more accurate recovery of the reference solution spectrum, particularly through stronger suppression of spurious high-frequency components, even when the operator is not exactly diagonal in the chosen spectral basis. Our results identify operator-aware spectral representations as a promising route toward trainable and physically grounded quantum methods for scientific computing.

</details>

### [Vertex-transitive quantum graphs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30730v1)

**Authors:** Mac Hayes, Trevor Jess, Andre Kornell, Remi Salinas Schmeis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30730v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30730_figures/2605.30730_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.30730_figures/2605.30730_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30730_figures/2605.30730_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30730_figures/2605.30730_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30730_figures/2605.30730_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously classifies vertex-transitive quantum graphs, which are subspaces of matrices in $M_n(\mathbb{C})$. The authors introduce the panoramic polynomial as a key invariant and use spectral analysis of the automorphism group to prove a complete, exhaustive list of all such graphs for the $M_3(\mathbb{C})$ case. This work provides deep structural insights into the symmetries of these quantum algebraic objects.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the subject is abstract algebra/noncommutative geometry, the rigorous classification of highly symmetric structures (like vertex-transitive graphs) using algebraic invariants is a powerful theoretical tool applicable to characterizing symmetries in quantum physical models.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to provide a complete classification of vertex-transitive quantum graphs, with a specific focus on those embedded in $M_3(\mathbb{C})$.

**Main result.** The authors provide a complete classification of all vertex-transitive quantum graphs in $M_3(\mathbb{C})$ up to isomorphism, identifying several distinct families parameterized by angles $	heta$.

**Method.** The classification relies on introducing and utilizing a polynomial invariant called the panoramic polynomial, alongside analyzing the spectrum of the adjoint action of unitaries in the automorphism group.

**Model / system.** The system studied is the quantum graph, mathematically defined as a $\dagger$-closed subspace $R$ of matrices in $M_n(\mathbb{C})$, where $n$ is the dimension of the ambient space (primarily $n=3$).

**Key observables.** The panoramic polynomial $p_R$, the automorphism group $	ext{Aut}(R)$, the dimension $	ext{dim}(R)$, and the spectrum of the adjoint action $	ext{Spec}(	ext{Ad}_R(u))$ are key observables.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $n$ (especially $n=3$) and the continuous parameter $	heta \in [0, \pi/2]$ used to parameterize certain graph families are important parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The classification heavily assumes that the dimension of the quantum graph is an isomorphism invariant, and that the graphs under consideration are regular.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4 summarizes the complete classification of vertex-transitive quantum graphs in $M_3(\mathbb{C})$ modulo isomorphism, listing 8 distinct types, each characterized by its defining elements, panoramic polynomial, and automorphism group.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general definitions of quantum graphs and vertex-transitivity, introduces the panoramic polynomial invariant, proves structural theorems relating regularity and vertex-transitivity, and culminates in the explicit, exhaustive classification of all such graphs in $M_3(\mathbb{C})$ using spectral analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We define a quantum graph to be vertex-transitive if the join of its automorphism group is the maximum quantum relation on its quantum vertex set, in direct analogy with the classical case. All simple quantum graphs in $M_2(\mathbb C)$ are vertex-transitive, but many simple quantum graphs in $M_3(\mathbb C)$ are not vertex-transitive. We provide a complete classification of vertex-transitive quantum graphs in $M_3(\mathbb C)$ up to isomorphism. To do this, we introduce a polynomial invariant for quantum graphs in $M_n(\mathbb C)$, which we call the panoramic polynomial.

</details>

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31307"></a>
### [Feasibility study of continuous electronic Pomeranchuk cooling with a flavor-degenerate Wigner crystal](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31307v1)

**Authors:** Robin J. Dolleman, Ammon Fischer, Lennart Klebl, Alexander Rothstein, Dante M. Kennes, Bernd Beschoten, Florian Libisch, Christoph Stampfer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31307v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `driven-dissipative phase transition` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5**

(b) Schematic of the locally top-gated multilayer graphene device and the simplified one-dimensional model used for the simulations. (c) Exemplary spatial distribution of nEL and nWC that is assumed for the simulations.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31307_figures/2605.31307_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Simulations to determine the minimum possible electron temperature. Fixed simulation parameters are the electrical resistivity Rs = 1 kΩ, bath temperature Tbath = 4 mK. (a) Temperature in the cold region as a function of bias current for different values of the relative Lorenz number λWC. (b) Exemplary temperature T(x) for different values of the bias current I, for the case nEL = 2.6 × 1011 cm−2, nWC = 4.88 × 1010 cm−2 and λWC = 1 × 10−6. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31307_figures/2605.31307_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Cooling power and its dependence on sheet resistance, Tbath = 4 mK and λWC = 1 × 10−6. (a) Cooling power ˙Qcool versus current for several sheet resistances Rs, the colours correspond to panel (b). Inset shows the temperature profile T(x) for currents I = 25 pA and I = 100 pA. <sub>FIG. 5. (a) Relative Lorenz number λWC versus temperature for a sample lengths L = 1 µm and L = 10 µm. (b) Contribution of the phonon branches to the entropy per particle. Inset shows the two phonon branches.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a novel method for continuous, on-chip electronic cooling by exploiting the large entropy difference between an Electron Liquid and a Wigner Crystal phase in 2D materials. By tuning the carrier density to induce this phase transition, heat is extracted from the electron system, potentially reaching temperatures below $1 	ext{ mK}$. The analysis uses advanced thermal transport simulations to model this non-equilibrium cooling cycle.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work connects fundamental concepts of phase transitions (WC formation) and entropy maximization/minimization to a practical, non-equilibrium thermodynamic process (cooling), which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and energy dissipation studies.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is achieving continuous, on-chip cooling of electron temperatures to sub-millikelvin levels in nanoelectronic devices.

**Main result.** The authors propose a cooling cycle based on the entropy difference between an Electron Liquid (EL) and a Wigner Crystal (WC) phase, demonstrating potential cooling powers up to $8.4 	ext{ aW}/\mu	ext{m}$ below $1 	ext{ mK}$.

**Method.** The cooling is modeled by simulating a 1D convection-diffusion equation that accounts for heat sources from Joule heating, entropic cooling ($\Delta S$), and phonon coupling.

**Model / system.** The system is a 2D, flavor-degenerate material (like multilayer graphene) where the charge carriers undergo a phase transition between an EL and a WC when the local gate voltage is tuned.

**Key observables.** Cooling power ($\dot{Q}_{	ext{cool}}$), minimum attainable temperature ($T_{	ext{min}}$), and the temperature profile $T(x)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low current densities ($< 1 	ext{ nA}/\mu	ext{m}$), WC density ($n_{WC}$), and the bath temperature ($T_{	ext{bath}}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The cooling mechanism relies on the entropy difference ($\Delta S = S_{WC} - S_{EL}$), and the model assumes the validity of the Wiedemann-Franz relation in the EL phase while accounting for phonon backflow in the WC phase.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the WC lattice structure, band structures for graphene variants, the entropy difference between EL and WC, and simulation results showing the temperature profile and minimum attainable temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical problem, details the EL-WC phase transition mechanism driven by entropy, derives the thermodynamic cycle using the Clausius-Clapeyron relation, and finally simulates the heat transport using a 1D convection-diffusion equation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Achieving sub-millikelvin electron temperatures in nanoelectronic devices could unveil new transport phenomena, extend quantum coherence times, and enhance the precision of quantum metrology. However, maintaining such low temperatures continuously remains a long-standing challenge. Here, we propose and simulate an on-chip cooling cycle that harnesses the entropy difference between an electron liquid (EL) and a Wigner crystal (WC) in flavor-degenerate flat-band materials. Cooling is driven by a current through a device with a locally gated region. Within this region, the charge carrier density is tuned such that a WC forms beneath the gate. As carriers transition from an EL to WC phase, their entropy increases, extracting heat and the sliding WC advects this heat along the device. The heat is then released when carriers transition back to the EL phase, which establishes distinct hot and cold regions and a steady temperature gradient over the device. Simulations show net cooling for sufficiently low current densities, typically below $1~\mathrm{nA}/μ\mathrm{m}$, whereas Joule heating dominates at higher currents. Within the gated region, we estimate cooling powers of up to $8.4~\mathrm{aW}/μ\mathrm{m}$ at a bath temperature of $4~\mathrm{mK}$. Our approach can achieve electron temperatures well below $1~\mathrm{mK}$ under suitable conditions, promising a route towards continuous on-chip cooling in this temperature regime. Our approach applies to any flat-band material with low-energy flavor degeneracy (valley and/or orbital) and low disorder, including gapped Bernal-stacked bilayer graphene, rhombohedral-stacked multilayer graphene, and magic-angle twisted bilayer graphene.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31411"></a>
### [Odd-Parity Magnons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31411v1)

**Authors:** Pu Zhang, Sun-Bo Xie, Junxi Yu, Yichen Liu, Cheng-Cheng Liu

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31411_figures/2605.31411_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Light-induced topological magnon phase transition and magnon thermal Hall effect. (a) Schematic of the bilayer A-type antiferromagnetic lattice structure featuring spatial asymmetry S1 ̸= S2. (b) f-wave magnons under circularly polarized light driving with λ = 0.4 and ℏΩ/J = 1. (c) Topological phase diagram in the plane of the optical driv- ing frequency Ωand the dimensionless coupling strength λ, illustrating the system’s evolution from a trivial phase C = 0 to a topological magnon phase C = 2. The inset shows the corresponding chiral edge states. (d) Evolution of the ther- mal Hall conductivity κxy (blue dots, left axis) with J=1 meV and T=50 K and the Chern number (red line,...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study establishes a general framework for odd-parity magnon splitting in antiferromagnets by classifying symmetry constraints. By applying Floquet engineering using light fields, the authors show how to induce tunable $p$- and $f$-wave magnon splittings and drive the system into a topological magnon phase. This work provides a foundation for designing ultrafast, optically controlled spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics and open quantum systems because it uses time-periodic driving (Floquet engineering) to induce and control topological quantum states (topological magnon phases) in magnetic materials, offering a pathway for optically controlled quantum devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the theoretical challenge of realizing and classifying odd-parity magnon band splitting in collinear antiferromagnets, a phenomenon forbidden by effective time-reversal symmetry.

**Main result.** The authors provide a complete spin-point-group classification for this splitting and demonstrate that breaking time-reversal symmetry (e.g., via circularly polarized light) induces tunable $p$- and $f$-wave magnon splitting, leading to topological magnon phases.

**Method.** The study employs symmetry analysis (spin-point-group classification), Floquet theory for time-periodic driving, and continuum effective Hamiltonian modeling derived from the Holstein-Primakoff transformation.

**Model / system.** The research focuses on 2D collinear antiferromagnets, specifically modeling bilayer systems like $	ext{MnPS}_3$, $	ext{FeBr}_3$, and $	ext{CrI}_3$. The physics is governed by the interplay between exchange interactions and light-induced effective Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interactions.

**Key observables.** Odd-parity magnon splitting ($\Delta\omega(k)$), magnon thermal Hall conductivity ($\kappa_{xy}$), and the total Chern number ($C_{	ext{total}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The transition occurs at a critical light-field coupling strength ($\lambda \approx 0.3$), and the splitting patterns are classified as $p$-wave or $f$-wave.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on truncating bulk structures to model the bilayer limit and assumes the effective time-reversal symmetry can be broken by external fields like CPL or loop currents.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic mechanism of symmetry breaking, the resulting magnon dispersion curves showing $p$-wave or $f$-wave splitting patterns, and the topological phase transition marked by the Chern number change.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general symmetry constraints for odd-parity splitting (classification) to applying Floquet theory to specific lattice models (honeycomb/bilayer) to calculate the effective Hamiltonian and predict the topological phase transition via $\kappa_{xy}$ jumps.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Magnons, as charge-neutral spin excitations, can transport spin information without Joule heating and therefore offer a promising platform for low-power spintronics. However, in collinear magnets, the effective time-reversal symmetry forbids odd-parity magnon band splitting. Here we propose odd-parity magnons and establish a general mechanism for realizing them in collinear antiferromagnets. We provide a complete spin-point-group classification of odd-parity magnon splitting in two-dimensional collinear antiferromagnets by identifying the leading splitting types and their symmetry-allowed basis functions. This classification serves as a practical guide for searching for odd-parity magnons. We show that breaking effective time-reversal symmetry, for example by circularly polarized light or loop currents, can induce highly tunable $p$- and $f$-wave magnon splitting. In bilayer systems, the dynamical modulation can drive a topological magnon phase transition, accompanied by chiral edge modes and an abrupt jump in the magnon thermal Hall conductivity. Material-specific first-principles calculations further demonstrate the feasibility of this mechanism in real van der Waals antiferromagnets. Our study identifies the odd-parity magnons as a new class of spin excitations and provides a theoretical foundation for odd-parity magnons and ultrafast optically controlled topological magnonic devices.

</details>

### [Ab Initio Spinor Kadanoff-Baym Approach to Nonequilibrium Electron, Phonon and Magnon Dynamics in Itinerant Ferromagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31467v1)

**Authors:** Giovanni Marini

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This work introduces an advanced theoretical framework using Kadanoff-Baym equations to model how magnetization changes ultrafast in itinerant ferromagnets. By treating electrons, phonons, and magnons simultaneously, it provides a first-principles method to understand demagnetization. This is crucial for developing predictive theories of femtomagnetism.

**Why it may be interesting.** The treatment of coupled, non-equilibrium dynamics involving multiple bosonic and fermionic excitations (magnons, phonons) within a Green's function framework is highly relevant to open quantum systems and non-equilibrium statistical mechanics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop a first-principles theoretical framework to study ultrafast magnetization dynamics in itinerant ferromagnetic systems.

**Main result.** It derives a tractable set of coupled equations for electron, phonon, and magnon dynamics, allowing for a unified description of coherent and incoherent magnetic dynamics.

**Method.** It employs the Kadanoff-Baym equations in spinor space, utilizing many-body perturbation theory and the Markov approximation.

**Model / system.** The system is itinerant ferromagnets, modeled using an ab initio relativistic Hamiltonian that includes spin-orbit coupling (SOC). The dynamics are treated via spinor Green's functions.

**Key observables.** Ultrafast magnetization dynamics, demagnetization, electron density matrix, and phonon density matrix.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin-orbit coupling strength, time scales (ultrafast/femtosecond), and coupling strengths between electron, phonon, and magnon sectors.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of the Markov approximation and various approximations (like time-locality for the screened interaction W) are necessary to close the coupled equations of motion.

**Figures summary.** Not specified in the notes, but the text references diagrammatic representations of the transverse particle-hole T matrix and the resulting effective electron-boson coupling.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the general Kadanoff-Baym formalism in spinor space, incorporates SOC effects, derives equations for electron and phonon dynamics, and finally formulates the coupled equations including electron-magnon interactions via the transverse T matrix.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work introduces a theoretical framework based on the Kadanoff-Baym equations in spinor space to study ultrafast magnetization dynamics in itinerant ferromagnetic systems from first principles. By incorporating spin-orbit coupling into the ab initio Hamiltonian and generalizing the self-energies to include terms beyond the charge sector, I derive scattering integrals within the Markov approximation and quasiparticle renormalizations for electrons and phonons in the presence of spin-dependent effective interactions within a many-body perturbation theory approach. I explicitly discuss how magnons emerge in this framework, and derive, through suitable approximations, a close and tractable set of equations for coupled electron,phonon and magnon dynamics that can be solved from first principles. This approach allows to treat coherent and incoherent magnetic dynamics on an equal footing, paving the way for a first principles understanding of ultrafast magnetic dynamics and demagnetization, and opening to a truly predictive theory of femtomagnetism in periodic systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study proposes an altermagnetic photovoltaic battery to generate a pure spin photocurrent using light. By simulating a $	ext{V}_2	ext{Te}_2	ext{O}/	ext{ZnSe}/	ext{V}_2	ext{Te}_2	ext{O}$ junction, the authors show that an antiparallel alignment of the altermagnetic electrodes breaks inversion symmetry, enabling a pure spin current without a net charge current. This establishes a promising, light-driven route for pure spin-current generation in spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the primary focus is condensed matter spintronics, the use of quantum transport simulations (DFT+NEGF) to model light-matter interaction and spin currents is relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the generation of a pure spin photocurrent using light in an altermagnetic photovoltaic battery, aiming for a light-driven pure spin-current generation mechanism.

**Main result.** The device successfully generates a pure spin photocurrent when the two altermagnetic electrodes are in an antiparallel (AP) Néel configuration, characterized by spin-up and spin-down photocurrents of equal magnitude but opposite sign.

**Method.** The study employs first-principles quantum-transport simulations, combining Density-Functional Theory (DFT) with the Nonequilibrium Green's Function (DFT+NEGF) method.

**Model / system.** The physical system is an altermagnetic spin photovoltaic battery structured as $	ext{V}_2	ext{Te}_2	ext{O}/	ext{ZnSe}/	ext{V}_2	ext{Te}_2	ext{O}$. Altermagnets are used as electrodes because they exhibit momentum-dependent spin splitting without net magnetization.

**Key observables.** Pure spin photocurrent ($	ext{I}_{\uparrow} = -	ext{I}_{\downarrow}$), photoresponse ($R$), and the dependence of the photocurrent on light polarization (linear vs. circular) and photon energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The effect is maximized in the AP state and is tunable by photon energy and polarization angle. The simulation uses a $	ext{V}_2	ext{Te}_2	ext{O}$ platform.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mechanism relies on the spin-selective photocurrent extraction imposed by the altermagnetic electrodes, and the central $	ext{ZnSe}$ region is assumed to be photoactive.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the device schematic, the operating mechanism for parallel (P) and antiparallel (AP) Néel vectors, and show the spin-resolved photocurrent response as a function of photon energy and light polarization.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical framework by detailing the physical system and the simulation methods (DFT+NEGF). It then presents the core results, contrasting the zero photocurrent in the P state with the finite pure spin photocurrent in the AP state, demonstrating tunability with light polarization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnets, featuring momentum-dependent spin splitting without net magnetization, provide a promising platform for spintronic functionalities beyond conventional ferromagnets and antiferromagnets. Here, we propose an altermagnetic spin photovoltaic battery consisting of a nonmagnetic semiconducting layer sandwiched between two altermagnetic electrodes. Using first-principles quantum-transport simulations, we show that a V2Te2O/ZnSe/V2Te2O junction supports a pure spin photocurrent for opposite Néel vectors in the two altermagnetic electrodes, with spin-up and spin-down photocurrents equal in magnitude and opposite in sign. The effect persists under both linearly and circularly polarized light and remains tunable with photon energy and polarization angle. Our results establish a realistic route toward light-driven pure spin-current generation in altermagnetic junctions.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work models how femtosecond laser pulses generate non-equilibrium spin and orbital magnetism in 2D semiconductors like TMDs. It shows that orbital and spin responses are governed by distinct physical mechanisms, leading to different temporal behaviors. The findings highlight the crucial role of orbital contributions in future ultrafast spintronic and magnetoelectronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit separation and comparison of orbital and spin dynamics, particularly the role of direct electric field coupling versus SOC mediation, provides a rich platform for understanding ultrafast quantum control in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the ultrafast generation and distinct dynamics of spin and orbital magnetism in two-dimensional (2D) semiconductors using femtosecond optical pulses.

**Main result.** The paper demonstrates that orbital and spin magnetic moments evolve via fundamentally different light-matter coupling mechanisms, allowing for selective control and distinct temporal dynamics.

**Method.** The dynamics are modeled using a time-dependent density-matrix formalism solved numerically, incorporating electron-hole dephasing effects.

**Model / system.** The system is a 2D gapped Dirac semiconductor, representative of TMDs, modeled by a tight-binding Hamiltonian incorporating spin-orbit coupling. The interaction is driven by circularly polarized femtosecond laser pulses.

**Key observables.** Time evolution of spin ($\mathbf{m}_{	ext{spin}}$) and orbital ($\mathbf{m}_{	ext{orb}}$) magnetization, and the dependence of remnant magnetization on photon energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Laser pulse duration (100 fs), peak intensity ($10^{11} 	ext{ W/cm}^2$), and specific photon energies relative to the band gap ($\Delta_g$) and spin-flip gap ($\Delta_{sf}g$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the minimal coupling in the dipole approximation and includes decoherence effects ($T_2$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the projected components of orbital and spin magnetic moments, the time evolution of magnetization under different excitation conditions, and the connection between remnant magnetism and valley-polarized conduction band populations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the generation of magnetization using time-dependent density matrix equations, comparing resonant vs. multiphoton excitation regimes, and contrasting the distinct dynamics of orbital (Rabi-like, fast) versus spin (gradual, SOC-mediated) responses.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We theoretically investigate the ultrafast generation of spin and orbital magnetism in a two-dimensional gapped Dirac system with spin-orbit coupling. This system is representative of two-dimensional hexagonal semiconductors, such as transition-metal dichalcogenides that exhibit valley-selective optical selection rules arising from the valley-contrasting magnetic texture of their band structure. Using a time-dependent density-matrix formalism, we demonstrate that circularly polarized laser pulses generate nonequilibrium magnetization under both resonant and multiphoton resonant conditions. We show that the induced spin and orbital magnetic moments can be distinctly controlled via the photon energy and polarization of the driving field. Furthermore, spin and orbital dynamics originate from fundamentally different light-matter coupling mechanisms, leading to qualitatively dissimilar temporal behaviors. The orbital magnetic moment couples directly to the external electric field, resulting in faster dynamics and pronounced Rabi-like oscillations, whereas the spin response develops gradually through spin-orbit coupling. Consequently, orbital dynamics is significantly more sensitive to electron-hole dephasing than the spin response. Our results highlight the importance of properly accounting for orbital contributions in future technologies that utilize femtosecond control of magnetism.

</details>

### [Observation and Control of the Magnetic Photogalvanic Effect from Strongly Bound Excitons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30575v1)

**Authors:** Xianfan Nie, Tarun Patel, Hyunggeun Lee, Chuangtang Wang, Meixin Cheng, Mingrui Lai, Bowen Yang, Matteo Pennacchietti, Shiyu Liu, Hechang Lei, Liuyan Zhao, Michael E. Reimer, Su Ying Quek, Adam W. Tsen  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30575v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper reports the observation and control of the magnetic photogalvanic effect in bilayer CrI3, a 2D magnetic material. The researchers demonstrated that this nonlinear photocurrent is strongly enhanced and controlled by the resonant excitation of strongly bound excitons. The findings highlight the potential of these 2D magnets for next-generation, tunable optoelectronic detectors.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides experimental evidence of a nonlinear optical effect (photogalvanic) whose mechanism is tied to many-body excitonic physics and magnetic symmetry breaking, which is highly relevant for understanding light-matter interaction in quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to observe and control the magnetic photogalvanic effect, particularly demonstrating its origin from strongly bound excitons in magnetic materials.

**Main result.** A sizable magnetic photogalvanic effect was demonstrated in bilayer CrI3, showing a clear signature dependent on light handedness and the magnetic state, which is unique to the bilayer structure.

**Method.** The research employed photocurrent measurements, varying bias, and using circularly/linearly polarized light, alongside TMR and RMCD measurements.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is a bilayer CrI3 tunnel junction, which acts as a magnetic tunnel barrier. The effect is analyzed within a many-body framework considering strongly bound Wannier-type excitons.

**Key observables.** Photocurrent ($	ext{I}_{	ext{ph}}$), photocurrent MCD ($\Delta 	ext{I}_{	ext{ph}}$), and the dependence of these signals on light polarization angle ($	heta$) and magnetic field ($B$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exciton binding energies (e.g., 1.1 eV, 0.8 eV), magnetic field switching, and electric field tuning of interlayer symmetry.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the photogalvanic response is strongly linked to the excitonic resonances and that the effect is suppressed when inversion symmetry is restored (e.g., in trilayer CrI3).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the symmetry properties of the magnetic states, the device geometry, exciton peaks via reflectance, and the dependence of photocurrent on bias and polarization angle.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically investigates the magnetic photogalvanic effect by first establishing the theoretical basis, then demonstrating the effect in bilayer CrI3 using polarization and field control, and finally confirming the exciton origin and layer dependence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Photogalvanic effects arising from the quantum geometry of noncentrosymmetric materials are promising for next-generation light-harvesting devices that do not require a built-in electric field. Recent theories predict photogalvanic currents generated in magnetic systems with spin-dependent symmetry breaking as well as by bound exciton states, allowing for potential magnetic field control of the photoresponse and enhanced detection of deep sub-gap signals, respectively. We demonstrate the magnetic photogalvanic effect in a bilayer CrI3 tunnel junction with both magnetic field switching and electric field tuning of interlayer symmetry. By controlling for the polarization and energy of light illumination, we disentangle the shift and injection current contributions and find that the peak response occurs under resonant excitation of strongly bound excitons in CrI3. Our results can be captured within a many-body framework of the photogalvanic effect, while our devices function as tunable, multispectral helicity- and polarization-sensitive detectors that highlight the potential of 2D magnets for future optoelectronic applications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30764"></a>
### [Crystal Dislocations as Atomic Scale Ratchets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30764v1)

**Authors:** Wu-Rong Jian, Yifan Wang, Wei Cai  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30764v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work demonstrates that jogged dislocations in FCC crystals act as atomic-scale ratchets, exhibiting asymmetric mobility when subjected to cyclic stress. Using MD simulations, the authors pinpoint the physical origin of this asymmetry to a unique coupling between atomic displacement and the jog's strain field. This finding has major implications for understanding fatigue and plastic deformation mechanisms.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on solid-state mechanics, the concept of an intrinsic, geometry-rooted ratchet mechanism driven by non-linear coupling ($\mathbf{a}$ and $\gamma^*$) provides an analogy for engineered quantum ratchets or directed transport in mesoscopic quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to identify and characterize intrinsic, atomic-scale mechanisms that break symmetry to produce a rectified (asymmetric) response to external cyclic stimuli.

**Main result.** Jogged dislocations in FCC crystals exhibit significant mobility asymmetry under opposite applied stresses, which is attributed to an unconventional coupling between atomic displacement and the jog's eigenstrain.

**Method.** The study employs Molecular Dynamics (MD) simulations and Nudged Elastic Band (NEB) calculations to model dislocation motion and calculate energy barriers.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is the jogged edge dislocation within face-centered cubic (FCC) nickel. The analysis focuses on the local atomic structure at the jog, which acts as an intrinsic 'lattice ratchet.'

**Key observables.** Dislocation velocity ($v$) vs. applied stress ($	au$), net directional drift under cyclic loading, and the coupling between the atomic displacement vector ($\mathbf{a}$) and the eigenstrain ($\gamma^*$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Applied shear stress ($	au$), crystal structure (FCC), and the specific geometry of the jog.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Conventional micromechanics models assume a symmetric response to stress, which the work challenges. The asymmetry is found to be general for jogged dislocations in FCC crystals.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the asymmetry in velocity vs. stress curves (Fig. 1a, S4-S6), net directional drift under cyclic loading (Fig. 1b, S8), and the atomic configurations detailing the force balance during forward versus backward jog movement (Fig. 2, 3).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general problem of symmetry breaking in materials, to simulating the asymmetric mobility using MD, and finally deriving the microscopic physical origin of this asymmetry through force analysis (NEB calculations).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The symmetry of a system's response to external stimuli is a fundamental concept in physics and materials science. At the microscopic scale, breaking this symmetry to achieve a rectified response is exceptionally difficult to engineer and remains rare in nature. Conventional micromechanics models of crystalline solids assume a symmetric response to applied stress, where reversing the load simply inverts the direction of defect velocity without altering its magnitude. In this work, we report an atomic-scale, geometry-rooted mechanism that breaks this symmetry. Molecular dynamics simulations of face-centered cubic nickel reveal that dislocations containing atomic-scale jogs exhibit asymmetric mobility under opposite applied stresses, reversing the loading direction triggers significantly higher drag. This asymmetry arises from an unconventional coupling between an atomic displacement vector and the second-order tensorial eigenstrain of the jog motion mechanism. Because jogs are ubiquitous structures in plastic deformation, this discovery challenges classical descriptions of plastic deformation mechanisms, with direct implications to cyclic creep, and opens new pathways for defect engineering to enhance fatigue resistance.

</details>

### [Surface lone-pair polarization probed by quantum-geometric transport in tellurium](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31197v1)

**Summary.** This work establishes that surface lone-pair polarization in materials like tellurium can be probed using quantum-geometric transport measurements. By analyzing nonlinear electrical responses, the authors successfully map measurable quantities to the first and second moments of the surface polarization field. This provides a powerful, microscopic route for engineering polarization-driven electronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** The methodology provides a concrete, measurable link between a subtle, symmetry-breaking microscopic property (lone-pair polarization) and macroscopic, nonlinear electronic transport phenomena, which is valuable for designing quantum-geometric devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop a microscopic understanding of how surface lone-pair polarization in crystals, like tellurium, influences electronic transport properties.

**Main result.** Quantum-geometric transport theory successfully links measurable nonlinear electrical responses (second and third harmonics) to the first and second moments of the surface polarization field.

**Method.** The authors combine first-principles calculations (DFT) with quantum-geometry transport theory, deriving effective Hamiltonians and analyzing nonlinear current expansions.

**Model / system.** The system is trigonal tellurium, where the surface lone-pair polarization is modeled as an inversion-odd dipolar component of the crystal potential, leading to quantum-geometric corrections to Bloch wavepacket dynamics.

**Key observables.** Linear and nonlinear transport coefficients ($\delta\sigma_{	ext{geo}}^{xx}, \chi_{xxx}$), DC rectification voltage ($V_{	ext{DC}}$), and temperature/thickness scaling of harmonic responses.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Surface lone-pair polarization field ($E_0$), temperature ($T$), flake thickness ($d$), and gate voltage ($\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on perturbative expansions in electric fields and current densities, and the use of a coarse-grained lattice model for polarization thermodynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figures show experimental data confirming angular dependencies ($\sin^2	heta, \sin^3	heta$) for harmonic responses, and theoretical plots mapping polarization moments ($\langle P_x angle_T, \langle P_x^2 angle_T$) against temperature or thickness.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical origin of surface polarization, deriving the quantum-geometric transport formalism, linking transport coefficients to polarization moments, and finally demonstrating the predictive power of this framework using harmonic response measurements.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stereochemically active lone pairs are ubiquitous microscopic sources of polarity in molecules and solids, but their collective behavior in crystals is often hidden by symmetry or confined to surfaces. Here we show that quantum-geometry transport provides a sensitive probe of surface lone-pair polarization in trigonal tellurium. This surface polarization appears microscopically as an inversion-odd dipolar component of the crystal potential, which shifts the center of mass of Bloch wavepackets and produces quantum-geometric corrections to their velocity. We describe this lone-pair polar texture through a minimal three-component lattice model, and we show that the resulting linear and nonlinear transport coefficients probe, respectively, the second and first moments of the net polarization field. Because rectified voltages in tellurium flakes are directly proportional to the surface lone-pair polarization, our results provide a microscopic route to understanding and engineering polarization-driven, quantum-geometric electronic devices based on tellurium allotropes.

</details>

### [Nonlinear Schrodinger Equations for Dense Bose Fluid and He4 Film at Low Temperatures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30704v1)

**Authors:** Vladimir I. Kruglov

**Summary.** This paper develops generalized nonlinear Schrödinger equations to describe the quantum dynamics of Bose fluids, including dense Bose gases and superfluid Helium-4. By linearizing these equations, the authors successfully derive the characteristic phonon-roton dispersion relation for superfluid films. The work provides a comprehensive theoretical framework connecting mean-field theory to observable excitation spectra in quantum fluids.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it applies advanced mean-field quantum field theory techniques (NLSE) to model superfluid dynamics, which is a cornerstone topic in AMO physics and quantum gases research.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper derives and applies generalized nonlinear Schrödinger equations (NLSE) to model the dynamics of Bose fluids, specifically dense Bose gas and superfluid Helium-4 films at low temperatures.

**Main result.** The analysis yields the phonon-roton dispersion relation for superfluid He4 films and provides generalized NLSE forms applicable to both dilute and dense Bose fluids.

**Method.** The methodology involves using the Hartree-Fock time-dependent mean-field theory, linearizing the resulting NLSE to study elementary excitations, and deriving coupled hydrodynamic equations for the two-fluid model.

**Model / system.** The system under study includes dilute Bose gases, dense Bose fluids (like liquid He4), and superfluid He4 films. The theoretical framework relies on deriving and analyzing nonlinear Schrödinger equations derived from many-body Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** Phonon-roton dispersion relation, superfluid velocity corrections, and the energy spectrum of elementary excitations $\epsilon(p)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Phonon velocity ($c$), roton minimum energy ($\Delta$), and the coupling parameters ($F_n$ or $\lambda_n$) derived from these physical constants.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Approximations include neglecting higher-order gradient terms in the dilute gas limit and assuming the validity of the mean-field approximation for the Bose fluid.

**Figures summary.** The notes detail the derivation of dispersion relations $\epsilon(p)$ and the final form of the energy spectrum $E(k)$ as a polynomial in $k^2$, relating it to the coefficients $G, eta, \sigma$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from deriving the generalized NLSE for dilute Bose gas, extending it to dense Bose fluids, then analyzing the two-fluid hydrodynamics for superfluid He4, and finally focusing on the specific NLSE and dispersion relation for superfluid He4 films.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We have derived the nonlinear Schrodinger equation generalizing the Gross-Pitaevskii (GP) equa tion for dilute Bose gas. The derivation is based on the Hartree-Fock time-dependent mean-field theory with an arbitrary intermolecular interaction potential. It is shown that obtained nonlinear Schrodinger equation with appropriate redefinition of coefficients can be used for description of dense Bose fluid at low temperatures. We also present the Schrodinger type equation describing the superfluid component of helium in two fluid hydrodynamics. This approach leads to quantum correction for superfluid component of velocity in two fluid hydrodynamics. We also have derived the nonlinear Schrodinger equation for superfluid He4 film at low temperatures. It is shown that this Schrodinger type equation for superfluid He4 film leads to phonon-roton dispersion relation for elementary excitation at low temperatures.

</details>

**Summary.** This work characterizes and optimizes the plasmonic properties of Au-film on nanosphere (AuFON) systems using a combination of advanced optical measurements and 3D electromagnetic simulations. By systematically varying parameters like nanosphere size and light polarization, the authors demonstrate precise control over the LSPR resonance energy. This tunability makes the AuFON platform highly promising for developing sensitive optical sensors for molecular detection.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical electromagnetism, the strong field enhancement and resonance phenomena are foundational to understanding light-matter interaction in nanoscale systems, which has parallels in cavity QED and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to optimize and precisely tune the Localised Surface Plasmon Resonance (LSPR) in Gold-film on nanosphere (AuFON) systems to enhance optical signal amplification for molecular detection.

**Main result.** The LSPR resonance energy is highly dependent on the nanosphere diameter and polarization, with the LSPR2 mode showing the highest electric field intensification capacity for sensing applications.

**Method.** The study combines experimental characterization using variable-angle spectroscopic ellipsometry with detailed numerical simulations solving Maxwell's equations in 3D using the finite element method.

**Model / system.** The physical system is Au-film deposited on polystyrene nanosphere arrays (AuFON). The analysis involves modeling the interaction of incident light (varying in angle, polarization, and wavelength) with the plasmonic nanostructure.

**Key observables.** Resonance energies of LSPR modes (LSPR1 and LSPR2), spectral reflectivity response, and the spatial distribution/amplitude of the electric field.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nanosphere diameter (500-900 nm), metal film thickness ($\approx 140$ nm), angle of incidence ($	heta$), and incident light polarization (TM/TE).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The simulation relies on the dielectric function provided by Johnson and Christy, and the analysis assumes good ordering of the nanosphere array.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the spectral response (reflectivity) for varying diameters and polarizations, and show the dependence of resonance energy on the azimuthal angle ($\phi$) and the angle of incidence ($	heta$).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically characterizes the LSPR modes by varying key parameters (diameter, angle, polarization) through both simulation and experiment, culminating in an optimized understanding of the system's tunability for sensing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Metal film on nanosphere (MFON) plasmonic systems have emerged as nanostructures with useful properties for molecular detection. This work presents the optimisation and discussion of the characterisation results of Au-film on nanosphere (AuFON) systems. Through experiments and numerical simulations, the resonance energies of localised surface plasmon modes and the spatial distribution of light on the nanostructured surface were identified. The results highlight the dependence of these modes on changes in nanostructure dimensions and the optical conditions of the incident radiation, thereby optimising the amplification of optical signals for applications in molecular detection.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31501"></a>
### [Kohn-Luttinger Superconductivity of Weyl Fermi Arcs in PtBi$_2$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31501v1)

**Authors:** Reuel Dsouza, Nikolaos Parthenios, Brian M. Andersen, Morten H. Christensen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31501v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31501_figures/2605.31501_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Phenomenological model. (a) Phase diagram of the 12-patch model assuming g0◦&gt; 0. To visualize the parameter space we additionally set w1 60◦= w2 60◦. The re- gion marked ’No solution’ is characterized by the absence of a positive eigenvalue in the 12-patch model. (b) Electronic interactions gθ and wθ of the 12-patch model. These corre- spond to intra- and inter-chirality scattering processes with fermionic indistinguishability taken into account.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31501_figures/2605.31501_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Electronic structure of minimal model. (a) Top view of the bulk Brillouin zone (BZ) with 12 Weyl points. Cir- cles/crosses denote Weyl points above/below the kz = 0 plane with blue being positive chirality and red negative chirality. (b) Fermi arcs at E = 0 on the surface BZ with the projected Weyl points colored green and brown. (c) Band structure for the slab geometry along the Γ −M −K −Γ path.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31501_figures/2605.31501_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Kohn-Luttinger phase diagram and supercon- ducting order parameters. (a) Symmetry of the supercon- ducting order parameter as a function of U, J/U and µ, the chemical potential relative to the Weyl point. Near the Weyl point, the i-wave state is dominant, while the nodal s-wave state becomes increasingly favored as the Fermi level moves away from the Weyl point. Superconducting order parame- ters appearing in the phase diagram: (b) s-wave, (c) nodal s-wave, and (d) i-wave.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work calculates the superconducting instability in the Weyl semimetal PtBi2 using the Kohn-Luttinger mechanism. By analyzing the pairing interaction on the topological surface Fermi arcs, the authors predict that the superconducting state is most likely an i-wave symmetry. This provides a microscopic mechanism for observing topological superconductivity in such materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter superconductivity, the use of symmetry analysis (noncentrosymmetry, topological features) and pairing mechanisms driven by repulsive interactions connects to general many-body physics concepts relevant to open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the nature and symmetry of unconventional superconductivity arising from electron-mediated pairing interactions in Weyl semimetals.

**Main result.** The Kohn-Luttinger approach predicts a dominant nodal i-wave superconducting state in a large region of the phase diagram for PtBi2, suggesting a mechanism for topological superconductivity on the surface.

**Method.** The authors use the Kohn-Luttinger approach, calculating the effective pairing interaction by dressing the bare Coulomb repulsion and solving the linearized gap equation projected onto the Weyl Fermi arcs.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the noncentrosymmetric Weyl semimetal PtBi2. The model employs a phenomenological patch model derived from the surface Weyl Fermi arcs, built upon a tight-binding Hamiltonian describing the bulk electronic structure.

**Key observables.** Superconducting order parameter symmetry (i-wave, s-wave), the superconducting gap structure, and the phase diagram dependence on interaction parameters.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bare repulsive interaction strength (U), chemical potential ($\mu$), and various hopping/coupling parameters ($\alpha, \gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The superconductivity instability is assumed to be driven purely by Coulomb repulsion, and the model simplifies the complex surface physics into a patch approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the phase diagram showing regions for different superconducting symmetries (s-wave, i-wave) and schematic representations of the electronic interactions governing the pairing.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the electronic structure of the Weyl semimetal using a tight-binding model, then applies the Kohn-Luttinger formalism to derive the effective pairing interaction, culminating in a phase diagram analysis of the superconducting symmetry.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recent experimental observations in the noncentrosymmetric Weyl semimetal PtBi$_2$ indicate unconventional superconductivity hosted by topological surface states -- Weyl Fermi arcs -- with a node at the center of each arc. Focusing on these Fermi arcs, we calculate the electronically mediated pairing interaction using a Kohn-Luttinger approach and find that, in a large region of the phase diagram, the leading superconducting instability has an $i$-wave symmetry featuring precisely such an intra-arc node. We study the dependence of the leading superconducting instabilities on electronic interaction parameters and chemical potential and show that the $i$-wave state is robust to changes in the model parameters. Our results provide a possible mechanism for the observation of topological $i$-wave superconductivity on the surface of PtBi$_2$ and may have implications for the broader landscape of superconducting instabilities arising from repulsive interactions on the surfaces of Weyl semimetals.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30773"></a>
### [Saturated and Anisotropic Magnetostriction in an Altermagnet](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30773v1)

**Authors:** Zhiyuan Duan, Qiyun Xu, Peixin Qin, Li Liu, Guojian Zhao, Yuzhou He, Xiaoyang Tan, Sixu Jiang, Jingyu Li, Xiaoning Wang, Qinghua Zhang, Wenhui Duan, Yong Xu, Ziang Meng, Peizhe Tang, Chengbao Jiang, Zhiqi Liu  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30773v1>

**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Figure 4b shows the magnetostriction curves measured at 50 K, allowing a direct comparison</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work reports the discovery of a saturated and anisotropic magnetostriction effect in the altermagnet MnTe. By combining detailed magnetometry and strain measurements with first-principles calculations, the authors demonstrate that this effect arises from a symmetry-allowed coupling between strain and the Néel order parameter. This challenges traditional understanding of AFM magnetostriction and highlights altermagnets as promising materials for magnetostrictive applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter magnetism, the study's rigorous coupling of electronic structure (DFT) to macroscopic mechanical response (magnetostriction) provides a model for understanding coupled order parameters, which has parallels in quantum materials research.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the magnetostriction, a coupling between magnetism and mechanics, specifically in antiferromagnets (AFMs) and the emerging class of altermagnets.

**Main result.** The authors report observing a saturated and highly anisotropic magnetostriction in the altermagnet MnTe, which is theoretically linked to symmetry-allowed coupling between elastic strain and the Néel order parameter.

**Method.** The research combines extensive experimental measurements (magnetostriction, magnetization, resistivity) with first-principles density functional theory (DFT) calculations and Landau theory analysis.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the MnTe single crystal, which exhibits altermagnetic properties. The theoretical framework involves modeling the total free energy, including lattice and magnetoelastic terms, based on the material's crystal symmetry ($D_{6h}$).

**Key observables.** Saturated magnetostriction ($\lambda_S$), two-fold symmetry anisotropy in the (0001) plane, Néel transition temperature ($\sim 307$ K), and the spin-flop field ($\sim 0.7$ T).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Elastic coefficients ($c_{11}, c_{12}$), coupling strengths ($\lambda_{1N0}^2, \lambda_{2N0}^2$), and the saturation field ($\sim 0.7$ T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the saturated magnetostriction is dominated by the spin-flop-related spin reorientation and that the magnetoelastic coupling can be described by symmetry-allowed terms.

**Figures summary.** Figures detail crystal growth, structural characterization (XRD, STEM), magnetic transitions (M vs T, M vs H), and most critically, the angular dependence of the saturated magnetostriction via pole figures.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of experimental characterization (synthesis, structure, magnetic transitions) leading to the core measurement of magnetostriction, which is then theoretically explained using DFT and Landau theory to explain the observed saturation and anisotropy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Magnetostriction, a fundamental phenomenon bridging magnetism and mechanics, has enabled a broad spectrum of applications. For almost two centuries, it has been mainly investigated for ferromagnets. Regarding the magnetostriction of antiferromagnets (AFMs), limitedly known examples for both conventional collinear AFMs and noncollinear AFMs predominantly exhibit non-saturating magnetic-field dependence. Herein, we report an easily saturated magnetostriction effect in a prototypical altermagnet - MnTe, which is an emerging class of collinear AFMs with special crystal symmetries. For high-quality MnTe single crystals, the magnetostriction saturates under a moderate field of ~0.7 T with an intriguing two-fold-symmetry anisotropy. First-principles calculations reveal that the saturated and anisotropic magnetostriction originates from symmetry-allowed coupling between elastic strain and its Néel order parameter. These findings break the traditional wisdom on antiferromagnetic magnetostriction.

</details>

### [Explicit Turn Resolution with Anisotropic Homogenisation for Efficient 3D Magneto-Thermal Finite-Element Simulation of Large-Scale No-Insulation HTS Magnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31009v1)
