**Summary.** This paper introduces the EXTRA homogenisation method, a novel technique for performing 3D Finite-Element simulations of massive HTS magnets. It efficiently reduces computational load by homogenizing most turns while explicitly resolving critical areas near contacts or defects. This allows researchers to simulate unprecedentedly large magnets while maintaining high accuracy compared to full turn-resolved models.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on superconducting magnets, the core contribution is a sophisticated, multi-scale numerical technique (anisotropic homogenization combined with adaptive resolution) that is highly relevant to developing efficient solvers for complex, multi-physics systems in condensed matter theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient and accurate 3D Finite-Element (FE) simulation method for the magneto-thermal behavior of large-scale No-Insulation (NI) High-Temperature Superconducting (HTS) magnets.

**Main result.** The proposed EXTRA homogenisation method achieves significant computational speedups (up to 13x) while maintaining accuracy, enabling simulations of magnets with 10,000 turns, which was previously computationally infeasible.

**Method.** The method combines anisotropic homogenization of turn-to-turn contact layers (T2TCLs) with the explicit resolution of critical turns (inner/outermost) to balance computational cost and physical accuracy.

**Model / system.** The system is a large-scale HTS magnet, modeled as stacked pancake coils. The simulation solves coupled magneto-thermal equations in 3D, accounting for current distribution, heat generation, and material properties.

**Key observables.** AC losses, temperature distributions, current density ($\vec{J}$), and magnetic field ($\vec{H}$) evolution during operation and thermal runaway.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of turns (up to 10,000), contact resistance ($R_{cl}$), and local critical current density ($J_c$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method relies on the Surface Contact Approximation (SCA) for homogenized layers and assumes that local defects only require explicit resolution of neighboring turns.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the conceptual geometry of the coil, the principle of separating turns into homogenized and resolved sections, and convergence studies comparing the new method against turn-resolved reference models.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical challenge, details the mathematical formulation of the coupled FE equations, rigorously derives the EXTRA homogenization procedure by separating turns, validates the method on smaller benchmarks, and concludes with large-scale simulations demonstrating efficiency and accuracy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

No-insulation (NI) and metal-insulation (MI) high-temperature superconducting (HTS) magnets require three-dimensional (3D) models to describe the current distribution around critical current defects. In this work, we design and validate the EXTRA homogenisation method, standing for explicit turn resolution with anisotropic homogenisation method. It allows 3D magneto-thermal finite-element (FE) simulations of large-scale magnets to be performed with high accuracy at a reasonable computational cost. The method combines the anisotropic homogenisation of turn-to-turn contact layers (T2TCLs) and their neighbouring winding turns with the explicit resolution of specific T2TCLs. In particular, the inner- and outermost winding turns and adjacent contact layers are explicitly resolved to properly describe the current distribution near current leads. In addition, the method is able to simulate local $J_{\textrm{c}}$ defects for a broad range of turn-to-turn contact resistances, provided the winding turns and T2TCLs next to the defect are explicitly resolved. For efficiency, the resolved T2TCLs are modelled using the surface contact approximation. The consistency of the proposed method is first verified on a 50-turn single pancake benchmark. It is shown to reproduce AC losses and temperature distributions obtained with a turn-resolved FE reference model, for both nominal operation and during thermal runaway. The computational efficiency of the EXTRA method is demonstrated with the simulation of a stack of three 150-turn pancake coils, for which computation time is reduced by a factor of up to 13 with respect to a turn-resolved FE reference model. Finally, the results of a large-scale 3D FE simulation, currently out of reach of turn-resolved models, are provided for an insert HTS magnet with 10,000 turns. The EXTRA method is open-source and input files to reproduce all results are made available.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses $	ext{THz-TDS}$ to study the electrodynamics of disordered $	ext{TiV}$ superconducting thin films. By analyzing the complex conductivity and kinetic inductance, the authors show that structural disorder offers a simple, tunable method to control superconducting properties. These findings position $	ext{TiV}$ alloys as excellent candidates for next-generation quantum detectors.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work bridges condensed matter theory (superconductivity, disorder effects) with advanced experimental techniques ($	ext{THz-TDS}$), providing quantitative data on superconducting response relevant to quantum sensing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how structural disorder affects the high-frequency electrodynamic response and kinetic inductance of superconducting thin films, which is vital for advanced quantum detectors.

**Main result.** The research demonstrates that structural disorder in $	ext{Ti}_{40}	ext{V}_{60}$ alloys can be systematically tuned to control the inductive response, establishing these alloys as promising materials for kinetic inductance detectors.

**Method.** Terahertz time-domain spectroscopy ($	ext{THz-TDS}$) was used to measure complex conductivity and penetration depth, which were then analyzed using the Mattis-Bardeen formalism and BCS theory.

**Model / system.** The physical system is disordered Titanium-Vanadium ($	ext{TiV}$) alloy thin films. The analysis employs theoretical models like the Mattis-Bardeen formalism and the Gorter-Casimir two-fluid model to describe the superconducting electrodynamics.

**Key observables.** Complex conductivity ($\sigma_1, \sigma_2$), penetration depth ($\lambda$), superfluid density ($	ext{n}_s$), and kinetic inductance ($	ext{L}_K$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Superconducting energy gaps ($\Delta$), critical temperature ($	ext{T}_C$), and the ratio $2\Delta(0)/k_B T_C$ (indicating strong coupling).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Mattis-Bardeen formalism is used for fitting, though deviations are noted in the low-frequency sub-gap regime.

**Figures summary.** Figures show temperature and frequency dependence of $\sigma_1$ and $\sigma_2$, illustrating the suppression of $\sigma_1$ and the characteristic $1/\omega$ inductive response in $\sigma_2$ below $	ext{T}_C$.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of problem definition, experimental measurement ($	ext{THz-TDS}$), theoretical modeling (M-B formalism, BCS fitting), and detailed analysis of superconducting parameters ($	ext{L}_K, \Delta, 	ext{n}_s$).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Disordered superconductors represent an important area in modern condensed matter physics, where superconductivity survives even in the presence of strong electron scattering and localization effects. Understanding how disorder modifies the high-frequency electrodynamic response is not only important from physics point of view, but is also essential for developing next-generation quantum detectors and superconducting devices. In this work, we investigate the terahertz electrodynamics of disordered Ti40V60 alloy thin films using terahertz time-domain spectroscopy (THz-TDS) to understand the relationship between disorder, quasiparticle dynamics, and kinetic inductance. By analysing the complex conductivity, penetration depth and superfluid response, we show that structural disorder can be systematically used to tune the inductive response while maintaining a robust superconducting phase. Unlike conventional nitride superconductors that require tightly controlled reactive growth conditions, Ti40V60 alloys provide a simpler and more adaptable route for tuning the superconducting energy scales directly through the deposition conditions. These findings establish Ti40V60 alloys as a promising material for kinetic inductance detectors and provide useful insights into the electrodynamics of strongly disordered superconductors.

</details>

### [Wetting as an emergent property of water: reformulating Young equation on molecular grounds](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30661v1)

**Authors:** Nicolas Loubet, Gustavo Appignanesi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30661v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.30661_figures/2605.30661_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Universal molecular description of aqueous wetting. Contact angles for chemically diverse systems plotted as a function of the molecular wetting coefficient ωm. Experimental data for self-assembled monolayers (SAMs) with different functionalization (from apolar to polar groups), together with results for dispersive graphene (with variable Lennard–Jones attractions) and polar silica (with varying polarity), collapse onto a single master curve defined by cos θ = ωm. Inset: Cavitation free energy for a small (methane-like sized) sphere tangent to the surface, −kBT ln P(N = 0), plotted against ωm for graphene and silica, demonstrating a thermodynamic closure linking wetting, adhesion,...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2: Nanoconfinement, filling, and thermodynamic closure controlled by the molecular wetting coefficient. (a) Minimum stable plate separation Dmin for water confined between two parallel graphene sheets as a function of interaction strength ε. </details>

**Summary.** This work provides a molecular reformulation of wetting theory, moving beyond empirical macroscopic equations like Young's. By introducing a molecular wetting coefficient ($\omega_m$), the authors demonstrate that wetting is an emergent property dictated by water's internal hydrogen-bond energetics. This framework unifies wetting, adhesion, and cavitation into a single, predictive physical principle.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical liquids, the rigorous thermodynamic closure and the development of a universal, microscopic parameter ($\omega_m$) to govern macroscopic phase behavior provides a powerful analogy for understanding emergent order in complex quantum many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The molecular origin of macroscopic wetting descriptions, such as Young's equation, particularly for water, has remained theoretically elusive.

**Main result.** Wetting is shown to be an emergent property of water's intrinsic hydrogen-bond energetic scales, unifying wetting, adhesion, and cavitation into a single molecular criterion.

**Method.** The authors reformulate wetting using a molecular wetting coefficient ($\omega_m$) derived from microscopic interaction measures ($V_{4S}$) and test this framework using molecular dynamics simulations.

**Model / system.** The system involves aqueous interfaces (solid-liquid-vapor, etc.) where the behavior is governed by the energetic cost associated with hydrogen-bond defects in the water network.

**Key observables.** Molecular wetting coefficient ($\omega_m$), contact angle ($\cos 	heta$), adhesion work ($W_{adh}$), and cavitation free energy ($\Delta G_{cav}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The molecular wetting coefficient ($\omega_m$), which quantifies the energetic compensation of HB defects relative to bulk water.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes that interfacial behavior can be mapped onto the intrinsic energetic scales of the water hydrogen-bond network, allowing for a universal description across different chemistries.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate that contact angles for diverse surfaces collapse onto a single master curve defined by $\cos 	heta = \omega_m$, and that thermodynamic quantities like cavitation energy show linear closure with $\omega_m$.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the macroscopic problem (Young's equation), introduces the molecular parameter ($\omega_m$), demonstrates its universality across various surfaces, and finally closes the thermodynamic loop by linking wetting, adhesion, and cavitation via $\omega_m$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Young equation provides a remarkably successful macroscopic description of wetting, yet its molecular origin (particularly for water) has remained elusive for over two centuries. Here we make the molecular basis of aqueous wetting explicit by reformulating it in terms of a molecular wetting coefficient, omega m, which quantifies how an interface compensates the intrinsic energetic cost of hydrogen-bond defects relative to bulk water. Across a broad and continuous spectrum of hydrophilicities, spanning chemically diverse experimental and model surfaces, macroscopic contact angles collapse onto a single universal master curve when expressed through omega m. This molecular reformulation closes Young and Young-Dupre relations on energetic grounds, establishing a unified and predictive physical link between wetting, adhesion, cavitation, and nanoconfined filling. By anchoring interfacial behavior to waters intrinsic hydrogen-bond energetic scales, our results reveal wetting as an emergent property of water itself, rather than a surface-specific attribute and provide a transferable molecular framework that recalibrates energetic intuition and guides the rational design of aqueous interfaces. (This document is the unedited Author version of a Submitted Manuscript subsequently accepted for publication in J. Am. Chem. Soc. For the published version, which includes a more complete molecular-thermodynamics grounding of the method see the published version)

</details>

### [Bending-like stress induced by solder joint under uniaxial tensile testing in 2G-HTS tapes: Impact and optimization approach](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30760v1)

**Authors:** Dean Liu, Yue Wu, Haoliang Xiang, Xiaofen Li, Caida Fu, Chiheng Dong, Yue Zhao  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30760v1>

<sub>Fig. 1 (a) illustrates the uniaxial tensile test results for a short sample of Tape Ⅰ. In this test, the</sub>

<sub>Fig. 1 Uniaxial tensile test and post-mortem characterization of a Tape Ⅰ short sample. (a) Ic degradation as a</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.30760_figures/2605.30760_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2 (b) ~ (d). Prominent high-strain regions consistently develop at both the upper-right and</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.30760_figures/2605.30760_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2 The DIC results of the Tape Ⅰ. (a) Photograph of one sample front surface with speckle, and the solder joint</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.30760_figures/2605.30760_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 FEM results of REBCO tapes with solder joint attached to the front surface under 600 MPa uniaxial tensile</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work addresses the critical issue of inaccurate mechanical testing of high-temperature superconducting tapes due to stress concentrations from solder joints. By combining experimental measurements with FEM simulations, the authors prove that the joint induces damaging bending-like stresses. They propose and validate an optimized back-surface soldering technique, leading to significantly more accurate characterization of the tapes' true performance limits.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on materials science, the rigorous application of continuum mechanics (FEM) to model localized stress fields and their impact on a critical performance metric (Ic) demonstrates advanced multiscale modeling techniques applicable to complex quantum devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The accuracy of measuring the reversible stress limit (Rrev) of 2G-HTS tapes is compromised by stress concentration induced by the voltage tap solder joint during uniaxial tensile testing.

**Main result.** Optimizing the soldering location to the back surface mitigates this stress interference, significantly increasing the measured Rrev (e.g., 42% increase for one tape configuration).

**Method.** The study combines integrated experimental characterization (tensile testing, DIC, SHPM) with detailed numerical simulations using the Finite Element Method (FEM).

**Model / system.** The physical system is a multi-layered 2G-HTS tape (REBCO) subjected to uniaxial tensile loading, with the primary focus on the mechanical stress state induced by the solder joint.

**Key observables.** Reversible stress limit (Rrev), critical current (Ic) degradation, local strain fields, and stress distribution maps.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Copper layer thickness ($5 \mu	ext{m}$ example), applied tensile stress, and the location of the solder joint (front vs. back surface).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The FEM model simplifies the tape into a multi-layered composite structure, and the analysis assumes the stress concentration mechanism is dominated by geometric inhomogeneity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison of Ic degradation and Rrev between front-surface and back-surface soldering approaches, alongside FEM stress maps demonstrating the induced bending-like stress profile.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the problem using front-surface testing results, then uses FEM and experimental diagnostics (DIC, SHPM) to mechanistically prove the existence of bending-like stress. Finally, it proposes and validates the back-surface soldering approach, showing quantitative improvements across multiple tape types.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The reversible stress limit (\mathit{R}_{rev}) of second-generation high-temperature superconducting (2G-HTS) tapes is a critical performance indicator, typically characterized through uniaxial tensile testing. In practice, the accuracy of the measured Rrev value is often compromised by stress concentration induced by the voltage tap solder joint. The present study investigates the underlying interference mechanism using integrated experimental and numerical methods. Mechanistic analysis reveals that under uniaxial tensile loading, the local geometric inhomogeneity introduced by the solder joint induces an external, bending-like stress in the vicinity of the solder joint, transitioning from additional tensile stress in the zone adjacent to the joint to additional compressive stress in the zone remote from it. When the solder joint is attached to the front surface of the tape (the side closer to the superconducting layer), the superconducting layer experiences localized additional tensile stress, triggering premature damage and early \mathit{I}_{c} degradation. Consequently, an optimized back-surface soldering approach is proposed, which positions the superconducting layer in a localized compressive zone. Experimental validation demonstrates that the proposed approach effectively mitigates testing errors for various tape configurations. Notably, for the tape with a copper layer thickness of 5 μm, the measured \mathit{R}_{rev} increased from 546 MPa to 734 MPa, corresponding to a 42% increase, and moved closer to the actual value. The findings provide essential insights for the precision characterization of the electromechanical performances (EMPs) of 2G-HTS tapes.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31206"></a>
### [In-situ operation of amorphous circuits under heavy-ion irradiation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31206v1)

**Authors:** Xuanzhe Sha, Shun Liao, Xiaoxi Li, Chengyuan Li, Jianli Liu, Yu Pan, Wenhai Wang, Yu Ye, Chengxin Zhao, Liyi Li, Hanwen Wang, Zheng Vitto Han, Jianming Lu  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31206v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

</details>

<img src="2605.31206_figures/2605.31206_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

The red laser beam was used only for sample alignment and was switched off during irradiation and measurement. b, Representative input, clock and output waveforms of a D-type flip-flop measured during continuous irradiation by 181Ta ions at a flux of 2.5 × 103 s−1, showing stable operation over 500 s. c input and d output of 8-bit ASCII sequence of the cascaded timing circuit under non-irradiated operation, reproducing the programmed string “HELLO WORLD”. The timing circuits were measured during continuous powered irradiation...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31206_figures/2605.31206_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 Simulated heavy-ion interaction with the ultrathin IGZO transis- tor structure. a, Schematic of a 181Ta ions traversing the multilayer device stack. b, Electronic stopping power Se as a function of depth, showing lim- ited energy deposition in the ∼2 nm IGZO channel. c, Depth-dependent summed recoil energy, indicating reduced displacement damage in the IGZO layer compared to surrounding materials. d, Lateral distribution of collision events, showing highly localized damage along the ion track with minimal lateral spreading.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work demonstrates the feasibility of building functional, digital electronic circuits using amorphous IGZO thin-film semiconductors that can operate reliably under intense heavy-ion irradiation. By leveraging the ultrathin nature of the semiconductor channel, the authors show that the circuit maintains stable logic operation, establishing a key milestone for radiation-tolerant electronics in harsh environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** While not directly quantum, the study addresses reliability in extreme physical environments, which is a critical engineering challenge for quantum computing hardware deployed outside controlled lab settings.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing radiation-hardened electronics for reliable operation in extreme environments subjected to heavy-ion irradiation.

**Main result.** The amorphous IGZO-based digital circuit demonstrated robust, functional operation, successfully outputting a 'Hello World' sequence during prolonged, powered heavy-ion bombardment.

**Method.** The study combines device fabrication, electrical characterization, and *in situ* testing using heavy-ion accelerators, supplemented by particle transport simulations (SRIM/Geant4).

**Model / system.** The system utilizes amorphous IGZO thin-film transistors configured into D-type flip-flops and cascaded timing circuits, fabricated on a multilayer stack structure.

**Key observables.** Stable digital output sequence ('Hello World'), operating current vs. time during irradiation, and the area-normalized Single Event Upset (SEU) cross section ($\sigma_{	ext{SEU}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** High particle flux ($2.5 	imes 10^3 	ext{ ions } 	ext{cm}^{-2} 	ext{ s}^{-1}$), total fluence ($1 	imes 10^6 	ext{ ions } 	ext{cm}^{-2}$), and the ultrathin channel thickness ($\approx 2 	ext{ nm}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The intrinsic geometric confinement of the active channel in amorphous semiconductors is sufficient to provide radiation resilience, mitigating the need for external shielding or redundancy.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the circuit design (DFF, timing sequence), the electrical testing platform, and the core results showing stable operation and preserved 'Hello World' output during heavy-ion irradiation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the need for radiation-hard electronics, detailing the IGZO device structure and fabrication, demonstrating functional testing, and culminating in the *in situ* heavy-ion irradiation tests and physical analysis of ion interactions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Radiation-hardened electronics using semiconductors beyond silicon are essential for computation and control in extreme environments. Yet complex digital circuits based on such material platforms operating in situ under heavy-ion irradiation remain largely unexplored. Here, we show a timing circuit based on amorphous thin-film semiconductors at the 100-transistor scale, and demonstrate its robust operation through a functional "Hello World" ASCII output sequence. Beyond static device characterization, we evaluate the circuit under powered heavy-ion irradiation using tantalum ions, providing an operationally relevant assessment of radiation tolerance at the system level. Under a high particle flux of 2.5 x 10^3 ions cm^-2 s^-1, the circuit maintains stable operation during the irradiation test, achieving a total fluence of 1 x 10^6 ions cm^-2, establishing a milestone of prolonged powered digital operation under extreme conditions. Our work expands the design space of radiation-tolerant electronics, highlighting amorphous semiconductors as a promising foundation for digital circuits deployed in harsh environments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.31134"></a>
### [Interplay of Cl Substitution and He$^{+}$ Irradiation in CrSBr$_{1-x}$Cl$_{x}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31134v1)

**Authors:** Satyam Sahu, Adeel Bukhari, Arijit Kayal, Valerie Černá, Bing Wu, Aljoscha Söll, Gregor Hlawacek, Zdeněk Sofer, Martin Kalbáč, Matěj Velický, Otakar Frank  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31134v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

The positions of the parent Ag modes and He+ induced D1, D3, and D# modes are highlighted by dotted vertical lines.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.31134_figures/2605.31134_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Raman intensities as a function of illumination power under resonant (1.96 eV) excitation for E∥a configuration for x = 0.2. (a-b) A2 g, P1, and P2 modes in non-irradiated samples. </details>

**Summary.** This work uses Raman spectroscopy to examine how introducing disorder via chlorine substitution and physical damage via helium ion irradiation affects the vibrational modes of $	ext{CrSBr}_{1-x}	ext{Cl}_x$. The findings show that while disorder significantly alters the spectrum, the underlying strong electron-phonon coupling mechanism persists, indicating the material's resilience in its electronic response.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides detailed experimental evidence on how lattice dynamics are modified by two distinct sources of disorder (chemical vs. physical), which is relevant for understanding material stability and electronic coupling in engineered quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how combining compositional disorder (Cl substitution) and external physical damage ($	ext{He}^+$ irradiation) affects the vibrational properties of the layered magnetic semiconductor $	ext{CrSBr}_{1-x}	ext{Cl}_x$.

**Main result.** The combined disorder leads to anisotropic reconstruction of the Raman spectra, but the resonance-enhanced electron-phonon coupling, evidenced by superlinear power scaling, remains robust even in defect-engineered samples.

**Method.** The research employs polarization-resolved Raman spectroscopy, supplemented by power-dependent measurements to probe the vibrational response.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the mixed-halide magnetic semiconductor $	ext{CrSBr}_{1-x}	ext{Cl}_x$, which is analyzed in thin flakes. Disorder is introduced via controlled $	ext{Cl}$ substitution and external $	ext{He}^+$ ion irradiation.

**Key observables.** Phonon frequencies, linewidths, polarization dependence ($E\parallel a$ vs $E\parallel b$), and the nonlinear scaling exponent ($	heta$) of Raman intensities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cl substitution concentration ($x$), $	ext{He}^+$ irradiation fluence (up to $1.2 	imes 10^{15} 	ext{ cm}^{-2}$), and excitation energy (1.96 eV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed spectral changes can be systematically attributed to the distinct effects of alloying disorder versus irradiation-induced defects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate composition-dependent spectra, fluence-dependent defect mode activation ($	ext{D}1, 	ext{D}3, 	ext{D}\#$), and the power-law scaling of various phonon modes before and after irradiation.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the effects of Cl substitution first, then the effects of He+ irradiation on pure $	ext{CrSBr}$, and finally combines these analyses to study the interplay in $	ext{CrSBr}_{1-x}	ext{Cl}_x$, concluding with nonlinear response measurements.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Two-dimensional magnetic semiconductors provide a promising platform for exploring the interplay between disorder, lattice dynamics, and resonant light--matter interactions. Among them, CrSBr exhibits strong in-plane anisotropy and pronounced resonance-enhanced Raman scattering. Here, we investigate the effects of Cl substitution and He$^{+}$ irradiation on the vibrational response of CrSBr using polarization-resolved Raman spectroscopy. Cl substitution activates additional phonon modes associated with local symmetry breaking, while He$^{+}$ irradiation introduces distinct defect-related scattering channels and enhanced phonon broadening. The combined effects of alloy disorder and externally introduced defects lead to strong anisotropic reconstruction of the Raman spectra and modification of the nonlinear Raman response under near-resonant 1.96 eV excitation. Power-dependent measurements reveal robust superlinear scaling of both intrinsic and substitution-induced phonon modes, indicating persistent resonance-enhanced electron--phonon coupling even in defect-engineered samples.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.30881"></a>
### [Spin Hall effect in two-dimensional materials with inverted bands and Mexican-hat dispersion](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30881v1)

**Authors:** Bagun S. Shchamkhalova, Vladimir A. Sablikov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.30881v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

</details>

<img src="2605.30881_figures/2605.30881_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This work theoretically examines the Spin Hall Effect in 2D topological insulators exhibiting Mexican-hat dispersion. The authors find that extrinsic scattering mechanisms can generate a spin Hall current that surpasses the intrinsic contribution. Crucially, this extrinsic current displays a complex, non-monotonic dependence on the Fermi energy, which is dictated by the interplay between inter- and intra-contour electron transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The complex, energy-dependent behavior of the SHE, particularly the dominance of extrinsic effects over intrinsic ones at certain energies, provides rich physics related to topological transport phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the Spin Hall Effect (SHE) in two-dimensional topological insulators characterized by 'Mexican-hat' dispersion and a ring-shaped Fermi surface.

**Main result.** The extrinsic SHE current can significantly exceed the intrinsic SHE current, and its dependence on the Fermi energy ($E_F$) is highly non-monotonic, exhibiting maxima, minima, and sign changes.

**Method.** The analysis employs the Boltzmann equation solved for two dispersion branches, incorporating spin-dependent skew scattering enabled by second-order impurity scattering processes.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using the Bernevig-Hughes-Chang (BHZ) Hamiltonian for topological insulators. Key features include the Mexican-hat dispersion leading to two isoenergetic contours and a ring-shaped Fermi surface.

**Key observables.** Extrinsic Spin Hall Current (SHC), Intrinsic Spin Hall Current (SHC), and the dependence of these currents on Fermi energy ($E_F$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fermi energy ($E_F$), hybridization parameter ($a$), and band asymmetry ($\delta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis initially ignores boundary effects, and the screening potential calculation utilizes the Random Phase Approximation (RPA).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the MHD and ring-shaped Fermi surface, and show the non-monotonic dependence of the extrinsic SHE current on $E_F$ for varying material parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory by calculating scattering amplitudes via the $t$-matrix formalism and solving the resulting Boltzmann equation system of linear equations to determine the non-equilibrium distribution functions and resulting currents.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the spin Hall effect in two-dimensional topological insulators with "Mexican hat" dispersion and a ring-shaped Fermi surface which are formed due to the band inversion. Electron transitions between different isoenergetic contours and the quantum metric of band states play an important role in the transport properties of such materials, since they largely determine the spatial distribution of the electron charges screening the impurity potential and the scattering probability [Phys.B, 719, 417942 (2025)]. Here we study a spin-dependent skew scattering, which is enabled by the second-order scattering processes, and show that the extrinsic spin-Hall current (SHC) can significantly exceed the intrinsic SHC arising from the Berry curvature. Furthermore, due to Mexican-hat dispersion, the SHC exhibits a very unusual dependence on the Fermi energy ($E_F$). The extrinsic SHC reaches a maximum at some $E_F$, then decreases with increasing $E_F$ and can even change a sign. This complicated behavior reflects an interplay of energy dependencies of such important factors as probabilities of inter- and intra-contour transitions, as well as different electron velocities in two contours.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.31459"></a>
### [What controls the superconducting dome of electron-doped FeSe?](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.31459v1)

**Authors:** Paul T. Malinowski, Chad J. Mowers, Yaoju Tarn, Darrell G. Schlom, Brendan D. Faeth, Kyle M. Shen  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.31459v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.31459_figures/2605.31459_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Electronic transport as a function of doping via Cs surface deposition. (a) 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.31459_figures/2605.31459_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Evolution and surface confinement of doping observed in ARPES. (a - d) 16</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.31459_figures/2605.31459_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Extracted sheet resistance of the doped surface layer. (a, b) Temperature 27</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.31459_figures/2605.31459_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Comparison of systems exhibiting superconducting domes. Normal state 39</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work investigates the superconducting dome in electron-doped FeSe using advanced epitaxial and transport measurements. The key finding is a robust linear scaling between the critical temperature ($T_c$) and the residual resistivity ($ho_0$) across the entire doping range. This suggests that the superconductivity in this material is fundamentally limited by disorder scattering rather than solely by the carrier concentration.

**Why it may be interesting.** The strong correlation between $T_c$ and disorder ($ho_0$) suggests a universal mechanism for high-$T_c$ superconductivity driven by scattering, which is relevant to understanding pairing mechanisms in strongly correlated systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To determine the physical mechanism controlling the superconducting dome observed in electron-doped FeSe, as its full phase diagram remains unexplored.

**Main result.** The superconducting transition temperature ($T_c$) scales robustly and linearly with the residual resistivity ($ho_0$) across the entire dome, suggesting that disorder, rather than carrier doping alone, is the primary control parameter.

**Method.** The study combines molecular beam epitaxy synthesis, alkali surface doping, in-vacuum electrical transport measurements, and ARPES to map the phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The system is electron-doped FeSe thin films, where doping is controlled by Cs surface deposition. The analysis models the film as a stack of resistors to extract the sheet resistivity ($ho_0$).

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature ($T_c$), residual resistivity ($ho_0$), and the evolution of the electronic band structure (Fermiology) via ARPES.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The doping level (controlled by Cs deposition), the residual resistivity ($ho_0$), and the elastic scattering rate ($	au^{-1}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the system can be modeled as a stack of resistors, and that the charge transfer of deposited Cs is confined to the topmost FeSe layer.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the doping dependence of $T_c$ and resistivity, highlighting a non-monotonic evolution of $ho_0$ and a clear linear scaling plot of $T_c$ vs. $ho_0$ across the dome.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically explores the entire superconducting dome using multiple techniques, establishing the $T_c$-$ho_0$ scaling, contrasting this behavior with other superconductors, and proposing that disorder limits superconductivity in this material.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconducting domes are conspicuous features of the phase diagrams of most unconventional and high-temperature superconductors. The superconducting transition temperature ($T_{c}$) of FeSe can be dramatically enhanced with electron doping, but unlike all other high-temperature and unconventional superconductors, its full phase diagram and superconducting dome have yet to be fully explored. Here, we employ a combination of molecular beam epitaxy synthesis, alkali surface doping, in-vacuum electrical transport, and angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy to investigate the entire superconducting dome of electron-doped FeSe, achieving a fully metallic state where superconductivity is suppressed in the heavily overdoped regime. We discover a robust scaling between $T_{c}$ and the residual resistivity ($ρ_{0}$) which holds across the entire superconducting dome, suggesting that the evolution of $T_{c}$ is heavily influenced by the evolution of the elastic scattering rate in the high-$T_{c}$ electron-doped phase. This in turn suggests that the superconducting dome in electron-doped FeSe appears to be fundamentally different than that of other unconventional superconductors where doping plays the primary role, and may be driven primarily by the sensitivity of the superconductivity to disorder.

</details>
